[Date]2011-06-07
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a7920c7-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/87c80ec71f915a4180b2a6c05f7e14f6/Both_tunnels_smooth.jpg
Seen here is the fork where trains can continue to head in the direction they were going, or switch to the crossover tunnel. That is expected to be done in cases of maintenance on one of the two one-way tunnels, or when there is an accident.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a71138f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6bdae3702c706cbd8cabd447af4a2b56/z-Tunnel_boring_machine.png
Most of the major excavation work was done using a tunnel-boring machine like this one.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a6966df-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8401c8ece8a3b39423d7e63d4f6d4164/Scaffolding.jpg
In order to work on the ceiling of the tunnel, workers had to set up major scaffolding, like seen here.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a638ca2-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6534d6833c48afb038f4ac26722ec454/Various_stages_membrane.jpg
Underneath its smooth concrete surface, the tunnel has three layers: a drainage layer, a waterproofing layer, and an inner shell. Seen here, a section of the tunnel has different layers showing and in progress.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a5c2e07-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2524cbe3e4599854bc5ad48112c0c202/Adding_membrane_to_ceiling.jpg
A worker adds part of the protective membrane to the ceiling inside the Sedrun section of the Gotthard Tunnel, the world's longest tunnel.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a55f8cf-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/710cc4bdd4a83dd2e51a3d9f930da7f9/Tunnel_covered_in_membrane.jpg
Here we see a section of the tunnel fully covered in the protective membrane. It will then be covered in concrete and smoothed out.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a4f3e07-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/90c03b01e4324825967a01c2844e8512/Cable_car.jpg
Anyone heading deep into the tunnel--800 meters below the surface--must first descent from the town of Sedrun on this little cable car.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a44efe7-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9bfa119eee2ae45d379e9b8bdd9836b7/Control_room.jpg
In this small office in Sedrun, a worker can monitor the control room monitors and see what is going on in several different places at once. Here, with computers, one person can run the ventilation system, the electrical system, traffic inside the 800-foot-deep shaft, and keep track of who's inside the tunnel.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a3d7b8d-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/58b8d7e234502e47f7ee4a0310f2629e/Control_room_screen.jpg
A look at a computer screen, on which the controller can monitor what's going on deep inside the tunnel.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a38020f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7e788d9bb4ade5e5c68f00875e0cfc02/Staziun_Alpina.jpg
At Staziun Alpina, small trains (that were once used in the construction of the Eurotunnel, otherwise known as the Chunnel) ferry workers from the surface to the shaft elevator that takes them 800 meters below the surface.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a3270d5-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4a6b0013e2e5b33219cb996206a3e8ec/Driver_and_cigarette.jpg
The driver of the train offers a passenger a cigarette.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a2ca108-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d7817fa8fb186ae9ab09204793abe378/Going_into_the_portal.jpg
Through the window of the small train, it is possible to see the entrance to the Sedrun portal to the Gotthard Tunnel.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a261ad1-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ca149623c1be9b40a1d696fb2dbd84e4/Driver_in_the_tunnel.jpg
Through the window of the train, it is possible to see deep into the 1 kilometer long tunnel that leads to the 800-meter-high shaft that takes workers and visitors down into the main tunnel system.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a1e29b3-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e0d4af2879a817cd3b06ffd37a60c6ee/Trains_in_the_big_chamber.jpg
At the bottom of the shaft there is a giant chamber, where workers come to and fro, and several small trains are gathered to take people or materials around.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a14088f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4550964e51355f298ce7048f4881ff9b/Finished_section.jpg
After years of work, this is a nearly finished section of the Gotthard Tunnel. Note the smooth interior surface. Under that concrete are several layers designed to keep the tunnel solid and to keep it dry.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a0bb025-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9bbc35c88a1d7e3cc4f8bfe7a1acaed0/Finished_emergency_station.jpg
One important element of the tunnel project is the construction of two emergency stations designed to quickly and safely offload passengers from a burning train. Though the stations are 20 kilometers apart, the idea is that if a troubled train can make it to the station, passengers can quickly disembark and escape through special tubes that take them to adjacent safety tunnels. Those tunnels, because of a slight overpressure, are supposed to be free of smoke, no matter how much is coming from a burning train.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/4a0597ba-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/50446d113208cbe606392c007bb266d0/Fire_door.jpg
A fire door is designed to keep fire and smoke out of the passageways through which passengers on troubled trains can escape.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/49feceed-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5d90cf92009113cf71dcd333961e8852/Down_ventilation_shaft.jpg
There are seven ventilation shafts like this one above the emergency stations. The idea is that smoke can be lifted out quickly, making it easy for passengers to escape a burning train that has made it to the emergency station without smoke inhalation.
If a train cannot make it to the emergency station, there are still escape passageways every 325 meters throughout the tunnel, though they only lead passengers to the parallel one-way tunnel rather than to a fully safe and secure section of tunnel.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/49f083af-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1242f5334527061e0e602d5bde542e0e/Finished_connecting_passageaway.jpg
This is a finished connecting passageway. There are sections like this every 325 meters throughout the tunnel. These passageways can allow anyone to escape a troubled train that cannot make it to an emergency station.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/49e8a5a6-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9a781971924c285a3f37f9357050c439/Future_station.jpg
Gotthard Tunnel planners decided to future-proof the project by excavating a cavern where a major train station could be built in the future. This station, which could be the deepest underground in the world, as well as the highest elevation major station in the world, could serve as many as 3,000 skiers a day in Sedrun, Switzerland. But there are no plans to finish this part of the project. Instead, the cavern will be closed off from the main tunnel. The idea, though, is that by digging out the cavern now, it opens the possibility of the station in the future, while not doing it now would prohibit its ever being done.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/49d7d374-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/db31c6e255ef81c44da95a1d263f46e0/Massive_ventilation_tubes.jpg
In a major tunnel system, ventilation is clearly important. Here, we see giant ventilation tubes attached to the ceiling of the tunnel.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/49d1437e-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3f5c0f30950aa83de4b6216a551d692d/Stop_Risk.jpg
Due to a series of safety programs, injuries and accidents have been cut down significantly on the Gotthard Tunnel project.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/49c77c7e-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0688f3440416d1b0e5ecf70cb703694b/Ventilation_tunnel.jpg
There is a ventilation tunnel above the emergency station, designed to pull smoke out from burning trains. This is a connecting passageway to the ventilation tunnel.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/49c3d3c5-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8b448f9cd605f0ae072d67b1a6e6289d/Where_the_door_will_go.jpg
The air pressure at the crossover sections could be so great that it could knock a train off the tracks. That's why there will be giant doors installed in front of the crossover sections that are designed to block the air flowing through, or to block fire or smoke. This shows where that giant door will be installed.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/05/49bc177a-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d6572de96c1817a6e613c72e037ba6d0/Weather_door.jpg
This is a weather door designed to minimize the heavy flow of air in sections of the tunnel that are still under construction. Sections will have these doors at both ends.

Indeed, Warhol painted one of BMW's most illustrious cars ever, an M1, (shown left) and he did the whole job in 28 minutes. In fact, he finished so fast, a TV crew that had been hired to document the work was too late to capture it.

The most recent addition to the BMW art car collection is this masterpiece by Jeff Koons. The 17th in the series is an M3 GT2 that first saw the light of day in 2010 at the Pompidou Center in Paris and later raced in the 24 Hours of Le Mans.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/09/801ab82a-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/722a42ee087f5ddc1dd05f96ab320a98/Alexander_Calder_right_side.jpg

In 1992, artist Sandro Chia completed his BMW art car, a prototype 3 Series racing touring car. Here's a shot of the left side.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/09/7fe55684-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4eca009d4975936a10961c7e2fb47777/Frank_Stella_straight_on.jpg
One of the earliest BMW art cars was this one by the famous Frank Stella, which was completed in 1976. Here, we see it from the front.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/09/7fdece27-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bc3c743a185df861737c9736a184fa07/Frank_Stella_right_side.jpg

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/09/7fda3f30-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f5cc2432c39457f33299b4b270efd610/z-Olafur_Eliasson_ice_car.jpg
Artist Olafur Eliasson decided to make his BMW art car a statement about climate change. Rather than paint a car, Eliasson cast a BMW H2R 12-cylinder hydrogen concept engine in ice. It was the 16th work in the collection and was completed in 2007.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/09/7fd46ec4-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0a3a1c38b4b160d374576eafe2c92d78/Michael_Jagamara_Nelson_left_side.jpg
The BMW art car collection has two submissions from Australian artists--one an aboriginal work, the other a Western work. This is artist Michael Jagamara Nelson's aboriginal interpretation of the series, done in 1989 on a BMW M3 Group A Racing Version. Here, it's seen from the left side.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/09/7fcd8b39-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/91df1a1d8de1e2718e4b01b582536c0a/Michael_Jagamara_Nelson_right_side.jpg
This is the right side of Australian artist Michael Jagamara Nelson's BMW art car.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/09/7fc3d285-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a21df0512449fda1045b6d728eb7f205/Ken_Done_left_side.jpg
This is Australian artist Ken Done's BMW art car, which he completed in 1989 on a BMW M3 Group A Racing Version. It is the Western counterpoint to Michael Jagamara Nelson's aboriginal interpretation. Here, it is seen from the left side.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/09/7fbea1ee-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f2fe85d83c04010e11e91db52914cfa8/Ken_Done_right_side.jpg

This is the right side of Jenny Holzer's BMW art car, a 1999 work done on a BMW V12 LMR.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/09/7fb15ed2-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e9614a2c4c86261233e5887e4a739a30/Robert_Rauschenberg_right_side.jpg

[Date]2011-06-13
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5e29c7bd-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/94ab8b54d794b6ebf376ee5a4401110f/Ambulance_arrives_at_landed_C-17.jpg
Here, an ambulance pulls up to a C-17 that has just arrived from Iraq carrying several severely wounded, as well as other less wounded, soldiers.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5e234e9a-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/02c287dcad29386642f2975a5238d12f/Bringing_out_litter_patient.jpg
Here, a crew from the 86th Contingency Aeromedical Staging Facility (CASF) carry a wounded soldier off the C-17. Though seriously injured, this soldier is not in as dire a situation as others on the plane. He is what is known as a "litter" patient, meaning he is carried on a cot and doesn't require special equipment. The most serious cases, those that need intensive care, require what is known as a Critical Care Air Transport Team (CCATT), a team of three specialists who travel with the patient and who carry all the gear needed to tend to him or her while in the air.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5e1917c0-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ac6b8941ad15679dab0e2276b663aba9/Taking_CCAT_patient_off.jpg
Here, the CASF crew carefully carries a CCATT patient off the C-17. These patients must be cared for at the Landstuhl Regional Medical Center until they are stable enough to be sent to a hospital in the United States. You can see all the gear required to care for the patient.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5e0f4282-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c5e66fce5282f7b8e234f17f43b18fd3/Lifting_CCATT_patient_into_ambulance.jpg
Counting gear required to care for the patient, the CASF crew must carry between 400 and 500 pounds when transferring him or her between the airplane and the ambulance. Here, it takes a group to lift this soldier onto the ambulance.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5e06cf9d-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e84ac549cde192b40567c6682a5caa17/C-17_after_all_patients_out.jpg
After all the patients--both CCATTs and others--have been taken off the C-17, it is nearly empty. This plane, like all others being used for aeromedical evacuations to Ramstein, is a cargo plane that can be configured for many different uses. Ramstein has the ability to call for an airplane at any time for an evacuation, regardless of one of the regular flights scheduled to fly there is ready to go. The pilots who fly there are trained to fly several different kinds of airplanes.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5e0084e6-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1fa7f7b251b0907ff3e620a474402e65/Offloading_patient_luggage.jpg
Here, the CASF crew works to take patients' luggage off the C-17 that has just arrived at Ramstein from Iraq. The red cross tag on the bag seen here means that it belongs to a patient who was flown to Ramstein on the plane.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5df951ca-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/246142f0ca6fc75dc0cb852de2184ea1/CASF_beds_male.jpg
Many of the less serious medical cases generally spend just one night at Ramstein before being flown home to the United States. The CASF maintains two rooms of 25 beds, one for men, and another for women. This is the men's room.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5df13249-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a0bde44a747fabb55deba9665faf1266/CASF_team_lined_up.jpg
Here, we see the CASF team lined up to help lift patients from an ambulance onto a C-17 that is waiting to take them back to the United States.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5de926eb-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e8030080ee6ca4a1201b6b2e604dada5/Thumbs_up_for_next_patient.jpg
Here, we see Air Force Maj. Mick LuCore, the medical team director, giving the thumbs-up that means the CASF crew can bring another patient off the ambulance and on to the C-17.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5de3295e-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/49630c28f236a24e29efe629761416d2/Jameson_on_plane_thumbs_up.jpg
Army Specialist Jameson Read, who was shot in the arm in Afghanistan, gives a thumbs-up as he is loaded on to the C-17 that will fly him home for a few weeks of physical therapy. He will then be sent back into combat.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5ddd3466-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/78f6d2b1749b3bf90d17871a41b6fe0b/Jameson_gets_his_cookies.jpg
Volunteer members of the USO (United Service Organizations) based at Ramstein often make cookies to send with the soldiers who are flying to hospitals back in the United States. Here, a USO volunteer hands Specialist Jameson Read his bag of cookies on board the C-17.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5dd68f3b-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/33f2c6afca081946f484081a94ab17d8/Taking_CCAT_patient_off_bus.jpg
Here we see the CASF team lifting a CCATT patient off the ambulance in order to take him on to the C-17 that will fly him to a hospital in the United States.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5dd01b66-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0b1b5f11b1705d4b7bb18cfbbb9be58a/General_Ellington_talking_to_wife.jpg
In this photograph, taken on board the C-17 that is about to leave Ramstein for the United States, we see Air Force Maj. Gen. John Ellington, who is also chief of chaplains in the National Guard Bureau, talking with Larissa Daniel, whose husband has suffered a traumatic brain injury, and whose prognosis is unknown.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5dc6fba1-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8faa8fa1643064506bd6ae950e2b199a/Medallion.jpg
Larissa Daniel shows the National Guard Bureau coin given her moments earlier by Gen. Ellington. The coin is meant to convey to her that she is not alone as she cares for her stricken husband.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5dc36b68-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/51ac1940207993db31b0ff2730a8d81f/Going_over_the_plan.jpg
The CASF team goes over the game plan as they prepare to begin loading patients from two ambulances onto the C-17 that is about to take off from Ramstein for the United States.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5dbb9a02-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dc1021d26fa85b7f27a3978309e99be0/Placing_CCAT_patient.jpg
Here, a CCATT patient is carefully placed into the rack aboard the airplane where he will lay during his flight back to America.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5db7106d-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9513c632059278269ce2db0306626ce4/Real_World_CASF_mission.jpg
A sign in the window of the ambulance indicates that the CASF team is not involved in a drill. This involves injured soldiers, and the team's mission is to care for them as best they can.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5daf35a7-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ba085b87819a72ae4e82b6f092d9c4ab/Patient_information_white_board.jpg
This whiteboard in the CASF center conveys crucial information to the soldiers who are spending the night there before being flown home the next day.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5da7673d-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5694ace7abb3c1f756cb03d6ab68f0ef/Special_quilt.jpg
A group called Quilts of Valor often makes special quilts for the wounded soldiers, and many of them take the quilts home with them. Here, we see one folded over the legs of a soldier being carried aboard the C-17.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5da1ac49-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6b44790d7da31600816fdc699c584030/CCATT_kit_on_plane.jpg
For every three CCATT patients, one CCATT team consisting of a physician, a critical care nurse, and a respiratory care therapist flies with them. The team will also bring all the gear needed to give in-flight treatment to the patients. Here, we see one of the CCATT kits aboard the airplane.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5d995330-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/09129a777a48f882d34078071f353dd1/CCATT_kit_ready_to_go.jpg
The CCATT teams also maintain full sets of their gear in a storage area so that the equipment is ready to go at a moment's notice.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5d91833b-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/aebc13c97004b5b6881e42a46b9f36b3/Dog_signed.jpg
In the CASF center, there is a large poster signed by many of the patients who have spent time there. Among those patients is a working dog, who was injured in a fall. The dog was actually given a military rank, and it was the same as the animal's handler. The military sent the dog home, and the handler has been trying to adopt it.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5d8943d6-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f7c5e490e5dbb00cd6cf9ace118d6f35/Information_on_the_Fish.jpg
For three years since Army Sgt. Jesse Ault died in combat in Iraq, members of the 332nd Expeditionary Medical Group there have been trying to send Ault's beloved fish back to his family. Now, the fish are finally being sent home after volunteers raised enough money to get a veterinary certificate allowing the animals to be sent to the United States. This packet contains all the critical details about the fish.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5d7e2ea1-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/eafa437ed5b9108419560ebd63d02f26/Fish.jpg
This is one of the two fish being sent back to Sgt. Ault's family in the United States. The two fish were put on the C-17 that left Ramstein on June 10, 2011.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/11/5d74dedf-f0f1-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e218a5ee4f418a6c68fb72a711df0704/USO_computers.jpg
At the Ramstein CASF center, the USO maintains a group of computers outfitted with Skype so that soldiers staying there can call their loved ones back home.

This is an iconic view of Neuschwanstein with the Alps behind it, taken on a fall day. The castle was never finished and to this day, its entire second floor remains incomplete.

Ludwig had a penchant for all things German medieval, as well as noble. His ideal design for his new castle was a combination of the medieval and the romantic, as seen here in this design drawing from 1869.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/13/5fe2132f-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2b4b0673151d11d3b5c85b4bdc020d20/After_death_of_King_Ludwig.jpg
This photograph of Neuschwanstein was taken shortly after King Ludwig's death and shows the scaffolding that conveys that the castle was never finished--even when the king was living there, as he did for about six months before his arrest and untimely death at the age of 41.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/13/5fdd37fa-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/76dd6dd367681144ef1fc50b776deda6/Neuschwanstein_far_below_side.jpg
This is a view of the castle from below and to the side. The castle sits high above the small village of Schwangau in Bavaria.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/13/5fd1a746-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/70f22f72dc642df89b163162127e5583/z-Throne_room.jpg
This is a photograph of the Throne Room in Neuschwanstein. A glorious room, it is missing one important element, the throne. It was not finished prior to Ludwig's death and upon his demise, the decision was made to stop working on it.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/13/5fcd8869-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c022d5ca7786a1cdc075b85ab08bf81f/z-Singers_hall.jpg
This is the Singer's Hall in Neuschwanstein Castle.

A lover of opera, Ludwig dedicated Neuschwanstein to the operatic works of Richard Wagner. As well, he had this grotto constructed as a small token "scene" he could wander through whenever he needed a taste of opera.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/13/5fc3524b-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/beb683eac34843b6a17b2f991742a105/Neuschwanstein_from_below_front.jpg

Here we see another view of Neuschwanstein, this time from below and far away.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/13/5fb63fd5-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/167325d99708175e180d32c898ae3763/Neuschwanstein_main_gate_interior.jpg
This is a view of the interior of the Neuschwanstein gate house and the courtyard inside.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/13/5fab4a98-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/472e7d46447388c6b6b92af646e72feb/Neuschwanstein_interior_courtyard.jpg
Here we see a view of the courtyard inside the gate house of Neuschwanstein Castle.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/13/5fa405fa-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0d38e0285a6d6d4ced888831e920fa95/Hohenschwangau_from_Neuschwanstein.jpg
In this picture, we can see the Austrian Tyrolean Alps, the Alpsee Lake, and on the right side, Hohenschwangau Castle, where King Ludwig II grew up. This castle was constructed by his father, King Maximilian, to replace the original castle on the site, known as Schwanstein. This photo was taken from Neuschwanstein, which means New Schwanstein Castle.

This is a painting of St. George and the Dragon, located inside Neuschwanstein Castle. Most of the artworks inside the castle were based on the themes explored in the operas of Richard Wagner. King Ludwig dedicated the castle to Wagner's operas.
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-06-15
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c7fe811-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/049aa257048036ad186b2698c873ddd6/1-Pr-Mini-figures_suit_tie_on_belt.jpg
How Lego makes its bricks 
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c7a56b9-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8e947600543f10817e060ec615b41efe/2-C-Lego_HQ_reception.jpg
This is the reception at Lego's world headquarters. Note the way the ceiling and the chairs make up what appears to be two interlocking Lego bricks.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c75402f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c502dfac6f47b5c2cc22f504018fecad/3-M-Bin_of_ABS.jpg
All Lego bricks are made from the same ABS plastic. It comes to Lego from suppliers and is held in giant silos. Generally it is clear or red, and the color for specific bricks is added at the molding machine. This is a bin full of ABS plastic, with some additional colors mixed in.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c6eadea-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/71284fb67df417b663f5e617e1c018ec/4-M-Mold_cross-section.jpg
This is a cross-section of a Lego brick mold. First, very hot plastic is injected into the mold on the right side. It then spreads through little channels and is then pushed into the press area of the mold through very small slits. When cold water is then flushed through the mold, it cools down the plastic, and the mold opens, allowing the bricks to snap off and drop down onto a belt below.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c66c727-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2bbb9b3dfb70ccc55cc3604ce066d188/5-M-Shelf_of_molds.jpg
Currently, there are about 7,000 active molds being used at Lego. All told, it keeps about 9,000 around, with many stored on shelves like this one. The average mold is worth $72,000, with the most valuable coming in around $360,000.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c619953-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/77332f0126093443f080e74450edfb66/6-M-Where_plastic_injected.jpg
Here, we see where the plastic is injected into the press area of the mold.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c5b077f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/56488e2b1dc2b83d43241cdccd0df507/7-M-Ellipsoid_pieces_in_the_mold.jpg
Here we see two ellipsoid pieces that have just been pressed, still sitting in the mold. In a second, they will drop off and onto a belt below.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c54e6ab-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/58f5e2ae142b63727248dcc85efe0550/8-M-Ellipsoid_pieces_mold.jpg
Here we see the mold for the ellipsoid pieces from the previous picture. Note the shape of the mold.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c4e2cea-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d53aac06b8fd8febf3a5eb3905503b57/9-M-Blue_heads.jpg
Part of Lego's system is to be as efficient as possible. That means making bricks and other elements that can be used in different ways. These blue pieces can either be used as heads for mini-figures, or as decorations for other pieces.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c489dc1-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f2d6e05f743f9e9a7505ced5a0fb5c7d/10-M-Purple_pieces_coming_off_belt.jpg
Thousands of purple Lego bricks sit in a bin, having just been pressed in a mold.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c43d346-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7d4bf4d5b61a8c096cf0803191d66709/11-M-Long_aisle_of_molding_machines.jpg
This is one of 12 molding 'modules' located in Billund. Each module, or room, has as many as 64 working molding machines, divided into two aisles of 32 machines each.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c386128-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/537a946b11aab6f8b02762e00cd3665d/12-M-Robot_arm_grabbing_injection_waste.jpg
Here we see a robot arm inside the molding machine grabbing the waste from the injection and molding process. This plastic will be put into a grinder, and reused almost immediately.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c334294-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5c1da4583130c7596fbed5aea3877171/13-M-Bin_of_injenction_waste.jpg
This is a bin full of the waste plastic from each mold.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c2ec21f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/740242c24a9f74612513a14a0b721f74/14-M-Spillage.jpg
Almost none of the plastic used in the Lego factory goes to waste, but a very small amount does get spilled. This giant bin is full of spaghetti-like plastic that has hardened and has no use.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c2629e4-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ea948770ca312d505ed010e95671590d/15-M-Tubes_carrying_ABS_inside.jpg
These tubes carry ABS plastic into the molding rooms. The sound coming from the pipes is very much like what millions of grains of rice would sound like going through pipes.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c1f012a-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5550eccb785f9b703b3e24e7ab7f59d7/16-Ma-Cleaning_a_mold.jpg
A mold is used for about four weeks before it is pulled from the production line and completely cleaned out. Here, we see a Lego worker doing a meticulous cleaning of a mold.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c19aef1-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1ec358776e2ee4d7b216b41b043f1080/17-Pr-Machine_dropping_mini-figure_bodies.jpg
Now in the processing part of the production system, this machine is dropping torsos for mini-figures onto a belt where they will have additional elements like arms and legs and heads added to them.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c14f6fb-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9cdf74e5d7fd273e81ef56c200c9370e/18-Pr-Putting_hands_on_mini-figures.jpg
Here we see a small robot adding hands to a mini-figure.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c0ee4d9-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6c126e59df2597e55fa29864a1aa3dfe/19-Pr-Stamping_details_on_mini-figure_bodies.jpg
Here we see the machine stamping on printed details, such as a face, and a shirt.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c0a7625-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fb97e702bc63c75de24ab1a9215484ff/20-Pr-Weighing.jpg
This display shows the weight of a small bag of Lego bricks known as a pre-pack. The weight must be between 94.9 grams and 95.7 grams. Coming in at 94.94 grams, this back passes muster. However, as the display shows, five bags came in too light, and one was too heavy. But 5,354 were in the target range.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4c031eda-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/de9c88c1f9ffa2515504d176dcf03714/22-Pa-Pre-packages_going_up_belt.jpg
Here we see pre-packs going up a conveyor belt, at the top of which they will be weighed.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4bfbdbf9-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6dd2f53a4ac14894167b40a19100b35d/23-Pa-Putting_big_pre-packages_in_boxes_by_hand.jpg
Now in the packaging department, most of the bricks are in packs that are dropped into a package automatically. But some of the packs are too big to fit in when dropped that way and must be shaken and flattened manually in order to fit properly.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4bf62078-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/88cac0380528fd5490c9cf560a7247c2/24-Pa-Flat_Star_Wars_boxes.jpg
These are hundreds of flat boxes for Star Wars Lego sets.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4bed4c65-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/91f1334a4f4c819a886ff9abd23096cc/25-Pa-Making_sure_not_too_full.jpg
This machine presses down into each Star Wars set that passes through to make sure that the packs of Lego bricks inside isn't too high up. If it is too high, the box may not be able to be closed correctly.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4be3b2a1-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0b5fe5212e2608e7921693c7f4d718a5/28-Pa-Full_Star_Wars_boxes_go_down_the_line.jpg
Here we see full Star Wars boxes moving down the line.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4bdaf84f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4acb522ab2671fb9c92c1925aeb40063/26-Pa-Closing_lids_on_Star_Wars_boxes.jpg
Here, the machine automatically closes the lid of the box of each Star Wars Lego set.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4bd3a064-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dce899948f7acf16636b598167c44c29/29-Pa-Full_Star_Wars_boxes_come_off_line.jpg
Here we see full and closed Star Wars Lego set boxes coming off the packaging line.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4bc5d097-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8c6b7a98cae17693a28b4ff0ba0cf100/27-Pa-Putting_Star_Wars_sets_in_boxes.jpg
As the Star Wars sets are finished, this machine grabs two at a time and puts them into cases of six.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4bbfff3f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b8ac360465bb680a048f7c994550e929/30-Pa-Pulling_out_mistake_by_hand.jpg
Here, a Lego worker has to reach inside the machinery and grab two boxes that have accidentally been spun off the belt.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4bb8b785-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e82fa9dfcbdf12fe1ea9abe65d68acc1/31-Pa-Boxes_of_six_go_down_the_belt.jpg
Each of these cases contains six Star Wars Lego sets.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/14/4bae8ee9-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/05bca3e1fa7e3406ad6520a495b18634/32-Pa-Shrink-wrapped_and_ready_to_go.jpg
Shrink-wrapped and ready to go, these cases of Star Wars Lego sets will be shipped to the Czech Republic, where they will be handled by a giant distribution center there operated by DHL for Lego.
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-06-17
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/5810dd72-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ce96d73a0b77bf9ce56f347ce1f27c1e/Airport_-_Planes_being_loaded_up.jpg
Planes loading up
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/58070b02-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d2bf2491a46b22d072f0a755837aef00/Airport_-_Taking_off.jpg
Here we see a plane "taking off" from the model Hamburg airport at the Miniatur Wunderland. Planes disappear behind flaps in the wall where they then lower down on the rods that at first lifted them up during take off, and then route their way back automatically to either the tarmac and an airport gate, or to a landing.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/57fbb4ca-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2e0f95be41b5631f63ed0e3fc8b7e6e0/Airport_-_Planes_on_tarmac.jpg
The airplane collection features a Thai Airways Airbus A380, an Air France Boeing 777, and many, many others. All told, there are 32 planes in the collection, including an Airbus A350, a plane that doesn't exist yet--but Airbus contributed a model of the forthcoming plane.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/57f4b172-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0a97087d788c0c1cc3e0228e98bb2a82/Airport_-_Lufthansa_landing.jpg
A "landing" plane emerges from behind the wall.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/57ec9627-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5fa891527d1062f6ef7d251721fc9c48/Airport_-_Air_France_777_turning_for_takeoff.jpg
During a nighttime scene at the Miniatur Wunderland airport, an Air France Boeing 777 prepares to turn onto the main runway in order to "take off."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/57e68881-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/52b2bd6744dbeab753f003f34496b4fa/Airport_-_Night_scene.jpg
A plane has just landed at the Miniatur Wunderland airport, seen here during a nighttime moment. At the attraction, days last for 15 minutes, and nights for three minutes. During the nighttime periods, thousands of LED lights automatically turn on, including those seen here.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/57de00a7-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/77d61de7f8908913c438fb9873d28036/Airport_-_Cars_and_trucks_auto_charging.jpg
One of the most impressive elements of the airport is that the dozens of small cars, trucks, buses, and other vehicle models are programmed to be smart enough to know when they are running low on battery power. When that happens, they automatically drive themselves to a section behind the walls, out of view of the public, where they dock themselves at one of these stations and stay there until they are charged back up. At that point, they automatically back out, drive back out to the airport and resume their "duties."
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/57d78eeb-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8ed20dea0c940f1ef98697165c3fa125/Airport_-_Rod_comes_up_for_takeoff.jpg
The way the planes take off is simple. As they taxi down the runway, a small rod emerges from the floor and lifts them up. The programming is smart enough to know which type of plane is taking off, and modifies the angle of ascent to match that of the real-life version of the plane.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/57cce903-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b165f6db7e73ab725e9444f9cd2bb172/Airport_-_Control_screen_for_airport.jpg
This is the control screen for the Miniatur Wunderland airport, which shows details on all the planes, all the vehicles, and much more about the complex 1:87 scale model of the Hamburg, Germany airport.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/57c150ad-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/268f3b46120a9f3447c864e05a7f8746/Airport_-_Planes_hidden_lined_up.jpg
After taking off, these planes then routed themselves into a section where they line up under the publicly-viewable section of the airport and work themselves back around until they "land."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/57bb1860-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e98b21dde330cab3a6842ef6140dd6c3/Airport_-_Parking_garage.jpg
Like so much else in Miniatur Wunderland, this airport parking garage was built by hand, piece by piece.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/57a8748d-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/94207445544937fe9ec759bd111c2379/Airport_-_Shuttle_landing.jpg
Though the real Space Shuttle never landed in Hamburg, at Miniatur Wunderland, it does.
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-06-18
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43e7aa2e-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4ab75bc100f19acd2e7943a57bb2d3ce/Wide_Swiss_scene.jpg
This is a wide view of one of the Switzerland sections, including the Matterhorn.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43deece2-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a17167e7fb33a5529f9f304ce8622a5e/Hamburg_Hauptbanhof.jpg
Being Hamburg, it only makes sense that the collection would have a major section on its home city. Indeed, the Hamburg piece is one of the largest parts of the collection, and it features many of the city's landmarks. This is a large representation of Hamburg Central Station.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43d3c3ab-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6f0258a9b94ca08c19fe8d2cc93d111b/Scandanavian_winter_train_station.jpg
This is a scene from the Scandinavia section of the collection, showing what a train station there would be like during a snowy winter.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43cd3f62-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/46cbbf2c6d838e626d06ef91c00d8a0f/Rail_bridge_across_Southwestern_canyon.jpg
This is a scene from the U.S. section of the collection, featuring a rail bridge spanning a Southwestern canyon, most likely a fictional one in Utah.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43c4bb66-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/090d7e52dd27deebedd95896cfaf5efd/ICE_arriving.jpg
In this picture, we see a major train yard, while a Germany Intercity Express (ICE) train approaches.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43bb18bd-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6159eec04a183ddc1f1f04e20829977c/Train_going_under_highway_under_rail_bridge.jpg
This picture shows another scene from the U.S. section of the collection, and we see a train on tracks going underneath a freeway that is itself underneath a rail bridge. The highway has its own traffic, featuring cars and trucks that drive themselves back and forth and, due to a homemade positioning system, manage to avoid collisions with each other--most of the time.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43b383ee-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e31453eb0e5ee6e64f601c14ea57182f/Concert.jpg
Besides a gigantic collection of trains and eight miles of train tracks, the collection also has 250,000 tiny people spread around an endless number of little dioramas, each of which tells a story of some kind. Here, we see a giant concert, with 25,000 "people." There is music playing, and the little screens show video from real concerts. Out of the frame, there is also a giant field of little tents and cars, and all the other things--like portable toilets--necessary for a large outdoor musical event.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43acbf50-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d237b26b95a562d0d0478ab61cd0c4a9/Aliens_taking_over_plane.jpg
This is another scene in which the planners at Miniatur Wunderland took an opportunity to tell a story. This is from the American section of the collection and depicts what appears to be a group of aliens taking over a U.S. military plane. Presumably, this is supposed to be Roswell, N.M.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43a71a92-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d1075f1d8fe951922ae51e2d3a9fb9f9/Underground_Roswell_scene.jpg
Many of the stories being told in the collection are hidden away and you have to either spot them by accident or go looking for them. This is directly under what is seen in the previous picture and shows what is supposed to be a hidden UFO, and a whole underground base for dealing with aliens. Again, this is likely supposed to be Roswell.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/439f9edb-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e2dac081b1799f7f7146125fbb25bc15/Control_Center_wide.jpg
This is the command center for the entire Miniatur Wunderland collection. Here, technicians can monitor all the train tracks, the lighting built into the system, and all the little cars and other vehicles. They can also control more than 200 cameras to see how things are going, and whether there are any situations that must be dealt with.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43994f7e-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a0d6404286b5489fa012f9ddc19ba168/Control_Center_-_close-up.jpg
This is a close-up of the command center, showing a technician monitoring a number of screens, each of which shows a section of track. Though the trains have pre-set routines, the technicians can also intervene and direct the trains.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/4392bc00-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/244cf0d648070487c5f71d9d0566e8b1/Backstage_tracks.jpg
Behind the walls, there are endless sections of train track, like the ones seen here. This is because the train tracks must be continuous in order for the collection to be considered the world's largest, and there is not enough room in the building for all the track. So, each of the eight major sections are connected by tracks that are under the floors, in the ceilings, behind the walls, and sometimes even on little bridges in doorways.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/438b20be-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7aaf59ec715c16e546824f2085aa2f83/Sebastian_ducking_down.jpg
Here, Sebastian Drechsler, one of the three brothers who run the collection, ducks down as he explains how the behind-the-walls parts of the track system work. He's ducking because the ceilings are extremely low behind the walls.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43852792-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f2c8daf8d4717998f050c53ce0b9f867/Climbers_and_southwestern_plane_crash.jpg
In this picture, we see some climbers scaling a Southwestern canyon wall, even as the pilot of a plane that seems to have crashed into the wall parachutes to safety.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/437fdbe8-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5f4cd3db4668677ffb88efa65f9127c6/Bike_race.jpg
Here, we see a group of bicyclists in the peloton of a race crossing a big bridge.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43781b8f-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/94e6f0609f79c9b51d1e3a2e932a1451/Mille_Miglia_race.jpg
This picture depicts a scene from the Mille Miglia auto race, built into the side of one of the Swiss Alps in the collection.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43717f9f-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/53dbf63a37e3f0cce1ac05fbb03e70c6/Coming_out_of_tunnel.jpg
Here, we see a train exiting one tunnel and about to enter a second in an Alpine section of the collection.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/436afebf-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c8497f8243e3320f1c93a71e86b58631/Porta_Alpina.jpg
During the construction of the Gotthard Tunnel--the longest tunnel in the world, at 57 kilometers--planners who were trying to figure out how to divert half of the truck traffic through the Swiss Alps also thought they might want to build the world's deepest train station.
Eventually, they decided to excavate the area where the station would go, but also decided not to build the station any time soon. However, because they excavated it, they can choose to go back and build the station when the need arises.
At Miniatur Wunderland, that time has already come, and this is their representation of what the platform in the station would look like.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/4364bb0d-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dc4758db5a2d9ccff2a52812e0428661/Porta_Alpina_number_2.jpg
This is a look through a second in-the-wall window at the Porta Alpina station, this time at what it would like beyond the train platform.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/435efd21-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d0f35620ce7b0d033f1e5da283f90c82/Ski_slopes_by_trains.jpg
Here, we see an Alpine ski resort alongside a small train station.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/4354b941-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6515c92790d15a507816f46e85205065/Scandanavian_monorail.jpg
This is a monorail system in the Scandinavian section.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43485d26-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a0f29bcc1a1fb50e5a0defe95b4136f5/Las_Vegas_at_night.jpg
Daytime in Miniatur Wunderland lasts 15 minutes. Then there are three-minute nights. This is a look at the Las Vegas Strip during one of the "nighttimes."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43406b5c-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7b32355ce177b6bcfcead2d36c8508f3/Chocolate_bars.jpg
In the Switzerland section of the collection, there is a scale model of the Lindt chocolate factory, including this diorama, where a little conveyor belt pushes tiny chocolate bars through a wrapping machine. The little bars then come out on a small tray, where visitors can grab and eat them.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/4337a890-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/04bde84e31259d27afee69a77109968b/Fire_scene.jpg
This is a scene depicting a burning building and the fire department response from the Hamburg section. The scene features lots of flashing lights and siren sounds.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/432ecb2b-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0bfdcfd0c4b6aff84a68f9d33af7ab10/Train_controllers.jpg
The brothers who built the collection are very technologically savvy, and built many of the back-end systems that control all the trains, cars, and so on. These are the processors responsible for running the vast train system.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43261197-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6e1dba96ab093f7f08672f61368ddb52/Servers.jpg
These are the servers that control the entire system.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/431d56f6-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ee04e8dd01266f28407abda1e095e120/St_Max.jpg
This is a scene that is supposed to be a depiction of St. Moritz, Switzerland. Apparently, however, the town thinks its name is a trademark, and so the collection renamed the town St. Max.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43167189-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0832d705dd7e261fb8f282208b50b2dd/Swiss_scene_with_hot_air_balloons.jpg
This is a scene from the Swiss section featuring trains and three hot air balloons.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/430f8729-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ec1a534565f2526adb2fab1add12b08f/Train_arriving_Swiss_town.jpg
Here we see a scene in which a train is arriving at a small Swiss train station.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/16/43024992-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9320919209dcb3a4bb1b76ccc5162130/Train_station_-_high_angle.jpg
This is a high-angle look down at a large train station in the collection. You can see all the platforms on which trains arrive and depart.
 
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-06-19
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/17/4c3850ac-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9eb9bd1abbd1be134f5737efec1fb5e6/Purple_lamp.jpg
This lamp is made from a nylon material and was made using a 3D printing process called laser-sintering, in which a high-temperature laser is used to fuse materials together.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/17/4c33e696-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ec3898c73cd8a7ac8eb0b78ade8ef965/Stereolithographty_for_Asics.jpg
At Freedom of Creation's Amsterdam office, there are countless examples of the company's work, many of which are tests and samples. Some are also items the company created for corporate clients. This item, which isn't for sale, was created for a shoe display for Asics, using copper-plated stereolithography. Note the many, many details in the piece.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/17/4c2d82da-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a48a39c6ae90e7e9153bb9cf766df558/Break_packaging.jpg
Freedom of Creation also made this piece for Asics as part of a promotion. The item has printing on it that encourages someone finding it to break it open and find a puzzle piece inside. Each one of these items was slightly different, and all were made in their own individual 3D print job. All that was different between each job was that the digital file was manipulated a bit to change some of the details. The puzzle piece is loose inside the frame, something that can be done with 3D printing, but would be nearly, if not totally, impossible using traditional manufacturing methods.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/17/4c26b149-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/438d678982b599ddaef0c221e2414524/iPad_case.jpg
This is a Freedom of Creation iPad case made out of nylon using a laser-centering 3D printer from 3D Systems. It costs $127. Many of Freedom of Creation's designs have a signature printed on them of the person that created the item, as does this case.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/17/4c1e37d6-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/797c3c21f90e954b3c0b9865c0245c5e/iPhone_case.jpg
Freedom of Creation is part owner of a separate company called Fresh Fiber that makes "fashion for electronics." It sells a variety of items like this iPhone case, which is available at Apple Stores for $41.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/17/4c18866c-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/605c7805a55c9cb3f988854e32fbdb4e/Dress.jpg
This dress features a complex design with interlocking chains of nylon-based links. It was a test piece done by Freedom of Creation, but it shows how 3D printing can be used to make items with elements like the interlocking chain that would be extremely difficult using normal production methods.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/17/4c14b5f7-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b81f0ced22dce19ed1b079ae843b2c65/Handbag.jpg
This is a handbag featuring a handle made to look like brass knuckles. It's made from nylon using a laser-sintered 3D printer. It sells for around $429.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/17/4c0fae91-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/68ba67c58b3941f1f289df00d4e8a279/Sand_fruit_tray.jpg
3D printers are capable of making products using a wide variety of materials. This is a fruit tray made from sand on a laser-sintering 3D printer. Yet, despite being made from sand, it is quite strong and is made to last.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/17/4c09198a-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/84749a02b1c01d9f33f67e2f65eb970d/Aluminum_8s.jpg
This item was made from a 3D printed wax cast that was then used as the basis for the final product, made from aluminum.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/17/4c001e12-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3b92742b19afdb680426dcc231d3abf9/Headphones.jpg
The base shape for these headphones, which are a Freedom of Creation prototype, are made out of nylon using a laser-sintering 3D printer.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/17/4bf963e5-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f8fc4f2ec89912a86143940f9a820cb2/New_bracelets.jpg
These bracelets are two of Freedom of Creation's newest products. They're made from three materials--glass, aluminum, and nylon. The laser-sintering 3D printer melts the nylon, which then binds all the materials together. The bracelets are sold for between $42 and $57 apiece.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/18/4bf13e7e-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/25111e6cbbead3f3dfeeeea9c2b123ba/Rings.jpg
This is a collection of rings designed by FOC, all done in nylon, and each costing around 27 euros.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/18/4be6c481-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/84a10514425df3f7eae6006b1550c211/3D_printer.jpg
This is a consumer-level 3D printer sold by Freedom of Choice's new owner, 3D Systems. It can't produce objects as high-quality as the ones in the previous pictures, but it can make a lot of functional things. It is available for $1,850.
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-06-20
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/57b48307-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/04f4db6ea88f9c44dba3ff65b22e682a/RAF_Hawk_TMK2.jpg
The plane is outfitted with OC2--which provides simulated radar and sensor functions that include defensive aids, a radar warning receiver, and "an enhanced weapons suite."
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/57ab09d1-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/028ac0155dce68332080d0df55708793/C-130_in_flight.jpg
This is the U.S. Air Force's C-130, seen in flight during a demonstration at the Paris Air Show.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/57a661bf-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/085da5bee1983652eb1250e50fba32ac/A400M.jpg
This is Airbus' A400M, a military cargo plane that is built to carry very large cargo loads, both by volume and weight. It flies at high altitude and at high speeds, and is built to land on soft and short airfields, meaning that it can deliver cargo wherever it is needed.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/57a22dec-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/839c0672e979e2b5c39b81763f7b61c8/C-5M_Galaxy_and_A400M.jpg
In this picture, we see two large military cargo planes, the C-5M Galaxy (left) and the Airbus A400M.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/579a595b-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e06a418b49ced28af20bdf64f7fee172/Dassault_stealth.jpg
This is a model of a Dassault stealth fighter, seen today at the Paris Air Show.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/57967432-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a9b5988603ba710b9c88e60b764564de/Fighter_flying.jpg
This fighter jet flew during demonstrations at the Paris Air Show at Le Bourget today.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/57912b2b-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d18d053cdd4a4201be981d56f3b12242/ETOP.jpg
This is ETOP, "a pure electric power airborne tethered observation platform," from IAI, as seen at the Paris Air Show. It features: low maintenance, full automation, low noise, unlimited hovering, single-click operation, "deployment to full operation and recovery to basic status within seconds," and more. It has a maximum payload of 44 pounds, and can carry multiple sensors. It can fly up to 328 feet high.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/578a32c1-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ccac8f42a328d327f3bf85e844a4821f/AH-1Z_helicopter.jpg
This is the AH-1Z from Bell Helicopter, as seen at the Paris Air Show. Used for attack and close air support operations, as well as aerial reconnaissance, armed escorting, control of supporting arms, and night and adverse weather operations, it weighs about 18,500 pounds. It can carry up to 6,733 pounds of payload, and has two T-700-GE-401 engines.
It can fly up to a radius of 125 nautical miles, cruising at 3,000 feet. Its maximum endurance is 3.3 hours, and it has a maximum airspeed of 200 knots.
The AH-1Z features 6 wing weapon stations, and 4 universal and 16 PGM weapon stations. It carries up to 16 Hellfire missiles, as well as 2 AIM-9 Sidewinder air-to-air missiles, as well as a 20mm gun, and 76 2.75-inch rocket pods.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/57822429-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d482d671d1fee2a4f1b0012ac4d51e10/Blackhawk_S-70i.jpg
This is the Blackhawk S-70i, from Sikorsky. It has a maximum takeoff weight of 22,000 pounds, can cruise at a maximum speed of 149 knots, has a range of 272 nautical miles, and can carry 11 soldiers plus two crew chiefs.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/577bdf40-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d33d8dad7b70089efbdaab67f719279a/AW159_Lynx_Wildcat_helicopter.jpg
This is the AW159 Lynx Wildcat, from AgustaWestland.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/57746554-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8aab82efd63f3e38cd520cf0ed4fe073/C-130_and_A380.jpg
In this photo from the Paris Air Show, we see the U.S. military's C-130 cargo plane (left) alongside the Airbus A380, the world's largest commercial passenger airplane.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/57701b61-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b07f201d1b7c1b2ad10699ada917240a/CH148_helicopter.jpg
This is the CH148 Cyclone, from Sikorsky. It has a maximum gross weight of 29,300 pounds, and a maximum cruising speed of 140 knots. It can hold 22 troops.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/576c9986-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c93d40a15cecba00bd75057f0b0f560f/Dassault_unmanned.jpg
This is an unmanned aircraft from Dassault.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/5765bfda-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c93a4de9105e4030a9f4c07c7aa88586/Eurofighter_typhoon.jpg
This is the Eurofighter Typhoon, which can reach Mach 2.0 and reach up to 9G. It is 15.96 meters long and 5.28 meters high.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/57605557-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/994306e08aff6c157cc1970b31f692a8/F-15E.jpg
This is the F-15E fighter, a mainstay of America's military.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/5757e2b9-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6ada3469b69d5450ffa2f011b1e1ec02/F-16.jpg
This is the F-16, another important American fighter jet.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/5750861c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/049939231e0e7b3cbe8a8c0d41f07b58/Flying_robots.jpg
This is the Flying Robot, a "soft wing UAV." It can carry a payload of up to 250 kilograms, and reach up to 80 kilometers an hour. It can fly for up to 20 hours autonomously, and cover more than 1,200 kilometers. It is a fully automatic unmanned aerial vehicle that can be optionally manned.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/574a33b8-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2049435c08ed27741a1361d9d8bdb70f/Guardian_UAV.jpg
This is the Guardian, an American UAV.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/5745342c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e898b39393353fb3d17aa3bd2e1a423a/Italian_C-27J_Spartan.jpg
This is the Italian C-27J Spartan, used by seven countries, including the U.S., "the only true, modern, and effective tactical airlifter available anywhere in the world," according to the manufacturer.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/573fb636-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4037d885813f20626216d82d723a8971/Italian_Coast_Guard.jpg
This is the Italian Coast Guard ATR 42 MP, a turboprop aircraft used for maritime patrols, developed by Alenia Aeronautica.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/5739b760-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6ca9e2ca26f90457f65ce82e400b9be1/Italian_UAV.jpg
This is the Sky-Y UAV, developed by Alenia Aeronautica. It is a demonstration of Italian unmanned aerial technology.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/5732e11b-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fd68b0c007b82a5ab6d7366e124e0844/NH90_Italian_Helicopter.jpg
This is the NH-90 NATO frigate helicopter, operated by the Italian Navy. "Designed to operate from surface vessels, a highly integrated mission system allows autonomous and net-centric antisubmarine warfare, antisurface warfare, and surface-to-air roles without role change," according to the manufacturer.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/572c659f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e6cabb992821f8ead929b0299d61f146/RQ-7_Tactical_UAV.jpg
This is the RQ-7 tactical unmanned aerial system, from AIA--Aerospace Industries Association. It is known as Shadow, and it's used by the U.S. Army and Marines. It can travel up to 90 knots for cruising, and 60 knots for "loitering."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/5726fceb-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ba1fe4c6bc3435c598d5d54af82607de/Talarian_and_drone.jpg
On the left is the Talarion, a UAV from Cassidian that is a "long-endurance aerial drone system designed for surveillance, reconnaissance, and target acquisition." On the right is a very small unmanned drone from Cassidian and EADS Innovation Works.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/5722e0ab-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0ddfbc5d4aa9615fadacde1ad077a16e/Thales_Watchkeeper.jpg
This is the Watchkeeper, from Thales, an unmanned air system that "delivers accurate and timely combat ISR--intelligence, surveillance, and reconnaissance--information to decision makers at all levels of command right down to the soldier on the battlefield."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/571a8695-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7556d3ddae73d5b78edf6cc4f2b6a9f1/UH-1Y_helicopter.jpg
This is the UH-1Y from Bell Helicopter. It is used for, among other things, airborne command and control, assault transport, search and rescue, night and adverse weather operations, aerial reconnaissance, control of supporting arms, aeromedical missions, and more. It can carry up to 6,797 pounds, and travel with a radius of as much as 130 nautical miles. Its top cruising speed is 150 knots. It carries 2.75-inch rocket pods.
 
 
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-06-21
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3ecb2917-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e0ad3c6541fb948892b5cd2fd902f734/The_Paris_Air_Show_1.jpg
Here, we see the 747-8 I, as it's called, sitting on the tarmac at Le Bourget, home to the Paris Air Show.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3ec65563-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ede5d77872667e134716a69076eddda4/Flight_deck.jpg
This is the flight deck of the 747-8 I. While there are a few tweaks to the existing 747-400 cockpit design, Boeing decided to leave things very much as they have been so that airlines don't need to fully retrain their pilots to fly the new plane. Instead, they need just three days of instruction to get ready to pilot the Intercontinental.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3ebfc3f2-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/23973efe8ad2ba22b43cfc240cc3c4ad/Captain_explains_flight_deck.jpg
Here, Boeing chief pilot Mark Feuerstein explains some of the features of the flight deck of the 747-8 I to CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3ebbe99b-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5874f1d5b1a120de5d3f4279b9f074ec/Back_at_top_level.jpg
The upper deck of the 747-8 Intercontinental is as long as an entire 737-700, Boeing says. Here, we see the upper deck on the first 747-8 I as it looks at the Paris Air Show--without seats, but with its luggage bins and lighting installed.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3eadae50-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4539b819c031664fdccb98e83efea0ce/Ceiling.jpg
On this 747-8 I, the full interior fixtures have not yet been installed, and visitors can see the ceiling of the plane above the luggage bins. This plane will eventually be fully outfitted as a VIP plane by Boeing Business Jets.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3ea94ac1-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/de0d5d44edaeeb5a915e71782b2ceddf/Captain_rest_cabin.jpg
One of the chief advantages of the 747-8 I, according to Boeing chief pilot Mark Feuerstein, is that there is a bathroom and rest quarters behind the flight deck door. This means that pilots will not need to open the door to the cockpit in order to go to the rest room, something that should increase security and allow pilots to not have to wait until service is finished before getting up.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3ea666b0-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4c175a0bd488ed607c48babcc17e6f65/Captain_poses.jpg
Capt. Feuerstein poses for a picture in the cockpit of the first 747-8 Intercontinental.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3ea0d358-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f9fefdb45bb53bf08944c2c1d5c00905/Checklist.jpg
Here, we see the electronic checklist, one of the new digital additions to the 747-8 I's flight deck.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3e9bd648-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9766f9038095ce508fe862a0d6ab4e3a/Moving_map.jpg
This is the moving map, another new digital addition to the 747-8 Intercontinental.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3e94dff9-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3e07c771edc6bf009f3c39fb239978d5/Equipment.jpg
The 747-8 Intercontinental that flew to the Paris Air Show is the first of the new line and hasn't yet been outfitted with normal seats. In fact, it is still full of testing equipment and water tanks used to distribute weight.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3e906aa5-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ac67128b9a1be7174a2996f91409cd1e/Main_deck_with_water_tanks.jpg
This is a view of the main deck of the 747-8 I, including the many water tanks used to distribute weight around the plane in the absence of hundreds of passengers.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3e8c7847-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0336c48c33b9e23c114784bee27acbc0/Crew_cabin.jpg
In the very rear of the plane, there is an eight-bunk crew rest area.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/20/3e87c9c2-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/73869cdfa8bec97634b5fdaeffdf17f5/Down_the_stairs.jpg
This is a look down the stairs from the upper deck of the new 747-8 I.

This is a look at the 747-8 I's new, highly aerodynamic wing, as seen from the inside of the plane.

On the right side of the flight deck, a digital display shows a map of Le Bourget, the airfield where the 747-8 I made its international debut Monday.

[Date]2011-06-22
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/582c26f8-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7c59f0b83c078cb0e0d3045145397919/Fighter_flying.jpg
Paris Air Show at Le Bourget here are military personnel looking to buy their next order of helicopters or fighter jets,
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/58276ff4-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4947f3052eeb98c5521c38e471af2f2a/Ground_crew_poses.jpg
Each plane that will be flying an exhibition flight at the Paris Air Show must be very slowly and carefully towed to the flight line, usually through large throngs of people who want to get as close to the aircraft as possible, even as it rolls along the tarmac. The job of keeping the audience back falls to a ground crew, and here we see that ground crew posing for a photograph with a giant Airbus A380--the world's largest passenger plane--behind them, about to turn onto the runway for its show flight.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/5820edb9-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a874f36361d361aa04d5e8eea9d7f9cc/Military_officers_walking_in_group.jpg
At the Paris Air Show, there are top military brass from countries all over the world wandering everywhere. They can be seen in small groups, often with younger officers escorting a colonel or a general, or in large clusters, as in this picture. They come from many different continents, and it's clear they've come to the show to investigate what equipment to spend money on.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/581ab9c0-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/aa1b2d98d71cbb526a5ae538455567e9/A380_takes_off.jpg
The Airbus A380--with Korean Air livery--takes off for its exhibition flight at the Paris Air Show.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/58150c39-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1553645ee2cdc7ac14926a3dec9db346/Plane_over_tail_of_A380.jpg
As the Airbus A380 waits for its turn to fly, a Russian firefighting tanker plane flies by doing its own exhibition. Here, it appears as though the Russian plane is on the tail of the A380.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/580e5c4c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/cf723824153f7ac5facf1c85bf18b11c/747-8_I_and_777_and_A380.jpg
In this photo, we can see three planes that compete for the business of the world's travelers. On the left is Boeing's new 747-8 Intercontinental, which is expected to begin flying commercially later this year. In the middle is Boeing's 777-200LR Worldliner. And on the right is the Airbus A380, the world's largest passenger plane.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/5808b2d8-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/54c2b31a5e32622d5983413a554cd1ce/Boeing_chalet.jpg
At the Paris Air Show, it's all about where you're located. The biggest companies in the military and aviation business pay to have "chalets" located close to the flight line and the so-called "static displays" of airplanes on the tarmac at Le Bourget, the airfield that hosts the show. The chalets can be small and modest, or they can be large and extravagant. This is Boeing's chalet. Based on its location, a few down from the edge of the flight line, it's not quite as well-situated as that of Airbus, which is just adjacent to the flight line. Then again, Boeing is an American company, while Airbus is a European operation.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/5800d916-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/62821f308f37ead6958d2cf2e3075d16/Crowd_waits_for_A380.jpg
A crowd gathers along the edge of the flight line, waiting to see the Airbus A380 make its flight. The airplane in the foreground is the A380.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/57fbc3bb-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/db6b89973a94d7b0d6c1177ca110d967/Fighter_flying_over_A380.jpg
Here, we see a fighter jet doing its exhibition and flying over the top of the A380.

The fighter is seen here with a long trail of smoke.

A military officer photographs an American guardian unmanned aerial vehicle.

Here, we see the Russian firefighting tanker just after dropping a load of water over the runway during its exhibition flight at the Paris Air Show.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/57e592b0-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/af751350950f778a5e265b4884907437/Pilot_and_Star_of_Switzerland.jpg

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/57df3dfc-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a87cc886b73b44f9e5a51bffc1feb216/Pulling_helicopter_to_flying.jpg
As each aircraft is taken to the flight line for its performance, crews must pull them through the length of the display area at Le Bourget. Often that can mean parting a veritable sea of attendees. Here, we see a military helicopter being pulled through the crowds.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/57d2568e-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2a3c864969090d0094c592ba0e368e79/Pushing_small_biplane.jpg
Here's another performance plane, in this case a small bi-plane, being pushed through the crowd toward the flight line. This job is easy with a plane of this size, but when it's a giant aircraft like the Airbus A380, it is a huge undertaking requiring many people to clear the plane's way.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/21/57cadfdd-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/101c6968fffb910b13de31efca59ab12/Senator_Shelby.jpg

CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman spotted similar Northrop carts being used for other members of a Senate delegation to the show, including Sen. James Inhofe (R-Okla.); Sen. Dan Inouye (D-Hawaii); and Sen. Jeff Sessions (R-Ala.).
