A view across the tarmac at Le Bourget, home to this week's Paris Air Show.
 
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-06-24
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2aeca11b-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/623a0d63dfa12324313a8f4e9d7344c9/Electromagnetic_Combabitility_chamber.jpg
This is the Electromagnetic Compatibility chamber in ESTEC's Test Center. It is a Farraday Cage used to measure all electrical properties of equipment being tested in a clean environment.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2ae60d7e-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/cfd3bd64c8ba1663e5d525a2923462d8/EMC_Cones.jpg
These are cones in the Electromagnetic Compatibility Chamber. They are crucial for isolating electrons in the room when measurements are being done.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2ae15468-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/85f31eba02edaecc7e58f124a8950be0/Acoustic_Chamber.jpg
This is the Acoustic Chamber at ESTEC's Test Center. It is employed to reproduce the noise made by rockets in order to evaluate whether satellites or other space-bound specimen can survive the intense sounds of a launch.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2adb92c8-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dcadb36a74387a3ee117859db83d1179/Big_speaker.jpg
This is the biggest of four large speakers in the Acoustic Chamber. Measurements will be conducted for about one or two minutes, approximately the amount of time that the most intense noise of a launch lasts. The noise can create vibrations strong enough to damage a satellite or other device, and it's better to learn what damage will happen in a test center than in space after an expensive launch.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2ad664d2-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1b6c74228775b092f76373b81b5adea4/Dummy_satellite_on_shaker.jpg
Another major feature of the Test Center at ESTEC is its shaker. This is used to evaluate whether satellites or other specimen are capable of handling the shaking produced during a powerful launch. Though there are other shakers located throughout Europe, this one is used when ESA customers--often telecommunications companies testing satellites--can't find another one big enough. Here, we see a dummy satellite on top of the shaker.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2ad1d3f7-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0f9c6c79ccc2e97c71b88940aa8d7347/Fuselage_ready_for_shaker.jpg
 
Here, we see an airplane fuselage that is ready to be moved onto the ESTEC shaker. This is a prime example of how non-space industries use the shaker--another shaker big enough to evaluate the worthiness of the fuselage couldn't be found outside ESTEC.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2ac70009-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/584c36b8f8bbe567d1f6cea0c26ed6a1/Small_space_simulator.jpg
This is ESTEC's Small Space Simulator, a smaller version of the facility's Large Space Simulator (LSS). The simulators are used to test spacecraft in realistic space conditions, and the main difference between LSS and the SSS is that the latter has no sun simulator.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2ac09d83-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dc8f43f725434bdca10e2ec0c40be2c7/Small_space_simulator_inside.jpg
This is a look inside the Small Space Simulator, one of the largest vacuum chambers in all of Europe.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2aba16a2-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/609252f725b5af6540f6eea44f7aa49f/Drop_tower.jpg
This is a scale-model drop tower in ESTEC's ERASMUS human spaceflight section. The drop tower is used to measure the effects on various experiments of several seconds of microgravity.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2ab13504-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8cac49803f51bdfac8d2aeb0d4432d45/ISS_model.jpg
This is a scale model of the International Space Station, found in the ERASMUS human spaceflight section at ESTEC.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2aabeba7-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/434fb363d35be6a08cf7d7842105cd46/Columbus_from_outside.jpg
The ESA's major contribution to the International Space Station was its Columbus module, which offers astronauts aboard the ISS a series of tools for various experiments. Here, we see a mockup of Columbus from the outside.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2aa5c37e-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d4b9812d9fc11b9efad0fb27129eb9ce/Columbus_mockup.jpg
This is the interior of the mockup of Columbus, as seen at ESTEC.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2a9e84c9-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/86b564e3433fc8ad740ef8e01e4229f9/Glove_box.jpg
This is a replica of the glove box ESA provided for use on Columbus on the ISS. The glove box is used for keeping scientific experiments 100 percent uncontaminated.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2a977a73-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2ee65d90f88bb433aae55a1363d00c37/Exercise_facility_on_ISS.jpg
Columbus includes an exercise bike and some other equipment that are used by astronauts aboard the ISS.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2a8f90de-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2427fc00a795d0766a003058168876d3/Eurobot_ground_prototype.jpg
This is the Eurobot Ground Prototype, a mockup of a rover the ESA is anticipating may be sent to the moon or, someday, Mars.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2a86ecbb-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c9c407a8d57d5d9fe795ee7cb16dd508/Parabolic_flight_trainer.jpg
This is ESTEC's parabolic flight trainer. It is used to help train those who will be taking parabolic rides on a plane for the conditions they will encounter. Such flights are useful for conducting experiments with microgravity.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2a7f987b-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e6f0f47f6d09f75a24599089a07d54d0/Demonstrating_flexible_exoskeleton.jpg
In this picture, Joao Rebelo of ESTEC's Robotics and Haptics Labs demonstrates a new kind of exoskeleton that is designed to allow people of many different body heights and types to practice controlling a robotic hand that is located elsewhere.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/22/2a77f228-f0f4-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d5f6ddc362b8a6ed57b65557dbc2e491/Authory_trying_non-flexible_exoskeleton.jpg
Here, CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman tries out an exoskeletal arm that is used to practice controlling a robotic hand. This model can control a digital version of the distant hand, meaning that the user can try to manipulate "objects" on a screen. The exoskeleton has built-in haptics, which offer force-feedback.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-06-26   
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d833f96-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/71d31acf76d77100187b546d9ec9c035/Low_wide_angle_Eiffel_Tower.jpg
Here, we see the Eiffel Tower from just in front of it.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d7c33ca-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1f3b7e6ebbd8727f65216772bcbc6b0d/Accumulateur_and_lift.jpg
Some might consider the hydraulic elevator system that Gustave Eiffel designed in 1889 to be well ahead of its time. Here's how it works, according to information provided by the organization that runs The Eiffel Tower:
The elevator "passenger compartments, mounted on a carriage and kept horizontal by a leveling system, are pulled upwards by cables that move in line with two parallel pistons located underground, via a cable drum system--the cables themselves, the ends of which are attached to the passenger compartment carriage--run back and forth eight times over two sets of pulleys, one of which is fixed and the other attached to the moving pistons, thereby ensuring that the [elevator] passenger compartments can travel [420 feet], i.e. eight times the piston travel--of 52 feet.
"The pistons are actuated by a water circuit with a pressure of 40 to 60 bar, which until 1986 generated motion thanks to three large accumulators of some 200 metric tons each, which provided both the pressurized water reserve--the energy to drive the motion--and the counterweight function.
"Since modernization in 1986, high-pressure, oil-driven hydraulic motors drive piston carrier motion while two of the three accumulators serve as counterweights."
Here we see two of the hydraulic lifts and one of the accumulators.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d73cdb5-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7e427278990eb7e31b6d5d5a1a1e9426/Red_wheels_of_hydraulic_lift.jpg
These big red wheels are one of the sets of pulleys mentioned in the previous captions. These are the pulleys attached to the moving pistons.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d6d6a74-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/05477d060bbfe8e8828ed273d729fec1/The_bunker.jpg
Just adjacent to the south pillar of the Eiffel Tower, there is a nondescript green cage. This is, in fact, the entrance to a "secret" underground bunker, which was originally designed to belong to the French military. Although today the bunker is open to small guided public groups and contains a collection of Eiffel Tower memorabilia, posters, and pictures, it still connects to military tunnels, and the public is only allowed to go a very short distance inside. It's not known how far or where the tunnels go, but legend has it that they stretch all the way from the Eiffel Tower, under the famous Champ de Mars, and to the Ecole Militaire, the major French military academy.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d645c24-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c6e5825de81b268bec3bd05c6cc966c9/Down_into_bunker.jpg
Here, a small public group heads down into the "secret" bunker.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d5d545f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a09dcd40b1949ae129ef58012eb6c4e3/How_hydraulic_elevator_works.jpg
This diagram illustrates the principles of the hydraulic elevator, as explained in the second caption. Today, two of the elevators are still hydraulic, but two are electric and were installed by Gustave Eiffel for the World's Fair of 1900. But the electric elevators have proved to be more fragile and require more maintenance. Two hydraulic elevators remain to this day as a cultural reminder of the origins of the tower.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d57b6e3-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/97ce482838610648ccbda22235fceef9/Top_of_the_tower--antenna.jpg
This is a view of the very top of the Eiffel Tower, an antenna that rises above everything else. Visitors can reach the third level of the tower but still must look up at a group of antennas and other telecommunications equipment.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d502a02-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c0a98288eb816796a4f09c57122d49a8/Up_at_second_level.jpg
Here, we see a wide-angle view of the underside of the Eiffel Tower's second level, as seen from the first level.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d4c1001-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9514f42f38e8e9c1d2b08279328971b8/Stairway_and_elevator_down.jpg
Here we see one of the tower's hydraulic elevators, just adjacent to one of the famed yellow accumulators that helps drive the system that brings the elevators up and takes them down again.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d43fdc3-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/281855bbee006af57a0029baef3a9818/Growth.jpg
This chart, on the wall inside the main administration office for the Eiffel Tower, shows the tower's growth during its construction period from 1887 to 1889.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d3c8a85-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5c6a0e8bc1e73d0e5a0b363553047457/Up_through_the_leg.jpg
Here, we have a view up through one of the legs (pillars) of the Eiffel Tower.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d377cc0-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/458267ab7a0364ba60cf7f81a726557a/Staircase_in_the_leg.jpg
Here, you can see one of the spiral staircases that wind up--or down--through the legs (pillars) of the Eiffel Tower.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d3131a7-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/489f37bb86c9e3eb4e6aaf85b38cbffb/Looking_down_from_first_level.jpg
This is a view down at the ground underneath the Eiffel Tower from its first level. You can see the characteristic arches of the lower parts of the tower, as well as its iconic shadow.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d2ad04c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/84aa62f38b87b8165502a3fa1acc3bed/Looking_up_at_very_top--antennas.jpg
In this photograph, taken from the third level of the Eiffel Tower, you can see the very tip of the tower and many of the antennas and cell phone repeaters that are located at the top.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d268692-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9a724d17e8bb813ef2c0be15807ed4e2/Worker.jpg
Here and there around the Eiffel Tower are mannequins of workers who helped build or maintain the famous monument.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d20e889-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6f3318905abc9fcfd280739c21ab0e1e/Eiffel.jpg
This is a bust of Gustave Eiffel, located just adjacent to the tower's administrative offices in the north pillar.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d18a7f1-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4ddfe30ed8431fd8940872ef1af5f9ce/Climbing_is_forbidden.jpg
Just in case anyone needed a reminder, this sign, located in a hard-to-see place, reminds people that climbing the Eiffel Tower is not allowed.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4d129590-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0c55fab05d15ce63bf557c4c8e2ae7d3/Foundation_Caissons.jpg
This is a photograph of a historical picture of the Eiffel Tower showing what are called the foundation caissons, essentially waterproof metal cages that made it possible to construct the tower's foundation adjacent to the famous Seine River of Paris.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4cf67920-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/626963afd93f60c63371a6b4e42be464/To_New_York_and_DC.jpg
On the third level of the Eiffel Tower, there are markers showing the distances from the tower to famous cities around the world in the direction you're looking. Here, we see the sign showing the distance to New York City,  Washington, D.C., and (more roughly) to Mexico.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4ce7666e-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2b2439f0ba6b77a3b8e9213a58f9335d/Metro_crossing_river.jpg
For many of those who have gone up the Eiffel Tower, one memorable sight is that of one of Paris' metros crossing the Seine on the bridge near the tower.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4cdee326-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9553bde9e82cd522c7a02f7b79c59b4e/Bar_a_Champagne.jpg
For those who don't bring their own to toast with, there is a champagne bar located on the third--highest--level of the Eiffel Tower.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4cd86ddd-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3eb91a93a07ee1ab3c5deb49fcf38ce4/Eiffel_and_Edison.jpg
There is an apartment on the third--highest--level of the tower, and those who look in the windows will see mannequins of Gustave Eiffel (right) and inventor Thomas Edison. The two were friendly, and Edison visited the apartment, which Eiffel kept for entertaining illustrious visitors, on September 10, 1889.
