Here we see one of the hydraulic elevators inside the structure of the tower.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4ca3c903-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d6ea5a8edccb23017f62cdec77eee546/Ghost_passageway.jpg
This is a passageway inside the military bunker that is said to have ghosts behind it.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4c99779d-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2d6f8da43badd3eda1e85fdcc992156f/Station_radiotelegraphique_militaire.jpg
According to a plaque in the military bunker, "Gustave Eiffel knew that in order to survive, the Eiffel Tower would have to prove its practical worth for science. The tower was scheduled to be dismantled after just 20 years. [So] he...allowed it to be used for a wide range of scientific experiments, from meteorological and astronomical observations to monitoring physical and air resistance phenomena.
"He secured the future of his tower with the installation of a huge wireless transmission antenna." The tower then became the site of the first radiotelegraph broadcasts of the early 20th century, the plaque says.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/24/4c8fe026-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3ae389bd71b2fc3ba3becb0d679be520/Queen_Elizabeth.jpg
In 1935, the Eiffel Tower transmitter became active, and on June 2, 1953, English Queen Elizabeth II's coronation was broadcast live throughout France from the Eiffel Tower.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-06-28    
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4ef14670-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b46cc10ac5aa9c8b6f38f4814116f168/OBM-Barge_with_a_charge.jpg
This is a landing craft, known as a Weasel--or "Barge with a Charge"--that is said to have been one of the very first types of vehicles that was driven onto Omaha Beach on D-Day. This model of a Weasel is housed at the Omaha Beach Memorial Museum.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4eec2802-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8655577acd8d6eedac9e7490c86bfdc9/Ar-Arromanche_pont_cleaned_up.jpg
One of the reasons for the success of the entire Normandy campaign was the construction and use of what were known as Mulberries. These were giant artificial ports built by the British and the Americans to bring in huge amounts of provisions and supplies after D-Day.
In order to build a Mulberry, large numbers of artificial barges were towed into harbors at Arromanches-les-Bains and at Omaha Beach and used to protect long artificial bridges that connected supply ships with the land. Once the ships were connected to the land, all manner of vehicles could simply be driven off of them and onto shore.
Though the artificial harbors were constructed at both places, a furious storm destroyed the one at Omaha Beach, leaving Arromanches-les-Bains as the main artery for supply for the Allies.
Here, in Arromanches, we see a restored section of one of the bridges. Many of these would be linked together in order to drive supplies off the ships and onto the beach.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4ee54278-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3beaaef1f001f6344e0a756267c07329/Ar-Arromanche_Phoenix_on_beach.jpg
The barges used in the construction of the artificial harbor were known as Phoenixes. At Arromanches, there are still about a dozen Phoenixes visible above the water line, and some are now on the shore. Here, we see one of them on the beach, where it is a popular structure for beachgoers to climb on.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4eddad2e-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1646c37f63ca4da6446cdcd38d0008d2/Ar-Arromanche_port.jpg
In this photograph, we see a model of the artificial harbor constructed at Arromanches-les-Bains. At the top of the photo, we can see the long line of barges used as a seawall to protect the harbor, and below that, we can see several long lines of bridge sections connecting supply ships with the shore.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4ed6b45b-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fa6febcde1e60b060b08939e7f609a23/Ar-Anchor_that_made_it_possible.jpg
Before the artificial harbor could work as a way to bring in supply ships--and therefore supply the troops fighting the Germans inland in Normandy--a problem had to be solved: How could you anchor the Phoenixes to each other in such a way that they would not sink, split apart, or otherwise be made useless by heavy waves or bad weather?
The problem was first raised by Winston Churchill in 1942. But the solution came from British Maj. Allan Beckett, who, according to a plaque in Arromanches, designed "the floating roadways, their piers, and the 'Kite' anchors, which enabled the harbor to survive the storm of June 13 to 18, 1944, and to become the key to victory in Normandy.
This is one of the 'Kite' anchors designed by Beckett.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4ec9ef92-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/153a08361193664490440e0c837dfaa6/Ar-Mulberry_Phoenix_model.jpg
This is a model of a Phoenix located in the Musee du Debarquement in Arromanches-les-Bains. Note the antiaircraft gun turret mounted in the middle.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4ec2308e-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/66765b66c7f9dbb7f35b4277e43cf406/Ar-Mulberry_Bridge_to_shore.jpg
Here we see a model of the bridges to the shore that were constructed in order to bring supplies in from ships.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4ebc23ff-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/82d058ab58a54a5e6d0eab74d779f90b/Ar-Troops_moving_up_slope.jpg
According to information in the museum in Arromanches, "On June 6th, Col. Talley was the 5th Army Corps observer surveying Omaha Beach from a landing craft. After some very anxious moments, he could finally send a positive message to his commanding officer, Gen. Gerow. The message read, "I join you thanking God for our Navy," and it subsequently became a famous D-Day quote implying the decisive role the warships played in dealing with the German defenses.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4eb522cb-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5bd7e90bd5cdb1a6165cfbfeb2742fee/Lo-Whole_battery_gun_raised.jpg
This is one of the four 155mm guns the Germans placed at Longues-sur-Mer, each with a range of 12 miles, enough to threaten the landing fleets at Gold and Omaha beaches.
According to a sign at the site, the batteries were made of "layers of earth, on an inclined plan, [which] rested on the...sidewalls. They were designed to cushion against bomb impacts, to prevent it from tipping over in the event of a near miss, and to conceal the blockhouses."
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4eab2c44-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8c22ae9adbbefec75d4dd9477b5c414b/Lo-Front_of_targeting_station.jpg
This is the range-finding post for the Longues-sur-Mer batteries. It was located 300 yards in front of the batteries, on the edge of the cliff, and it was protected by machine-gun nests, as well as "Tobruks"--concrete niches mounted with a weapon or tank turret, according to the sign--mines, and barbed wire. The post was outfitted with a telemetric aiming device to help the crew reach their targets accurately.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4ea34d94-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9dc637e1b16ad1456aa5dc6b1c482305/Lo-Back_of_battery_rubble.jpg
According to the sign at the Longues-sur-Mer site, overnight on June 5 and into June 6, the Allies fired a thousand tons of bombs at the Longues-sur-Mer batteries, but made little ground there.
Early on June 6, the battery became engaged in a duel with the American cruiser Arkansas and two French ships. The battery "stopped firing, then trained its guns on the...headquarters of the 3rd Infantry, which was forced to [back off]. The cruiser Ajax replied. Again the battery fell silent, until the afternoon, when it resumed firing on the beaches at Colleville [Omaha Beach] and Asnelles.
"Late in the evening, the Arkansas and [the French ships] knocked out the battery once and for all."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/27/4e9c763d-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5a7c11f7abeab185aaf40b3619d47f4a/PDH-point.jpg
This site, west of Omaha Beach, and east of Utah Beach, is Pointe-du-Hoc. The site was thought by the Germans to be safe from attack from the beach due to the cliffs atop which it sat, which had perfect views of both Omaha and Utah beaches. The Germans maintained long-range artillery here, and could reach both invasion beaches. Knocking out the guns at Pointe-du-Hoc was assumed by Allied command to be key to a successful invasion.
Three companies of the 2nd Ranger Battalion were chosen to attack Pointe-du-Hoc. Led by Lt. Col. James Rudder, 225 Rangers were assigned the task of landing on the beach, climbing the cliffs, and taking out the guns.
A navigation error led the Rangers to land three miles away, delaying them and costing them the element of surprise. And "despite rigorous Allied aerial and naval bombardments, the [Germans] remained and desperately attempted to ward off the the attackers with grenades and small arms fire," reads a sign at Pointe-du-Hoc. Eventually, however, the Rangers, using specially designed climbing equipment, reached the top.
"Upon reaching the top they found only empty gun emplacements among the craters of the preinvasion bombardments. The force then gradually advanced inland where two Rangers spotted the well-camouflaged 155mm gun battery, now positioned south of the point and sitting mysteriously silent. With the enemy gun crews close by, the two men employed thermite grenades and destroyed the guns. At the end of the day, [Col.] Rudder sent a message to V Corps saying, 'Located Pointe-du-Hoc--mission accomplished--need ammunition and reinforcement--many casualties.'"
Indeed, of the original 225-man attack force, just 90 combat-effective men remained after the attack.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/27/4e976075-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a56391940ea2b6faa457f8740efccfd5/PDH-Beach_and_cliffs.jpg
This is a view of the beach below Pointe-du-Hoc and of the cliffs that climb to the point.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/27/4e8d2603-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/427030ddcf3eda4221a3efc2cced7edb/PDH-Bunker_gun_ports.jpg
This is a bunker on the edge of the cliff at Pointe-du-Hoc where machine guns were placed to cover the beaches below, and to protect the artillery pieces behind.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/27/4e86a376-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6a8c0192decd4cbecb458c554e1be710/PDH-View_from_gunport.jpg
This is the view from inside the machine-gun boxes at the edge of the cliff at Pointe-du-Hoc.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/27/4e7e85bd-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/131dfd0127e66c29cee45072123b5887/PDH-Craters.jpg
Here, we can see many of the craters left at the Pointe-du-Hoc site by the relentless Allied bombing in advance of the Rangers' attack and the general D-Day invasion.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/27/4e75d752-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f43ef7d22ddbda94f9ff8300a6dd039b/PDH-Entrance_to_bunker.jpg
This is the entrance to the German bunker at the edge of the cliffs at Pointe-du-Hoc.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/27/4e631de9-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/30e86f051c83e2caddb6ba0d2f51370b/PDH-Gun_mount.jpg
Here, we see a former German gun mount at Pointe-du-Hoc.
 
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4e5ce368-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e1e364b3502cb21eb6cd972ef9d6a81f/OBM-Pointe-du-hoc_ladder.jpg
This is one of the special ladders used by the Rangers to scale the cliffs at Pointe-du-Hoc.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4e55ddae-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2e41bcbe21305bb3e2550411bbc2c701/Cim-Cemetery.jpg
This is a view of part of the American Cemetery at Omaha Beach. Here, 9,387 American soldiers are buried.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4e4f45eb-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c91a832c8ad730a8b0761f5271ba1990/Cim-Memorial_front.jpg
This is the memorial site at the American Cemetery at Omaha Beach in Colleville-sur-Mer, in Normandy, France. The statue is called "Spirit of American Youth Rising from the Waves."
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4e4856b5-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1efa159ab96834c1fd547dc6eeeb499b/Cim-D-Day_soldier.jpg
This is the gravestone of Allan Mann, one of thousands of American soldiers who died on June 6, 1944, D-Day, on the beaches of Normandy.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4e426510-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dac5cfe9f5858ce72bd716da7441d790/Cim-Memorial_with_pool.jpg
Here, we see the entire memorial site at the American Cemetery, including a reflecting pool.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4e38be1e-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fc89cea6c439863f23057bd2856e015b/Cim-Unnamed_soldier.jpg
The American Cemetery at Omaha Beach contains the grave sites of many unknown soldiers. Their gravestones read, "Here rests in honored glory a comrade in arms known but to God."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/27/4e32a63f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d1d243538e198abf0835fa7b96b4cff5/OB-Omaha_Beach_long.jpg
Here, we see a view of the long beauty of Omaha Beach.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/27/4e2840ab-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/043915a4b0d7545ed4d3c45086dc6ae5/BRO-Big_Red_One_insignia.jpg
At the front of the assaulting force on D-Day was the 1st Infantry Division, known as the Big Red One. A small museum in Colleville-sur-Mer pays tribute to the crucial division.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4e2057b6-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0a548b47e27ba74a516c9d9792ae4248/OBM-Rommel_inspects_beach.jpg
This photograph, which is included in the archives of the Omaha Beach memorial museum, shows German Gen. Erwin Rommel inspecting the fortifications and defenses of Omaha Beach not long before the invasion.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4e180423-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3a01a4061a1477baa52c75a62d470216/OB-1st_ID_memorial.jpg
Just inland from Omaha Beach, this memorial pays tribute to the Big Red One, the 1st Infantry Division, which led the assault on D-Day at Omaha Beach.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4e0b8fe3-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/62463846da9728d1c6f3dcd16b4ee616/OB-Fifth_brigade_memorial_on_7-65_cannon_casemate.jpg
A memorial to the Fifth Engineer Special Brigade sits on top of a German 7.65mm cannon casemate at Omaha Beach.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4e01dbab-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/db4eea7fb0b301a3d23be2ff30c2d2b6/OB-Room_inside_Germany_quarters.jpg
This is a room inside one of the German quarters in bunkers at Omaha Beach. The hillside overlooking the beach was covered in batteries, bunkers, guns, and many kinds of fortifications and defenses.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/27/4df8b863-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f481f0288f3d0fd91c54d1c4f755e272/OB-Bridge.jpg
This is a restored bridge used at Omaha Beach to bring supplies and reinforcements in from ships. The bridges were protected by the artificial seawall, like the one used at Arromanches-sur-Mer. This is found at Omaha Beach.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4df20153-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/16e36062f48f26871e8793991afd953e/Sub-DD_tank_sunk.jpg
Close to the town of Port-en-Bessin-Huppain in Normandy, there is the Musee des Epaves Sous Marines. This is a fantastic collection of D-Day- and World War II-related military equipment recovered from underwater in and around the area in the years after the war.
This is a "DD" Tank that was sunk four miles from Omaha Beach. According to a sign at the museum, "On [the] morning of June 6...32 amphibious 'DD' tanks...are launched four miles from Omaha Beach. Twenty-seven of them sink, unable to fight against the heavy seas. Two reach the beaches due to the skill of their drivers."
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4deb10a8-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d54e6a781d3b9e61fe99edaf657f1eb4/Sub-DD_tank_sunk_propellers.jpg
This the rear of the DD tank, with its propellers still intact, but clearly showing the effects of the sea.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4de615f9-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a2b54730c3de542c82eed6a7d354315b/Sub-M17_priest_howitzer.jpg
This is an M7 Priest Howitzer from the 58th Armored Field Artillery Battalion, which was recovered three miles from Omaha Beach.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4ddec083-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b5568bbf7d123e5d6ff4380f6cf59dd7/Sub-Barnacled_gun.jpg
Here we see the barnacles and corrosion on a gun found submerged, and now on display at the Musee des Epaves Sous Marines.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4dd854a5-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2bc4221fea4ec7a6959d4d6a1253c01c/Sub-M17_electric_mine.jpg
This is a British M-17 electric mine, recovered from the seas off Normandy.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4dcbed7b-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/03e70c12ca43c21cdf27813d21580c61/Sub-M3_A4_Sherman_dozer.jpg
This is an M4-A3 "Sherman Dozer," which weighed 40 tons, and carried five men. It had a 75mm cannon, and a .30 caliber machine gun. It was also found submerged in the seas off Normandy, having been lost at some point during or after the invasion.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4dc5df6c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/171ec3ec337fbf55f41ca1473ad010e9/OBM-Landing_craft_with_Jeep.jpg
This is a model of a landing craft, at the Omaha Beach memorial museum. It could carry 36 men, or 3 tons of freight or vehicles, such as the Jeep seen here. It had two 7.62mm machine guns, and was powered by a gasoline or diesel engine.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4dbecfa5-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/142eba258640f9d860760875e796c738/OBM-German_flame-thrower.jpg
This is a German flamethrower, on display at the Omaha Beach memorial museum.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4db91a10-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/de5dedd6eea8f3121575fddeddb4063b/OBM-Doll_with_parachute.jpg
This is an example of a doll and parachute, which the Americans launched to try to confuse Germans grappling with invading paratroopers.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4dad740c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2afa2389a9d9b873c3345fdf6c58f6d9/OBM-German_machine_gun.jpg
This is a Mitrailleuse MG-42 German machine gun. It could fire 200 shots a minute up to 6 kilometers.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4da556c4-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/05329409ef606b8a5d438f5aa5b63a4a/OBM-105mm_cannon.jpg
This is an American 105mm cannon, on display at the Omaha Beach memorial museum.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/26/4d9cd71e-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d0980bf25b7414e38fe895c23cbf2500/OBM-German_sea_mine.jpg
This is a touch-sensitive German floating sea mine.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/27/4d9455e1-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/53f09d148e461a1d388679534b86efd5/BRO-Canteen.jpg
This is the canteen belonging to Pvt. First Class Allan Lawyer of the First Infantry Division, otherwise known as the Big Red One. The canteen has "Lawyer" inscribed on its bottom, and is on display at the Big Red One Assault museum in Colleville-sur-Mer.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-06-30    
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4fd90eda-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c22da968e66886efb31968db80b6074c/Tunnels_train_coming_out.jpg
Here, we see one of Eurotunnel's own locomotives emerging from the tunnel, a view that is not available to the public. 

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4fd36e38-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dde1359da567116c08a3e096bee16607/Going_into_Eurotunnel_from_cab.jpg
As part of Road Trip 2011, CNET got a ride in the cab of a passenger vehicle train heading through the Eurotunnel. Here, we see the entrance to the tunnel--actually, both tunnels, including the one that carries trains from north to south, and the other, through which trains go south to north, just before entering from the France side.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4fcee240-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5b09626d17cb4c81c2a334ee09f18cae/Train_driver.jpg
This is Eurotunnel train driver Tracy, smiling as she makes her way deep into the darkness of the 31-mile-long tunnel between France and England.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4fc9e460-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f5455e24715da6c6fb64a87dd6c26320/Train_Speed_indicator.jpg
Eurotunnel train drivers have their maximum speed dictated by operators in the railway control center, either on the UK side of the tunnel or the France side. Generally, non-Eurostar trains have a maximum speed of 140 kilometers--87 miles--an hour. Eurostar trains are allowed to travel up to 160 kilometers--99 miles--an hour. Here, we see that Tracy's train is going 138 kilometers--86 miles--an hour.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4fc2b519-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1ec2dcfc1fd71500dbe79ee3de2e0a0d/TCO-The_whole_thing_2.jpg
At the Eurotunnel's railway control center (RCC), operators can monitor every train that goes through either of the two tunnels that travel between England and France on the largest real-time optical control board in the world. Here, we see the entire board, known as the TCO, in the RCC on the UK side of the tunnel. There is a second RCC on the France side with an identical TCO.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4fbc645e-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a4ecb323cd3423e655971bbe045ef2cf/TCO_-_French_terminal_side.jpg
This is the optical real-time control board that allows operators in the Eurotunnel's railway control center to monitor all train traffic through the side-by-side tunnels between France and England. On this side of the board, we see the section that corresponds with the giant Calais, France-based terminal from which huge numbers of trains carrying everything from passenger cars to buses to cargo trucks depart each day. Each of the main green lines represents a track and the 4- or 5-digit numbers correspond to a specific train. If the number begins with a "7" it means it's a train carrying trucks and cargo. The all-digital board updates in real-time.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4fb69471-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/894db990224e54efc1157a8555221887/7365_1.jpg
In the middle left of this image, we see a yellow line with the number "7365." This corresponds to the length of a truck-carrying train that is heading from France to the UK.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4fab3d01-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/852d6f98be837acf824918304661352a/Space_and_time.jpg
This is a computer-based graph that shows the progress of any train in either of the two tunnels in space and time. The train's time of departure is on the X axis, and its place in the tunnel--designated by a code--is on the Y axis.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4fa2c915-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/352daa5f6027ad3fe285dad5db778c18/Tunnels_empty.jpg
Here we see the two tunnels--on the France side--with no trains passing through. Moments later, a truck train emerges having just completed the journey from England.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f9a05e4-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3981b9f6451f98d18c31a7e7e9fc80b8/Tunnels_trucks_coming_out.jpg
Having just emerged from the tunnel, this train is carrying trucks and other heavy equipment.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f921852-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/47258d4345309193e15849cfd3da7580/Tunnel_illustration.jpg
This illustration shows the configuration of the Eurotunnel--its two main tunnels, as well as its service tunnel, ventilation system, and cross passages.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f8be16a-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/39263ba335586aa56e25957e8ec7d05f/Photo_of_tunnel.jpg
This is a Eurotunnel photograph of the interior of one of the tunnels.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f8481cd-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/04e7da6f32d3e1d08ab02ef28cac43ce/Walking_into_service_tunnel.jpg
Here, we see two Eurotunnel employees, including Jean-Luc Pochet (left), the manager of the service tunnel infrastructure, walking into the service tunnel from the France side. The service tunnel allows maintenance and other official vehicles to drive through, as well as being the escape route passengers would enter if there was an emergency in either of the train tunnels. An overpressure system built into the infrastructure is designed to pull smoke out of the main tunnels, allowing passengers to escape a fire without the danger of smoke inhalation.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f7e32d2-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2d68d33f0719f94a6ba1bcd1091f3770/Service_tunnel_fire_door_closing.jpg
Here, we see one of the fire doors that are located throughout the service tunnel closing. The doors are designed to isolate a fire if one should break out, as well as to maintain the proper pressure in the tunnels.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f78368b-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/84336492be3371bb8d1abe4d4a426acb/Inside_service_tunnel.jpg
This is a view of the Eurotunnel service tunnel.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f70688b-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/cd8fdcecafe65e08d68572fe27dee99c/Locomotive_being_retrofitted.jpg
Eurotunnel maintains a fleet of 57 locomotives and more than 900 wagons. The company is in the process of upgrading many of the locomotives from 5.6 megawatts to 7 megawatts because of increased passenger and freight traffic through the tunnels between France and the UK. It is also being done in anticipation of further increases in traffic due to the London Olympics being held in 2012.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f67cfb3-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/cd59c493f9804c3b62335bd62439755e/Tunnel_shuttle.jpg
This is one of the shuttle vehicles that ferry people through the service tunnel. The vehicles drive along a magnetic line that keeps them on a straight line inside the tunnel. Otherwise, drivers would have trouble staying on a straight line.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f605cc7-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/72d4df2dd30b25f2295a842e6022b169/Locomotive_on_blocks.jpg
Part of the upgrading process for the locomotives involves replacing the complex equipment underneath the train, including air conditioning, ventilation, and more. These systems are entirely unique to Eurotunnel's trains, and are said by the company to be among the most sophisticated installed on any trains anywhere in the world. Here we see one of the locomotives lifted up for its retrofit.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f593037-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/95e2ad4764afe2dd80831acf63fa5b44/Fire_door_and_two_hose_fittings.jpg
This is a fire door in one of the passageways between the two main tunnels. On the lower left of the door, you can also see hose fittings for fire crews from both the UK and France. This is so the first crew that arrives, whichever country they come from, can attach their hoses--which are different in each country.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f4ea6d5-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2687a797f7f6aabaa53876966e7a57a1/Interior_passenger_carriage.jpg
This is a look into an empty Eurotunnel passenger car wagon. Each wagon can hold several cars, and during the ride between the Continent and the UK, passengers can get out of the cars and relax in comfort. The wagons can hold buses, RVs, cars, and other passenger vehicles, even those that are taller than normal cars.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f4895bb-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4264e7cfb1978942be2718eb447e0e7e/Bus_coming_off.jpg
Here we see a bus emerging from one of the passenger vehicle wagons on the UK side of the Chunnel.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f4141fc-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/db74ee4ffe3d7ddae580fb09ef5715db/Long_trains_in_shop.jpg
Eurotunnel maintains what it says is the largest train maintenance shop in Europe. While much of the work that goes on there is done on individual locomotives or wagons, there is a section of the shop that is long enough to hold entire trains, such as the two seen here.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f3c2d38-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5e92ff19833f34bc70fd25629ce76ff3/Many_axles.jpg
Just outside of the maintenance shop, we see many train axles lined up for various purposes.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f3505ca-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/50f92ac0646c5ac39fa989f0c637458c/Passenger_carriage_inside.jpg
Here, we see a close-up view of the interior of one of the passenger-car wagons.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f2fde1a-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d72aa3e118e718ce1105542376218e6c/Chassis_being_retrofitted.jpg
Here we see a train chassis that is being retrofitted. This is at the Eurotunnel maintenance shop in Calais, France.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f28211d-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1c776a530119f6b9e6b46394005ede91/Axle_being_lifted.jpg
Here we see a train axle being lifted by a crane and being carried to where it will be worked on or re-attached to a train wagon or locomotive.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f1eadbe-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f8e4e04c28a57ece48077e2eca65561b/Carriage_lifted_up.jpg
Here we see a Eurotunnel wagon lifted off the ground so that crews can work on the complex equipment located underneath.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f10e268-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ac9d68a900277dc2d2cc89a6e9f8e67e/New_wheel.jpg
This is a new wheel attached to a train axle at the Eurotunnel maintenance shop in Calais, France.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f07ad06-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bf6432b20ac988477e9bebe8a1a9cb74/Staight_up_shaft.jpg
This is one of two gigantic shafts at either end of the Eurotunnel that houses piping for ventilation, water for firefighting, and cooling. The equipment is located at the top of the shafts, which on the France side is about 3 kilometers north of the entrance to the tunnel. The shaft is 187 feet wide, and 131 feet tall, big enough, it is said, to fit the entire Arc de Triomphe.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/29/4f00287f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/15d45247d9c3d27801d9cf19b8e4bda4/Dog.jpg
This is an Airedale that is in the back of a car on a Eurotunnel passenger-car wagon. Passengers are able to carry pets back and forth between France and the UK, so long as they acquire a "passport" for their animal.







[Date]2011-07-01
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/50aff2cf-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/18e1fd9226f0a6045703b8ad312f69d5/ARS_door_DT.jpg
Today, it is still very much a working studio, and artists from all over the world come to record and mix there. CNET got a chance to visit and see some of the most impressive studio rooms and equipment in the industry. Including a microphone that both McCartney and Lennon often sung into.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/50aa8905-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fe7c9d03a9d2d758d9decde4041df2c0/Beatles_4-track.jpg
Throughout Abbey Road Studios, there is recording and mixing equipment everywhere. And since the studios have been used by everyone and anyone in the industry for nearly eight decades, you never know what you'll run into. In one hallway, placed unceremoniously against a wall, this four-track recorder sits in relative obscurity. Until you find out that it was used to record many of The Beatles' songs.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/50a79b90-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2fbb13eca496ca2b1e3acdcb877d1714/Beatles_mike_held_by_Lester.jpg
Lester Smith oversees Abbey Road Studios' collection of hundreds of microphones. Stacked on several sets of shelves are case after case of them, most of which are still used today, despite--or perhaps because of, their age. 
Here, Smith proudly displays a 1950s-era vocal microphone used frequently by The Beatles. "I can happily say that both John and Paul used this same mic," Smith said.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/50a117c5-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d93c7be429aa6dccc97f8b21c913af41/Zebra_crossing_DT.jpg
In the United States, we call this a crosswalk. In London, it's a zebra crossing. Either way, this one is perhaps the most famous in the world, made so by The Beatles on the cover of their "Abbey Road" album. Today, fans of the band from all over the world come to show their respects by posing for pictures in the crossing.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/509d260c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c66b3979f1bd3d2ae46b39629a8dbee1/Studio_2.jpg
This is the famed Studio 2, a large recording room considered a "mecca" by many in the music business because it's where The Beatles made the majority of their songs. All told, the band recorded more than 90 percent of their songs at Abbey Road Studios, with most of those being done in Studio 2.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/50971bb2-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/168a224064a40457f37680b240517488/Studio_2_from_floor.jpg
This is a view of Studio 2 taken from floor level.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/508fb685-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9d850297e89a9cc45f9dce4630f2d9b2/Lady_Madonna_piano.jpg
In a corner of Studio 2, this is the "Lady Madonna piano," which Paul McCartney played in The Beatles song of the same name. Recently, McCartney surprised people working in a nearby studio room by showing up and pounding out "Lady Madonna" on the piano, something that the sign on it clearly says is not supposed to be done.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/5089477d-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8c61caa2a7e5fe33495f7695332678fb/Ribbon_mike-RM1B_1930s.jpg
According to Lester Smith, this is an original ribbon microphone from the mid-1930s that has been a mainstay for Abbey Road Studios ever since. It is used on many film scoring sessions, as well as many orchestral recordings.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/507ee440-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/66ad684c4d8b3616f433c16182a2b70b/Studio_1_mixing_board.jpg
This is the mixing console for Abbey Road Studio's Studio 1. According to sound engineer Simon Rhodes, the studio works with the manufacturers of equipment like this to ensure that it gets the exact machines it needs. A console like this Neve 88RS costs about $800,000. Although digital is all the rage in music, these consoles are strictly analog due to the fact that for professionals like those at Abbey Road, digital is not yet up to the quality standards demanded by clients who pay a lot for the privilege of working there.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/5071cccc-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4adbfb448f35913162b3635173760e30/Abbey_Road_NW8.jpg
This is one of the most famous street signs in Europe.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/506a3850-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/182e961e43e54271672dd073e9240a1b/Studio_1_patch_bay.jpg
This is the patch bay in Studio 1. Usually, this would have so many wires plugged into it--bringing sounds in or out of the mixing board--that it would look like a "swirling mass of snakes," said Rhodes.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/5064ba7e-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8a21823969891eadba74e01521c35758/Studio_1.jpg
This is Studio 1, which has a 40-foot-high ceiling and is used extensively for the recording of film scores and orchestral pieces.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/505b9fa0-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fb33e43214b5cbbdad7f0c878a309a04/Studio_3.jpg
This is Abbey Road Studios' Studio 3, a longtime popular room. In here, Pink Floyd recorded "The Dark Side of the Moon," and more recently, Lady Gaga made "Born this Way."

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/50549e34-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f66a8b60809457876052e9e96415dcf1/Toy_cupboard_shelves_of_equipment.jpg
This is the "toy cupboard" at Abbey Road Studios, where hundreds of pieces of "outboard" equipment are stored and regularly shuffled in and out of recording sessions.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/504dbacd-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ac59313f63eb47b69467fcab605c9fdc/Very_early_mastering_EQ.jpg
This is a "very early" mastering EQ, according to Rhodes. "It's quite hysterical what's in this cupboard if you appreciate it," he said.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/5047e0e3-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c5c679e0dfa456aecd2a00f57d69cfa5/Penthouse_Neve_digital_desk.jpg
Abbey Road Studios maintains a mixing studio that is all-digital known as "The Penthouse." There, musicians working on many kinds of recordings, including the scores for large numbers of feature films use this Neve digital mixing console.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/50418b68-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/185409f64d1dc44b3921f27dcc4bbdbc/Studio_2_mixing_room_desk.jpg
This is a brand-new Neve 88 RS that is used to mix sound in Studio 2.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/503c5614-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/23389ad5bdf12d124cf1996862d4aee1/Fairchild_660_valve_compressor_DT.jpg
This is one of at least six Fairchild 600 valve compressors owned by Abbey Road Studios. Each one is likely worth at least $15,000 on the open market.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/5035df9a-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1f0972827c1c1af413d65c3b4ac49f94/TLM_50_and_M_50_mikes_Neumann.jpg
On the right is an M50, and 1950s-era microphone made by the Neumann company, and on the left is a later version of the same mike, a TL M50 that's about 20 years old. The newer model employs transistors to make sound as high quality as its predecessor, but while the newer model has great sound, Rhodes said it's not quite up to the same standards.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/502de01c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/561045af135bc945fa4158e240e003e3/Studio_2_screen.jpg
This is a large folding screen that has been in Studio 2 for decades. It can be moved around as required by each artist.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/5027c68d-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c944c32e76f87ba8cc4c1ca2bca62119/Studio_2_mixing_room.jpg
This is the mixing room for Studio 2 at Abbey Road Studios in London.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/502050ef-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b97da3b531d7628db6d6f04e3cefbdc5/Studio_1_piano.jpg
This is a piano in Studio 1, which is used to record many orchestral sessions.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/50196a8f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e98ed790438d12044320742aa380970d/Studio_1_cable_bunches.jpg
In a small cubby space behind the Studio 1 mixing room, there are endless numbers of cords and cables, mainly because the studio used analog recording equipment that requires a different cable for each of dozens and dozens of channels.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/50132223-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/73fe0d705f28f6ee16e6c542bf1ed330/Rooftop_echo_chamber.jpg
On the roof of Abbey Road Studios is a very small room behind a tiny door. Inside is this old echo chamber.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/5004f73f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/20a90ff7ab00e163f852cc04fd6b2721/Brain_of_the_Penthouse_desk.jpg
These are the computing "brains" behind the digital mixing console in the Abbey Road Studios "penthouse."

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/4fffe9a3-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ceea62505e9b7b1a297b84866ef085fc/Giles_Martin.jpg
This is Giles Martin, a producer who has recorded at Abbey Road Studios for years, is the son of legendary Beatles producer George Martin. Among his most famous projects is the collaboration between The Beatles and the Cirque du Soleil on the remixing of the famous band's songs for the soundtrack of the Las Vegas show "Love."

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/4ffa7319-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8fe7e5053be152510a95e0f955039910/Kid_writing_on_wall.jpg
Outside Abbey Road Studios is a stone gate, on which Beatles fans have written endless amounts of homages to the band.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/4ff2f311-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9409709ea74fd7bbd1b4787daa98cdbf/Magnetized_mikes.jpg
Lester Smith shows how the magnets in two microphones are strong enough to allow one mike to hang from another

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/4ff2f311-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9409709ea74fd7bbd1b4787daa98cdbf/Magnetized_mikes.jpg
Lester Smith shows how the magnets in two microphones are strong enough to allow one mike to hang from another

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/06/30/4fe51ed2-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3d7e018d81135940220e86e590128587/Plate_A.jpg
This is one of four reverb plates stored in a musty rooftop room above Abbey Road Studios. The plates are used to add reverb to recordings but are able to be kept permanently in the small room because wiring connects them to recording equipment below.






 
 
[Date]2011-07-04
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2012/03/20/7e50deef-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/cbd01391e563658589ff607ace253691/Colossus_rebuild_1.jpg
This is a rebuild of the famous Colossus Mark 2 machine (left) that finally allowed the code breakers to quickly and efficiently break the high command's ciphers. The original Colossus was so secret that after the war was over, it was broken apart and its components destroyed. But because there were 11 surviving pictures of the machine, Bletchley Park museum personnel have been able to build a fully working reconstruction, and visitors to the National Museum of Computing here can see the new Colossus for themselves. 

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2010/08/05/7e417cd8-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6a84899b7c8f954c8d2d743d0b1ffbf7/Enigma.jpg
This is an Enigma machine, from the collection of the National Cryptologic Museum in Fort Meade, Md.--the museum of the U.S. National Security Agency.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7e31eb6c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a98151eb6a4e3075d55d006570e33f6c/Colossus_body_close-up.jpg
To tackle the job of quickly analyzing intercepted German high command messages, leaders at Bletchley Park "called in the help of Tommy Flowers, a brilliant Post Office Electronics Engineer," the museum's Web site reads. "Flowers went on to design and build Colossus, a much faster and more reliable machine that used 1,500 thermionic valves (vacuum tubes). The first Colossus machine arrived at Bletchley in December 1943. This was the world's first practical electronic digital information processing machine--a forerunner of today's computers.
The Germans used a "high security teleprinter cypher machine to enable them to communicate by radio in complete secrecy," writes Bletchley Park scientist Tony Sale on his Codes and Ciphers Web site. It was called Lorenz, and this device "had to be cracked by carrying out complex statistical analyses on the intercepted messages," the Bletchley Park museum site reads. "Colossus could read paper tape at 5,000 characters per second and the paper tape in its wheels traveled at 30 miles per hour. This meant that the huge amount of mathematical work that needed to be done could be carried out in hours, rather than weeks."
This is a closeup of the Colossus rebuild at Bletchley Park today.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7e2b6b76-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/04814ba907dae9de9d5f339530a07ea8/Colossus_tubes-valves.jpg
This is a closeup of some of the 1,500 vacuum tubes used to do the work on Colossus. The machine was built entirely from off-the-shelf Post Office machinery parts. 

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7e212148-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5f5646f33cb69107b681108e78c64d4c/Colossus_behind.jpg
A look at the rear side of Colossus. The rebuild at the National Museum of Computing at Bletchley Park is a fully functional machine that can decrypt Lorenz codes on demand.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2010/08/05/7e19b914-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f5050f763d1ac6df362145b4154979f4/Operational_rotors.jpg
These are the operational rotors of Enigma. According to the National Cyptologic Museum in Fort Meade, Md., "The German military issued extra rotors with each machine--two for Army and Air Force machines and four for Navy. Each rotor was wired differently and identified with a Roman numeral. Setting up a communications net involved selecting the rotors for the day and placing them in the machine in the proper left-to-right order."

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7e120a6c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/221a70978c1af058c5f927ca09a6f487/Colossus_ticker_tape_reader.jpg
This is a device attached to Colossus that constantly read characters back into the machine--because Colossus had no on-board storage. Instead, the tape that was produced by teams at other machines was continuously read back into Colossus until it had the entire message.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7e06bb15-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b6fb5aaca8cce64f24f2a391404e8bd6/Photo_original_Colossus.jpg
The rebuild of Colossus was possible because although the original machine was nearly destroyed, there were at least 11 surviving photographs of it with which to base the reconstruction project on.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7dfcbf02-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4435293430e5d5b8238fd15dbad8f2b9/Early_intercept_machine.jpg
This is an earlier version of an intercept machine, which captured the Germans' communications and put out a ticker tape that was then read and its contents input into a second machine, called a "Tunny," for deciphering.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7df31ce1-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/14ced8b927f26d705b86c73b56cb1c7f/HR_Undulator.jpg
This device converted the coded messages captured by the intercept machine into a ticker tape that was then read and its contents typed into a Tunny machine.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7ded8121-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/11aa8ac4f0d9d833f1951b30ed020f35/HR_Tunny_machines.jpg
At Bletchley Park during World War II, groups of women would take the ticker tape containing the coded German messages and type the codes into these Tunny machines. The Tunnys would then convert the coded message into a new ticker tape that would then be put through a bombe machine and analyzed.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7de50534-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/46df026a39c0bf772a229931597892da/HR_ticker_tape_reader.jpg
This is part of the Heath Robinson, a successor to the Colossus. It was not successful because it tended to shred the ticker tape as it fed it through its systems. It was called Heath Robinson because it was considered a crazy invention, something that was regularly a part of a 1940s-era cartoon by that name.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7ddb86db-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c769b6583418d1b091fa999609615852/Turing_office.jpg
This is a re-creation of Alan Turing's office in Hut 8 at Bletchley Park. From this office, Turing directed efforts to break the German high command's codes and in the process may have helped shave two years off World War II.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7dd32a79-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1260c2c8368157b770fa6aa59ec5fb26/HR_Pin_board.jpg
This is a pin board that's part of the Heath Robinson. 
Though its many "huts" and "blocks" look rundown and somewhat decrepit today, Bletchley Park was in its day the world's first purpose-built computing center, built specifically to break the Germans' codes and help the Allies defeat the Germans and win World War II.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7dcbf0a4-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/882d24414ab3a6ca33b26516de03549f/HR_test_run_code_decypher.jpg
This is a test run of the deciphering capabilities of the reconstructed and fully working machines at the National Museum of Computing at Bletchley Park.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7dc49247-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/145297c272147df4ade2a287c653b497/Code_system.jpg
This is the key that the women working the Tunny machines would use to input the Lorenz codes after they'd come off the undulators at the intercept machines.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2010/08/05/7db0fdb7-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1b9668803b0af7e28a9f98f64f2befe8/Tunny.jpg
This is a German tunny machine, displayed at the National Cryptologic Museum in Fort Meade, Md. A cryptographic typewriter, the tunny offered encryption and decryption, meaning that an operator could type in plain text and get encoded text out. They were built to handle large amounts of text at high speeds. An early version of these machines was called "Swordfish," and, learning that, the Americans and the British began to give fish nicknames to various versions of the machine. 
It is a Schlusselzusatz 40 (SZ40), made by the German company Lorenz and was used by the German Army for high-level communications. "It provided on-line encryption and decryption of messages and was capable of handling large volumes of traffic at high speed," according to the National Cryptologic Museum. "The Tunny depended on wheels for its encryption/decryption, but unlike Enigma, it did not substitute letters, but instead encrypted elements of the electrically generated 'Baudot Code' used in normal telegraphic transmissions."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2010/08/05/7da5be8a-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fe168e1eda8653ae1d2d804d592c1beb/Cryptanalytic_Bombe.jpg
This is a U.S. Navy cryptanalytic bombe. Bletchley Park analysts worked on British-built bombes.
The machines "worked primarily against the German Navy's four-rotor Enigmas. Without the proper settings, the encrypted messages were virtually unbreakable," according to the National Cryptologic Museum. "The bombes took only 20 minutes to complete a run, testing each of the 456,976 possible rotor settings within one wheel order. Different bombes tried different wheel orders, and one of them would have had the final correct settings. When the various U-boat settings were found for the day, the bombe could be switched over to work on the German Army and Air Force three-rotor messages."

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7d9bedaf-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/649c28f0f3b5bcd760ace34b9de4ea88/Tony_Sale-George.jpg
This is George the robot, built by Tony Sale, a vintage computer specialist who works at and is the original curator or the Bletchley Park Museum and who maintains the Web site Codes and Ciphers. 
Sale built George in 1950, and it was the world's first working, walking humanoid, according to Sale. At the time, Sale and George were filmed for British TV, and more recently, when the creators of the "Wallace and Gromit" movies saw the broadcast, they asked for--and received--permission to include George in one of their films.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7d8d4b0b-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b213d0df8ceb82c2ec7ac9df1a55de71/The_millionaire.jpg
Built in 1895, this is the "Millionaire," made by Switzerland's H.W. Egll. It was the first calculating machine in history that was capable of doing multiplication instead of just doing repeated addition. Machines like this were common in the insurance industry until the 1930s, according to the museum.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7d842674-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b38a5f4074c56b3e82e49b47b23a4900/TR-48.jpg
This is the TR-48, an early analog computer. To solve a problem, the machine creates "a mathematical model of a problem," according to the museum, "then [plug in] an electronic circuit which simulates the system represented by the mathematical model.
"This is achieved by techniques such as summing, integrating, and applying other functions to create gradually changing voltages within the machine."

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7d7b8086-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bee4c6ef859453f77407c0fc15e5d081/PDP11.jpg
This is a PDP 11/34, built in 1976 by Digital Equipment Corporation. It was a 16-bit minicomputer that ran on a single bus system called Unibus.
"The usage of a single bus was quite a departure in terms of computer technology," according to the museum, "as many minicomputers at that time had a separate dedicated I/O bus. The designers at DEC could do without this separate bus by mapping all of the I/O requirements to addresses in memory so that no specialized I/O instructions were needed.
The PDP-11 could use several different kinds of peripherals, such as a disk drive that had as much as 2.5 megabytes of storage space. As well, it had dual 8-inch disk drives that could each store 256 kilobytes of information. It also had a tape drive. This version of the computer "is running the RT-11 single-user real-time operating system," according to the museum.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/03/7d5839d3-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d33d2e23bd3117a713ead3b0f59f3599/Air_Traffic_control_radar.jpg
This is an air traffic control radar station, from 1978, that was once used at London's ATC center near the famous Heathrow airport.
"Along with 30 similar stations, they were manned by the controllers responsible for flights in and out of London Heathrow--the busiest international airport in the world," according to the museum.
"Two PDP11 computers control each desk...One is responsible for processing radar data and the other for controlling the display."
Deployed in the 1970s, it was amazingly only retired in 2008.



 
 
[Date]2011-07-11  Venece Flood
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/5166cb7b-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/96c307cef9c7c7bbdf985190fa4262a1/Flood_suitcase.jpg
Venice Flood Control Project
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/51608f66-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c3e07952a89ac538cc6ba5c62621a572/Venice_inlets.jpg
This image shows the Venice Lagoon and the three inlets that allow shipping traffic in and out, but through which storm surges have regularly flooded Venice. The gate systems of the Mose project will be installed at the three inlets.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/51577bbb-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0a4ba8315522212f54d197abf7203f5d/Inlet_diagram.jpg
This diagram explains the new infrastructure that is being installed at each of the three inlets of the Venice Lagoon. A total of 78 large gates will be installed in four rows, hopefully by 2014, at the three inlets--two sets totaling 41 gates at the Lido inlet, 19 at the Malamocco inlet, and 18 at the Chioggia inlet.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/5152aa3b-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/334c7c02cceb96024f14c9d15e4a9c8d/Gate_diagram.jpg
This illustration explains the five major elements of each of the gates. When needed, the gates will rise between 40 and 42 degrees from the hinge seen in this image in order to protect the interior of the lagoon from the seas. The gates will be opened only in groups of threes in order to keep too much water from flooding through the other inlets.
In this illustration, the Adriatic Sea is on the right side, and the Venice Lagoon on the left. The gates are intended to maintain a lower water level in the lagoon than in the Adriatic.
The thickness of the gates will vary from inlet to inlet, from 11.8 feet to 16.4 feet. The lengths will also vary depending on the depth of the canals where they are located, from 60.7 feet to 95.8 feet. But all the gates will be 65.6 feet wide.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/514e8ed8-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/de20b7d3bd61383ce411fc1485917f81/Gate_in_action.jpg
In this illustration, we can see one of the gates as it will look when it is in use. Today, the Consorzio Venezia Nuovo's construction contractors are working on building the many caissons that will house the gates, and the gates themselves will be built prior to the planned 2014 debut of the Mose project. When needed, the gates should be able to be opened in about 45 minutes, and closed in about the same amount of time.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/5148c9db-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b63d61a434ebf700b0f09988914048b6/Gate_function.jpg
This illustration explains the basics of how the gates are intended to work. The gates, which are hollow, will rise up at their hinges from the caissons on which they are installed under water. According to a document from the Consorzio Venezia Nuova, "When a high tide is forecast, compressed air is pumped into the gates to empty them of water, causing them to rise above the surface and create a continuous barrier dividing the sea and the lagoon for the time necessary. Small craft harbors connected to locks allow vessels to enter the lagoon when the barriers are raised--small locks for pleasure and fishing boats at Lido and Chioggia and at Malamocco, a lock for large ships heading for the port."
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/51435904-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/17b7976788d9b6560a13ab044c97bf24/1st_site_one_in_place_one_building.jpg
While the caissons--otherwise known as basements--that will house most of the gates are being constructed away from where they will be installed, that's not the case at the Lido inlet. There, because there is enough space to build them, they will be constructed in place.
In order to do that, the area around the caissons was drained of water, a stone system placed underneath to protect the bed of the caisson, and then the whole thing reinforced with a system of piles to avoid the collapse of the bed. Then, at Lido, the caissons were built on top of this system.
Here we see one of the Lido caissons under construction. It will hold three gates, each of which will eventually be installed on top of it and connected with special hinges.
The plan is that in the spring of 2012, all the water will be put back into this section, and this caisson and those around it will all be back underwater.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/513d4b5a-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2023f6a6ac128fc68566affd1daaa5c3/2nd_site_long_rows_of_building_caissons.jpg
Just alongside the Malamocco inlet, a huge construction site has been set up for the building of 18 of the caissons. They will be made at the site, and then launched into the water via a special system called the Syncrolift. The caissons will then be towed into place nearby.
All 18 of the Malamocco caissons are being constructed at the same time, although according to a very specific plan, and not at the same pace. Here we see a row of caissons that are under construction.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/5135adfa-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/07d98019f418a694a34705e963b92799/1st_site_line_of_caissons.jpg
This is at the Lido site, and we see a curving row of installed caissons, each of which will house three gates.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/512a6de3-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3b2196e966f83b9d81806c7581a290bf/MALAMOCCO350.JPG
This is an aerial view of the caisson construction going on at the Malamocco site. What this picture shows is that the caissons, while constructed of steel and concrete, have large hollow spaces in them that will allow them to be buoyant when launched into the water before being sunk into place at the nearby Malamocco inlet.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/51238c0c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a495e1563300edb30d51b125a2fab727/Line_of_caissons.jpg
This is an aerial view of the construction and placement of the caissons at the Lido inlet.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/511add13-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/81b53834c4f8422b6a8462609f7a2ea8/MALAMOCCO029.JPG
This is an aerial view of the Malamocco inlet (in the foreground) and the Malamocco caisson construction site (on the left). The caissons are being built there and will be launched from the Syncrolift and towed into place in the inlet. Later, the gates will be installed atop the caissons.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/51118704-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fa4440cfffb7eb912bd7e552f0d812bf/Flood_Venice.jpg
In this archival photograph, we see Venice underneath flood waters. The city is being flooded up to five times each year, and the Mose project is intended to protect the city from the rising tides that threaten the city's well-being.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/510b7bea-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/51e1ac95424a33f3d1cdf5f612812b16/2nd_site_trolleys_in_a_long_row.jpg
Each of the caissons will be launched into the water via the Syncrolift. In order to get to the Syncrolift, however, they must first be rolled into place on specially designed tracks. And they will be placed on top of 84 large trolleys. Here we see a long row of the trolleys, each of which has its own hydraulic piston jack that is used to support some of the massive amount of weight of the caisson.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/510687f9-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5bea7e1e41fd4f034c6b9e5fdc1134e1/2nd_site_trolley_close_up.jpg
This is a close-up of one of the 84 trolleys that will be used to roll each of the caissons into place at the Syncrolift.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/5100ba01-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/403bd81df6262788d0dcdd28628f5b8b/2nd_site_tracks.jpg
These are the tracks on which the caissons will be rolled into place at the Syncrolift. The tolerances for the construction of the tracks was minuscule--a fraction of a millimeter--because there was almost no room for error in ensuring that the caissons are moved totally straight toward the Syncrolift.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/50fbe1c0-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/13304e4b9dc802fdb3746da20a9a873e/2nd_site_trolleys_in_front.jpg
Here, we see a trolley for each of the tracks underneath one of the caissons. There is an extremely delicate plan in place for moving each one of the caissons into position to be rolled onto the Syncrolift. It involves sliding them forward and to the side from their construction places. When moving to the side, each trolley will lower its jack, rotate 90 degrees, and then raise its jack, one trolley at a time. This is expected to be a very slow, laborious process.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/50f500c5-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/11739aff8d14c68151cf598d0bb01fc0/Flood_Piazza_San_Marco.jpg
In this archival photograph, we see the world-famous Piazza San Marco under water.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/50e8e193-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8735366e067e59922058d3073db4eea8/MALAMOCCO286.JPG
This is an aerial view of the Syncrolift. You can see how it is at the end of the artificial pier on which the caissons are being constructed. There is one row of caissons that is lined up directly with the Syncrolift. But two rows are to the side, and the caissons being made there will have to be moved to the side using the process described in the previous caption.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/50dfc681-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/cb9d097b5cf6f3a5c6bc5d98682fa172/1st_site_Caisson_with_tunnels.jpg
Each caisson is being built with two tunnels inside that will house all kinds of electric and mechanical systems, and which will be waterproofed so that people can operate inside of them.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/50d93b3f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9f75ce6ea5bbed259e03dd572b46f034/Female_and_male_connectors.jpg
These are a matching pair of the connectors that will join the gates with the caissons. Three of these connectors will be used for each gate.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/50cfa0e5-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/71c6ee9022a5cd28fe1aa3896fb1a97b/2nd_site_line_of_winches.jpg
Two long rows of special Rolls-Royce winches are installed alongside the Syncrolift in order to lower the caissons onto the lift, from which they will be launched into the sea.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/10/50c6b569-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e845b1c76056e9bcad6e18575466d3b0/2nd_site_pillars_in_place_for_next_caisson.jpg
These are the many pillars that are in place for the construction of a future caisson.
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-07-12  Swiss knife
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/529b9032-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e99fce56753a2f7cb05775201f8a89af/Tweezers.jpg
Here, we see a bin of the tweezers that go in just about every one of those models.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/5294f11a-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/19dba172d4aa636c35341a773538ee24/Original.jpg
This is one of the original models of Swiss Army Knife, from 1891. Made by the company's founder, Karl Elsener, it was a commission to the Swiss government to make knives for every one of the country's soldiers. Though it didn't have the now-famous logo, and Elsener's company wasn't yet called Victorinox--that would come years later, in honor of his mother, Victoria, and because of the French word "inox," which means stainless steel--the knife is recognizable as the predecessor of what nearly everyone has today. The Swiss government still purchases a knife for all its soldiers from the company, and there have been just eight different models since 1891 for the Swiss military, with the most recent being released in 2008.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/528c1d5f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/358f60ae1a78b2912a6d36e657b7d6b5/Elsener.jpg
At the base of the main blade of this original soldier's Swiss Army Knife, you can see that prior to being named Victorinox, Karl Elsener's company took his family name.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/52818fcb-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4b05c92f804f9d84e67da40233d36aab/Officer_knife_1931.jpg
Elsener wanted to make an official Swiss Army Knife for officers as well, and began producing them. But the Swiss government decided that officers would still have the soldier's knife they got when they joined the Army and decided not to give Elsener the contract. Still, the company produced officer's knives for years, and this is one from 1931.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/5277e070-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/38a284c7c8586190177629b2d0a2927d/Box_of_Handyman.jpg
At the Victorinox factory in Ibach, a box of finished Handyman model Swiss Army Knives await being moved off the floor.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/52727b9c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ee3df2e142af25d18cc7585855f0b4c5/Rake_for_pulling_out_blades.jpg
As blades for various models of Victorinox knives come off a stamping machine, they can be pulled from a bin using this rake so that workers don't cut their hands.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/526a15d0-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/237109f27c466aec5c40bede36af76ba/Wheel_feeding_aluminum_for_dividers.jpg
This wheel feeds strips of aluminum into a stamping machine in order to make the dividers that go between the different tools in a Swiss Army Knife. The dividers are aluminum in order to save weight, but the tools are generally made from stainless steel.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/525bebc3-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bffe443fc0d0474eceb1ae8772648b9f/Five_knives.jpg
With these five knives, you can see the long history of Swiss Army Knives played out. They include the original official soldier's knife from 1891 in the lower right; the latest model, from 2008, in the upper right; a previous model, from 1968, in the upper left; an officer's knife, from 1931, in the upper right; and a more recent version of one of the standard, red knives in the center.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/52547590-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/74640578cfc913c1952e6ab26ce24189/Keyrings.jpg
In the stock room, there are dozens of bins of parts for Swiss Army Knives. This is a bin of keyrings.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/5248b1b7-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3464af813c58827b7c363a67fb190748/RS5_at_Victorinox.jpg
CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman parked the Audi RS5 he is driving on across the street from the Victorinox factory in Ibach, Switzerland, with two of the region's mountains in the background.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/52423ed7-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9c8720102cfd5bc8da2819ae457325f7/Feeding_sheets_into_stamping_machine.jpg
A worker at the Victorinox factory feeds sheets of stainless steel into a stamping machine in order to make knife blades.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/523daa1f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dfb5c554cb6733fd8f6a9ee50ca36abb/Small_blades_into_the_fire.jpg
Here, a worker puts spindles of small standard Swiss Army Knife blades on a conveyor in order to put them through a furnace that will subject them to temperatures of 1,050 degrees Celsius in order to harden the stainless steel, making it possible for the blades to last for decades rather than begin to fall apart nearly immediately.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/5235f8bc-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9bd743099f433afe6b84e4186d4d5321/Shaking_out_leather_tool.jpg
After being stamped, many of these Swiss Army Knife leather tools still have small pieces of metal that haven't fallen out. So the tools are put on a machine that shakes them violently, a process that ejects the small pieces.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/522d61ff-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7df2fd1b48e9cedbce6b036372413e61/Smoothing_roughing_blades.jpg
After being stamped, the blades are still rough. So they are placed in these machines, along with artificial ceramic stones and some water, where they are polished for between five and eight hours, after which the tops of the blades come out smooth.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/5227ea51-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8a4a48c9c3f286fd0f83be3a0d444a24/Corkscrews_close-up.jpg
A look at a bin of Swiss Army Knife corkscrews.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/521eb38c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/579c5628ade2b0a726fcca6e2d308871/Box_of_toothpicks.jpg
Here we see a bin of Swiss Army Knife toothpicks.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/5217f0df-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/aca42e661f0307a4a6a6f9c41a88b7dc/Rows_of_half-scissors.jpg
These are long stacked rows of blades that will become Swiss Army Knife scissors.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/52123c94-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/502518d05d0b5a93f989bd317399fd52/Many_scissors.jpg
Here we see boxes that have long spindles of completed scissors. They will next be taken onto the assembly floor and added to Swiss Army Knives.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/520619fc-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8769196cf2f373015112f619b129478f/Long_rows_of_small_blades.jpg
Here we see many stacks of small Swiss Army Knife blades.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/51fe4416-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/94b3704b35ed9634c907453dd7fd5e8a/Many_carousels_of_handles.jpg
These are many stacks of carousels of Swiss Army Knife handles in several different colors, including the standard red and blue.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/51f78a12-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/765d7e3c39b9564309f2186521bbd3c1/Close-up_of_red_handle_carousels.jpg
Here we see a close-up of several carousels of red Swiss Army Knife handles.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/51ea55be-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1b4d8c2d9c9ed96e7acc4ec755c6b6a3/Bodies_waiting_for_handles.jpg
Here we see a box of Swiss Army Knives that are complete except for one thing: they haven't had their handles pressed onto them yet.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/51e570e7-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dca2d04805606b7f2cd8142f9847dcda/Handles_for_pressing.jpg
Here, we see a line of handles that are taken by workers and put, along with the body of a Swiss Army Knife, into a pressing machine. The handles are then pressed on to the body and out comes a completed knife--except for the tweezers, toothpick, and keyring.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/51dc0580-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6100be13d8970796a89fef39a110203b/Knife_making_station.jpg
Here, a Victorinox employee sits at her work station, making Swiss Army Knives by repeatedly and rapidly taking individual parts from the many boxes in front of her and putting them together.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/11/51d47f21-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8cd74aee806d944ddd1a94a02edc67f8/Making_a_Swiss_Army_Knife.jpg
