[Date]2011-07-13  Heinenkin Bear
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c56b8750-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/802942c8d948d9491166cf91d3f0a47e/Heineken_Brouwerij.jpg
This august building used to house the Heineken brewery--the main brewery is now located elsewhere--but the company has maintained it as a museum and homage to its 147 years of beer-making.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c5638d58-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f9ba11cb169f942c5fb7fed2782da6a9/Mr_Heineken.jpg
According to information provided by the Heineken Experience, "Gerard Adriaan Heineken founded Heineken &amp; Co. on [February 15, 1864] at the young age of 22. Thus began a proud family tradition of brewing premium-quality beer. Not only did Adriaan express a great deal of fortitude and entrepreneurship in founding the Heineken Brewery, he did it with an overwhelming devotion to provide the finest product possible."
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c55c8363-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9549439c4c0b9cf3d17fcbda8bc3b952/Bierbrouwerij_Maatschappij_poster.jpg
Throughout the old Heineken brewery, you can see old posters and signage for the brand.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c5557448-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7303bd091959ca35eaa5de05337d391d/Storage_silos.jpg
This is one of 15 large storage silos that are located in the old Heineken brewery and that are as much as 65 feet tall. "They were used to store malted barley for crafting and brewing Heineken," a sign about the silos reads. "Historically, the barley arrived by ship via the canal across the street and was transferred to the top of the building directly into these historic silos. The brewing process went on for 24 hours a day with up to six brews a day."
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c54d728e-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/508b75a65175316b3e580584cde5f340/Original_kettle.jpg
This is one of the four original Heineken brewery copper kettles. They were installed in 1913, and in 1956, Heineken added four additional kettles.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c547fc64-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ae2a427e9a0c2a26ba15b80a484d9fdc/Original_copper_kettles.jpg
These are the four additional copper kettles Heineken installed in 1956.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c53c37e7-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/49299807466bfb04e31a1e0e4fb51c9f/The_wort.jpg
Here we see an example of wort used in the brewing of Heineken beer. It is water and barley that is heated and filtered.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c533f680-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b0dad2a86a8122b6cad2125c695bf2a8/Fermentation_tank.jpg
This is a stainless steel fermentation tank that was installed in the Heineken brewery in the 1960s. "The new beer was transferred into these tanks along with Heineken's famous 'A' yeast," reads a sign about the tanks, "and allowed to ferment for up to three weeks before it was bottled."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c52d8ed6-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d89bca34c2ca653d4d07cba533a75f00/Late_19th_century_tankard.jpg
This is a ceramic tankard from the late 19th century, as seen at the Heineken Experience in Amsterdam.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c5269d84-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/80e3790f7bf3f8624bbc04eaf69d0fc3/Wagon.jpg
According to the Heineken Experience, "Horses have always been a significant part of Heineken.... From the founding of the Heineken Brewery in 1864 until the beginning of the 1960s, barrels of beer were delivered to local cafes and establishments in Amsterdam by horse and cart."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c51fb18f-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bf403ed127b74fd106143ee84b975e0b/Freddy_the_horse.jpg
This is Freddy, one of the horses that Heineken still keeps in a stable inside its old brewery.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c517a4ac-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/88750ce7ff573f353f15cd5b2a065478/Harness.jpg
This is a classic harness that was used with the horses that pulled carts full of Heineken around Amsterdam.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c5122c09-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/29cb9dcd50c599247bc515245dffc425/1939_bottle.jpg
This Heineken bottle was a new design for the Dutch market in 1939.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c509effe-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b18c24ace55115b06560c27f7672b7c1/Different_Heineken_bottles.jpg
These are four Heineken bottles that were introduced over the years. On the left is the first bottle used for export to the United States, in 1933. Second from left is the "Apollinaris" bottle, from 1948, which was the same kind used when Heineken first bottled its own beer, starting in 1931 (previously, bottling had been done by independent companies). Second from the right is the company's general export bottle, from 1970. And on the right is the first Heineken longneck bottle, from 1996.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c502ef57-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0cbb84f1796fbfc55dee88e38babad78/Early_1900s_price_list.jpg
This is a price list from the Die Port van Cleve Hotel in Amsterdam from the early 1900s, showing a list of Heineken beers.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c4f7e27c-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e4d225718aeae204528a72eff1bacf2f/WOBO_Bottles.jpg
According to a sign at the Heineken Experience, "After seeing the poverty on the island of Curacao in 1960, Alfred Heineken came up with a solution for sustainable housing. He invited acclaimed Dutch architect John Habraken to design the World Bottle, or 'Wobo,' as it soon became known. Freddy [Heineken] referred to it as a 'brick that holds beer.' The empty Wobo could be used to build affordable houses.
"The Heineken Wobo went through several design processes with one aim in mind--to design a bottle that was interlocking and self-aligning. A final design was achieved and two sizes--350 and 500mm--were manufactured. The bottle, however, never went beyond a prototype."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c4f1dd99-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bd8a4af47f357b8e3db75b464d2fb695/Logos.jpg
These are several Heineken logos from the company's 147-year-long history.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c4ea1307-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bba28bd15422b33b4df52b9d36a644be/The_red_star.jpg
According to Heineken, the red star featured on all the company's beer labels can be traced back to the 14th century "and is one of the most ancient and mysterious symbols in the history of beer making. It is said that medieval brewers hung the enduring symbol of the star over their vats to protect the brew and guarantee its quality. The protective powers of the five-point star symbolized earth, fire, wind, and water, with the fifth star point representing the unknown element that the brewers believed was 'magic.'"
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c4df41e2-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3b9c87975239c2ec04a40fcb8a17c140/Bottle_wash.jpg
Although Heineken is no longer brewed in the old Heineken brewery that now houses the Heineken Experience, there is a scaled down bottling system that is used to demonstrate the process.
This is the first step, the bottle wash.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c4d8a9fb-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7d7f59a5257d2661a582813564e2eb11/Bottle_test_and_bottle_filling.jpg
Next comes a bottle test, and then the filling of the bottles.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c4cbef4b-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0932f7c9547a97dff332b8fba275a562/Bottle_crowning.jpg
After that, the caps are added, or the bottles are "crowned."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c4c4c4e9-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/12e0e8d318a3011f9a99823c301e9334/Label_application.jpg
Next, the Heineken labels are added.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c4bdf340-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/17cc06d547e5000a6b923a430945d432/Final_inspection.jpg
Finally, the bottles are inspected, and then sent off for packaging.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c4b5d377-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/acec9c65c61eb636ffef37d8ca99bc4b/Paco_and_Icone_bottles.jpg
Heineken has been making beer since 1864, and has used a lot of different kinds of bottles. These are two of the most unusual, and newest: The Paco, from 2004, which was "designed for a fun night out on the town...an award-winning, minimalist, and sleek molded aluminum design," and the Icone, from 2006, made from aluminum materials and designed by France's Ora-Ito.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/12/c4ae6e07-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e9529d7742c497d1ed4ebd369a7ee680/Heineken_experience.jpg
This is Heineken Experience signage mounted over the door to the old Heineken brewery in Amsterdam.
 
 
[Date]2011-07-15
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/53b4777f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3fff9ac8be3aa670ddcdaf9b97551998/Nose_section.jpg
This is the nose section of an A380 being built in what is known as Station 40, at the facility in Blagnac.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/53ad1cbb-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dc4f6a938c6bda4799ee6c4bb9435740/On_tarmac.jpg
Two A380s that have been fully assembled sit on the tarmac outside the final assembly factory in Blagnac, France. The planes have not yet had their carrier livery painted on--that will be done after they are flown to Hamburg, Germany, where the customers will pick up their new planes. The tails have been modified in this photograph so as not to reveal the planes' customers.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/53a3db03-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ebe7c98a7350b55727a779af962554d7/Nose_and_both_wings_wide.jpg
Seen from across the giant Airbus assembly facility near Toulouse, France, an A380 is being constructed at what is known as Station 40. The plane's major components--its fuselage, wings, tail, tail fins, and more--are joined together at one large station. It's a different system than the one used at rival Boeing, where planes move down a line to have their major components added.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/539e28b4-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3f9e03bea75e7eb55818d3891449c9a6/Wings_and_cranes.jpg
In the foreground of this photograph is an A380 wing resting just above the ground. In the background, we see another wing elevated. The one on the ground is being stored here for the next A380, while the one in the background is already part of one of the giant planes that is currently being built.
Above, on the ceiling, are two large cranes that are used to hoist major plane components, like these wings, into place.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/53946cd8-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/93aa26311d84b98770eed6d021cde1b0/Underneath_left_wing_assembly.jpg
Here, a view up at the left side wing of an A380 that is being assembled at Station 40.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/538cdcf8-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/06773747e3cea1c5aa5924b9077e48ac/Where_wing_joins.jpg
This is the end of an A380 wing that's joined with the plane's fuselage.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/5387d73c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dd8ad63b367d34766f93972588d3feef/Nose_section_and_wheels.jpg
In the background, we see the front section of an A380 that's currently being assembled. In the foreground, we see the two tires that will be part of the plane's front landing gear.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/537e9bec-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8586c23d1f5f277877be0f5a1d07908f/Landing_gear.jpg
This is the main part of an A380's front landing gear. The components are waiting to be added to a future A380.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/5377de80-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/131e7d3b1c039dd86a9748e2ecf91bb3/Landing_gear_wheels.jpg
These two wheels will be part of the front landing gear of an Airbus A380.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/5370649c-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b6eba1e7d8e960799e388b164a50e9c2/Working_inside_wing.jpg
An Airbus employee works on the systems inside one of the plane's massive wings.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/536a5c56-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/80c752d684ce4cf282f47c636fea6d6e/Air_Conditioning_in_wing.jpg
The A380's wings are big enough that they can hold the plane's air conditioning packs. That, says Airbus, is an advantage over most other large planes because the packs don't have to be stored in the A380's belly fairing. That, in turn, means the plane's fuselage is more streamlined than it would otherwise be, reducing its drag.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/53628520-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1706422bd950dec171bce2f34d53f358/Working_on_wing.jpg
An Airbus employee works on one of the A380's wings. You can see some of the air conditioning systems installed just on the inside of the hollow space of the wing.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/53576077-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0d62d45a626a6ffc82c19936d59d76c5/Nose_section_and_left_wing.jpg
Here we see the A380's nose section and its left wing inside the jig, where it is currently being assembled.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/5351e437-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c1debbdc164c98cd6c47c62a459f1ccd/Landing_gear_door_open.jpg
In this photograph, we can see the opening for the A380's front landing gear, just under its nose.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/534c41b4-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3fef40451e784b297ef8912c386a9693/Left_wing_assembly.jpg
This left wing of an A380 has already been joined with the fuselage of one of the giant airplanes.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/53450a88-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/946713e6fa1972c7f449be1ed76d3117/Left_tail_fin_assembly.jpg
The left tail fin of an A380 that's under assembly at Station 40.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/533b3144-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0bdc3d6f1ed321b1ef4875c2f756b6e9/Left_wing_leading_edge.jpg
An A380 wing awaits the arrival of the next group of major components for the next of the massive airplanes. Airbus always has a second set of wings lying around for future construction.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/532feeb6-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8178718195ae26b61d6dd7fe8fcea2d4/Left_wing_tip.jpg
This is the tip of a left-side wing that's awaiting the arrival of the components for the next A380 assembly.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/53276620-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fdde585ea9f0ea6de35af670eb9685d3/Wide_shot_of_A380_at_station_30.jpg
After the major components of an A380 are joined together at Station 40, the plane is backed out of the assembly building and brought back in again at what is called Station 30. There, the plane has all its interior systems installed, as well as its engines added. Here, we see an A380 that is at Station 30.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/53208ed4-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5c13bee23d8effe71280a95e16fa1335/Left_engine_mount.jpg
This is the pylon for a left-side wing A380 engine. It is the only major A380 component that is actually made in Toulouse, France. While the plane is assembled there--actually, in nearby Blagnac--most of its components are built in Airbus facilities in England, Germany, and Spain.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/531a5d27-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1659319e76c7f2136484c6d54749e8fc/Tracks_in_floor.jpg
These tracks in the floor allow the rear side of the jig to move as the assembly process requires. The front section of the jig--from the nose until the wings--is stationary, while the rear section can move on these tracks.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/5314d562-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ab4759d547ed963e9e77175d190f13e7/Tail_section_straight_on.jpg
An A380 tail section waits to be joined with the fuselage of one of the giant airplanes.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/530e93d6-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8880539a4e1baed8b45d118b50b55a26/Door_open.jpg
One of the main A380 doors opens to the interior of the fuselage of one of the planes that is being assembled.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/5308602d-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/87d6227ce13708266f2bd8885091fd7b/Side_of_plane_at_station_30.jpg
This A380, seen from the right side, is at Station 30, meaning it has already had all its exterior components added, and crews are now installing the plane's interior systems--hydraulics, avionics, wiring, doors, and cockpit.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/53023b80-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f2d76b3eddd6b7011c97e67d582ccaae/Right_engine_station_30.jpg
One of the two engines on the right wing of an A380 is being worked on at Station 30.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/52f8e3d7-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b47c6194e69ea286ed58694bce8b6ce3/Plane_off_ground_wide.jpg
Each A380 at Station 30 is hoisted about 1.5 meters off the ground to have its landing gear installed. Once the landing gear is joined with the plane, it can be lowered down to rest on its own weight. Here, we see one of the planes still hoisted off the ground.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/52eb9881-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/daccfaa7aaf8c122de8256fd7351ae34/Left_front_of_A380_station_30.jpg
Here, we see the left front side of an A380 at Station 30.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/52e4d056-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8a6d3e58e501457d7000d86fed07903b/Landing_gear_off_ground.jpg
A close-up of the landing gear of an A380 that is hoisted off the ground. Soon, it will be lowered down so it can hold its own weight on the landing gear.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/52d3ac22-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/81472c35f3d47ead0d7221213a7fcb14/Airbus_A380.jpg
This is a former A380 test plane that has been sold to a private customer. Here, the plane sits at Station 30, where it's having its interior systems installed.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/52cadb3f-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3543731105fb8a1d6a0c414e143c8372/Engine_open.jpg
A380 customers can choose from two kinds of engines--the Engine Alliance GP7200--from a joint venture of General Electric and Pratt &amp; Whitney--or the Rolls-Royce Trent 900.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/52c3a564-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/73a8d21d8a811360f28d29ebd426017d/Landing_gear_close-up.jpg
This is a close-up of the front landing gear of the A380.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/52b81bed-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/27a0792bcdbfe4523f7d7283d460f075/Enging_on_wing_and_engine_on_floor.jpg
This is the front landing gear of an A380. All told, an A380 has 24 wheels--22 on its main landing gear, and these 2 on the front.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/52b054aa-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/78284ea0e32eadd67d4f4a54e88039b7/For_joining_engine.jpg
This is an engine pylon for the Airbus A380.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/14/52a72067-f0ed-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a328c7d07e01e72d8d1469e4844ab105/Engine_open_and_engine_on_wing.jpg
Here is an engine destined to be installed on a future A380, as well as one that is already mounted on the wing of one of the giant planes.

 
 
[Date]2011-07-18      
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/17/a7d7ccda-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/694e9db544c9257bd63332280c1cff0a/Airbus_A380_Boulangerie.jpg
And it makes for some funny pictures, too, such as here, where we see one of the three fuselage sections passing within feet of a sign for the local boulangerie, or bakery.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/17/a7d29e3b-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0f682acf65d7ded3b7d34e0530fdb0b2/Attention_Convoi.jpg
The convoy began passing through Levignac at about 11:15 p.m. local time, and for about an hour before that, police made sure no cars were parked anywhere on the town's main street. Electronic sign boards were put up alerting anyone who didn't already know that the A380 convoy would soon be coming through.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/17/a7cb3d2a-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9998b83d4adf4b0cbcc45163470af691/First_wing_section_across_intersection.jpg
The first piece of plane to pass through Levignac while I was there was one of the A380's wings. Actually, Airbus routinely ships the wings for the next A380 with the convoy, so the wing seen here will not be used in the assembly of the same airplane that's getting the other major components. Still, the wing was part of the parade, and in this photograph, you can see that it's long enough to stretch more than across an intersection.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/17/a7c5d204-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2e4cb304e2dbce64d206584eb5864b67/First_wing_section_arrives.jpg
The first wing of the convoy arrives in Levignac.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/17/a7bfcc49-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4c0cc559f93e9d12260e94c289d626b6/Streets_packed.jpg
The A380 convoy provides the people of Levignac the opportunity for a nighttime party. Before the components began passing through town, hundreds of people lined the streets, many with wine and other drinks in hand.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/17/a7b80c69-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6b67e3482b055feee38a7f944bf6e438/Wingtip--convoi_exceptionnel.jpg
The truck carrying this wing has a sign on it that says it all: "Convoi exceptionnel."

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/17/a7af9604-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fdc4a08c68efdd6de5de38f4332fcf43/Second_wing_section_coming.jpg
The second wing begins to pass through the center of Levignac.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/17/a7aa4120-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bbc11a7a75cb4e44dec1e9a304d7cbec/Wing_tip_second_wing.jpg
Here we see the tip of the second wing, just after it made its way through the center of the village.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/17/a7a57800-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d44fd213b5aa36a9a8e3b596104084f5/Tail_fins.jpg
In this picture, the gigantic tail fins of the A380--which together are about the same size as the wings of an Airbus A320--pass through the center of Levignac.

[Date]2011-07-19  tallest bridge
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a39ef668-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9faf467acfa1e67234549b84e613d938/Just_in_front_all_towers.jpg
When faced with a problem--a significant missing link on one of the major north-south highways out of Paris--the French came up with the perfect solution: build the tallest bridge in the world. Called the Millau Viaduct, because it spans the Tarn River just outside this small French town not too far from Clermont-Ferrand, this massive bridge towers 1,125 feet above the Tarn Valley--and by the way, cuts 37.3 miles off the drive from Paris to Perpignan in the south.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a3990bb9-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b3d1ddc1df891fa5eec5a3fc5a50aa5a/Just_in_front_tall_towers.jpg
For years before the Millau Viaduct opened for traffic in late 2004, teams were working on the idea of completing the missing link on the A75 autoroute that connects Paris in the north to Perpignan in the south. The first sketches were made in 1987, with each proposing a bridge that spanned the Tarn Valley, though some had the crossing east of Millau, while others had it to the west.
In 1994, the decision was made to locate the bridge just a few kilometers west of Millau. And in 1996, the bridge design by Michel Virlogeux of Ponts et Chaussees and architecture by Norman Foster was selected. According to the official Millau Viaduct Web site, "A multi-stay cabled construction will be seen on the Aveyron skyline. Its aesthetic aspect and integration into the countryside attracted the government departments. It took preference over four other potential projects: a bridge of constant thickness, a variable thickness bridge, a viaduct with stays stretched under the deck, and a construction with one single arch."
In December 2001, the first stone for the Millau Viaduct was laid.
In this photograph, the bridge is seen from just in front and to the east.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/18/a394e27c-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9b8e6e2d0be88997863ec62339d982a8/Viaduct_under_construction.png
 
In this archival photograph, the Millau Viaduct is seen under construction. It opened to traffic in late 2004.
According to the bridge's official Web site, it is 8,071 feet long, 105 feet wide, 1,125 feet tall (which makes it 62 feet taller than the Eiffel Tower), and cost about $562 million to build. It has seven towers, and 154 stays--11 pairs per tower pylon.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/18/a391a808-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/84505054059b0dafbcfa6b1358e5aa76/Viaduct_with_fireworks.png
Fireworks explode over the Millau Viaduct to celebrate its opening in 2004.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a38dbf4a-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5226354027f527f2557ad07f08994080/Straight_up_on_the_Viaduc.jpg
This is a look straight up at one of the towers of the Millau Viaduct from the deck of the bridge itself.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a38810d5-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/02ef54bd76ea28851e292ab96d72a52e/Two_towers_up_high.jpg
From a hill far from the bridge, two towers of the Millau Viaduct dominate the Tarn Valley.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a38153ba-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9d78925f68e1e862ffd2ce5b7258d2c9/Three_towers_north_from_info_center.jpg
Just underneath the bridge on the south side, there is an official information center. This photograph shows the view of three of the towers to the north of the center.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a379d422-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d6a8b2af85a7b006f38a83b7b9f64fac/New_Viewing_Platform.jpg
There are seven official Millau Viaduct viewing areas, including this one on a hill high above and several kilometers away. It is a popular place to visit to check out the world's tallest bridge.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/18/a372b3d5-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d37459b347a9390754b5869fccc9bfa6/Viaduct_with_clouds.png
In this photograph, the Millau Viaduct is seen with a cloud bank underneath it.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a36cf260-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1940c5a609a069816ec4124b05409ec2/New_With_RS5.jpg
Six towers of the Millau Viadcut--the world's tallest bridge--are seen here in the background with the Audi RS5 that CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman is driving on Road Trip 2011 in the foreground.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a3649169-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b4e2a14f1b4d47e49791f1cfbe5c11a5/Two_towers_north_to_ground.jpg
In this photograph, two of the bridge's massive towers are seen to the north.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a35fc244-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/faed652b87ad9bab231682f419c322ee/Low_angle_six_towers.jpg
From many kilometers away, six towers of the Millau Viaduct are seen spanning the Tarn Valley near Millau, France.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a3593446-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6cdaff87a140ab9a04445b9896411dc8/On_the_Viaduc.jpg
This is a view of the Millau Viaduct taken while driving across the deck of the bridge itself.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a3539ae8-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9631c9d4a5c928898d5eafe3dc67d32f/At_night.jpg
At night, the towers of the Millau Viaduct are lit and are the only part of the bridge visible from afar.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a348a44b-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/55137a2d8cdba9f2cbf9a4f9cc72a190/Across_rooftops.jpg
From many places in Millau, it is possible to see the bridge, even across rooftops.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a3439e5a-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/80d9e0a8f0d07522f6e8905877c53f53/Viaduc_de_Millau_2.jpg
Here, we see five of the towers of the Millau Viaduct--called the "Viaduc de Millau" in French--from just underneath and to the east.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a33f0056-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6f9f70c7840c4c8c2b47b5f04b18bcf6/Just_on_the_Viaduc_1.jpg
Driving south, this is a view of the Millau Viaduct from the north side of the deck.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a3394ba6-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c267b038bccaaf3d9cf14dab637a064d/Aire_du_Viaduc_1.jpg
Just north of the Millau Viaduct, there is a sign indicating one of the many official viewpoints. You can see all seven towers of the bridge in the distance.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a3316d42-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b61ca19b639aff0a8f02ec4c5da60c8f/Approaching_from_highway_1.jpg
Just before crossing the bridge, it can be seen in the distance ahead.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a32bdede-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/87127e7987a0e93309ae8ec52d95a18e/New_Seven_towers_across_field.jpg
All seven towers of the Millau Viaduct are seen from across a golden field of wheat.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/15/a31fefda-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3aa2b327fef6839bc7ac4f84f6fa9ce5/New_Seven_towers_up_high.jpg

[Date]2011-07-26  BMW
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6ebab15f-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ffd976eb4beb257a5c51ad98a91793b0/First_BMW_car_3-15_PS.jpg
This is the first BMW model of car, a 3/15 PS, made from 1929 to 1932. "Although basically a British Austin Seven built under license," a sign at the museum reads, "BMW's engineers were able to incorporate many improvements such as four-wheel brakes, a larger all-steal body, and a higher standard of interior equipment and trim."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/25/6eb84232-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/921e1fad30a65fd964f27b4cf998fb23/Spiral_stairs.jpg
Reminiscent of the Guggenheim Museum in New York, the BMW Museum's central feature is a bowl that brings visitors into a "system of seemingly hovering platforms,"
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6eb0779d-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4446a0dcbcb8ff8483a8217722014fc1/First_BMW_car_3-15_PS_-_Van.jpg
In addition to being offered as a standard car, the BMW 3/15 PS was also offered starting in 1930 as a small van that had two seats and a loading space in the back. Though it topped out at 47 miles an hour, this version of the van was sold as an "express delivery vehicle."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6eab30dc-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/691edd316ea948323b764efaf489c72d/1936_328.jpg
This 1936 BMW 328 roadster, is "among the legends of automobile history," claims the BMW Museum. "The sum of its qualities made it almost unbeatable in the two-liter motor racing category. Only 403 cars were built to standard road-going specification, and are now among the most exclusive and historically significant classics."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6ea53a6e-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ac47aeca31040b797a677db307ecad15/1939_328_Mille_Miglia_roadster.jpg
This is the 1939 BMW 328 Mille Miglia Roadster, a car that the company sees as a "unique source of inspirations for BMW Design" even today thanks to "an emotive formal idiom that united aesthetic and functional aspects."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6e9d6497-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/96589f2a916c17d2bd6f9a1625f11172/1939_BMW_328.jpg
This is a 1939 BMW 328.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6e93d5b9-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/cdfb2dc24a4304cc07291a3806fe65a8/1934_315-1.jpg
This a 1934 BMW 315/1, the company's first 6-cylinder roadster. The two-seater was designed to be lightweight, yet have a high power output.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/25/6e8a42a6-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/127608323df86a9e1c313dfc994a88ea/BMW_Museum.jpg
On the right is the BMW Museum, with its entry building in the foreground. On the left is the BMW headquarters office highrise.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6e82d788-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3e94455c2b66e077a21713c893efbb4c/1939_BMW_355.jpg
This is a 1939 BMW 355, the car maker's first luxury "saloon." "The BMW sales department named it the 'Autobahn' model because of its suitability for Germany's new high-speed roads," a museum sign reads. "The powerful 6-cylinder engine and the lightweight body and running gear made it the fastest car in its class. For well-off customers, a two- or four-door convertible was available as an alternative to the saloon."
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6e7f8ef3-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fa408bcc3243c67613dcca5a5f53c39e/1956_507.jpg
This is the 1956 BMW 507. According to the BMW Museum, this car "is regarded as one of the most beautiful sports cars of all time.Almost all the BMW 507s ever built have survived in museums and car collections."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6e7b3ce2-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/92341d4421cca14371b2fd8f5c9e3f31/First_BMW_roadster.jpg
This is the 1930 BMW 3/15 PS DA 3 Typ Wartburg, the company's first roadster. It was made under license from Austin. "The small two-seater weighed only [904 pounds], so that despite its low power output, it scored many a success in motor sport."
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6e74efb2-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a4f70a57b5682d86118beb7e46c505e6/1955_Isetta.jpg
This is the 1955 BMW Isetta, "Motocoupe," which was originally created by Italy's Ise. The car "helped BMW to overcome through a difficult commercial phase and avoid dismissing members of its workforce," according to the museum. "Various versions of this successful 'bubble car' were built, amounting to more than 160,000 by the time production ceased in 1962."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6e6f68e4-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/19859691df1ae476d8fcee6b28e1cf52/1968_BMW_3.jpg
This is the top model in the BMW 2500 and 2800 line, the 1968 3.3 Li, the immediate predecessors to the company's long-successful 7-Series.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6e653a99-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ba2ebc582b22495a23187d14e434c02f/1966_2002ti.jpg
This is the 1966 BMW 2002 ti. According to the museum, "BMW introduced a new midsize model in 1966: the BMW 1600-2. It marked the beginning of an exceptional success story. Over the next ten years, more cars from what was later known as the 02 Series were sold than the complete output of BMW cars since 1929. The combination of modern, compact bodies, sports engines, and running gear and suitability for more modest family motoring provoked world wide interest....The most notable cars in this model line were those bearing the 2002 badge [whose] engines developed between 100 and 170 horsepower and gave these lightweight saloons, convertibles, and touring models their remarkable dynamism."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6e5c1337-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5dd16779eaef92ce4998d5125e3b016e/M1.jpg
This is the world-famous BMW M1.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6e54b986-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/aa66d6df799a4c1efee63ab968b665b7/R32_motorcycle.jpg
This is a 1923 BMW R 32 motorcycle. Its transverse flat-twin engine and shaft drive came to be characteristic features of the company's motorcycles, according to the museum.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/24/6e460552-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bb32ce6237b3096c0345553aef6ccc53/1999_Z9_GT.jpg
This is the 1999 BMW Z9 GT which "ushered in a new design era for BMW's large saloon and coupe models," according to the museum. "Its combination of presence, dynamism, and sculptured styling reformulated classic BMW design elements, and its engineering innovations both inside and out exerted an influence throughout the automobile industry."

 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-07-31  Customize Poche
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4bdfad6f-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/89563400746a7f0ab7b4d90f5d2d1d10/Working_on_Porsche.jpg
Here, in the Porsche Exclusive workshop in Stuttgart, a highly trained employee labors to help a customer get just what he or she wants out of his or her new car.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4bd969f4-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8d8f2c545abb5c0776827f6ab983c202/Red_with_black_hood.jpg
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4bd12c86-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/96d57d016faf91a5f15c3b4f0ddd7a43/Rear_spoiler.jpg
This is the same red 911 GT with a black spoiler.
In most cases, Porsche will do whatever customization a customer asks for. An American ostrich farmer, for example, got his new Porsche with his own ostrich leather used in the interior. And an Arabian king ordered cars in the country's national colors for all his ministers.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4bcabbc8-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c47b514e7f615993c37d9d4e534f5291/Last_Speedsters.jpg
Porsche made just 356 of one of the hottest models to come off its production lines in years: the Speedster. Each Speedster is considered special enough that it is outfitted in the Exclusive workshop, and all options buyers had were considered special, though they were included in the car's price. These are the last two Speedsters Porsche produced.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4bc41644-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a9581b4d1fcf1e6c76d0713df89925b6/Interior_with_red_trim.jpg
This is an example of a custom red trim ordered by a Porsche customer.
The company does its best to satisfy buyers' requests, but sometimes it has to say no. For example, one buyer asked for a different spoiler for his car's front than is included in the aerodynamic kit that also includes a rear spoiler. Porsche had to say no because the change would have affected the car's aerodynamics. Another customer asked for a different sound from the tail pipe, and Porsche said that while it would be happy to do the job, the law wouldn't allow the modification.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4bba0a5a-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c27727fd3c59155e37c80853a2ebfb41/Five_being_worked_on.jpg
Here, we see six Porsches being worked on in the company's Exclusive Department shop.

A group of brand-new Porsche cars are lined up and waiting to have one kind of special customization or another added.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4ba9907f-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b765a46b92089a5e7c757e760c947a73/Yellow_trim.jpg
In the Porsche Exclusive showroom, the company showcases many of the different kinds of customization it will do for customers. For example, this yellow exterior--with matching trim in the interior--is not standard.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4b9f8cfa-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/86e55eab8c4dd5b36bd6883fb2abb78b/Blue_stitching.jpg
This blue stitching was requested by a new Porsche customer to replace the standard black.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4b99d909-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6c2d9fddcf24ee703b60e5841525d6fb/25_years_Porsche_Exclusive.jpg
For 25 years, Porsche has offered customers willing to pay a bit more for their cars a wide range of special customization choices. Some choose to have a plate with the Porsche Exclusive logo added to their car.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4b900a25-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c5758b3a52207d0cbc0846d28810a956/All_brown_coloring.jpg
Few would likely choose this all-brown color scheme, but if someone wanted it, Porsche would produce it. For a fee.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4b894d7d-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a7334ba32dcb0c7928ff2875462ad816/Leather_choices.jpg
Customers can visit Porsche's Exclusive showroom in Stuttgart, and during a consultation that might take hours, they can examine a wide range of potential leather choices that they could have the interior of their new car outfitted in.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4b834c8e-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3930ab1c2a817322afc2b2504917d9a2/Color_chips.jpg
Similarly, customers can also examine a wide variety of colors that they can request for the exterior of their new Porsche.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/07/28/4b7db74e-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c900e2f43aac42a742d5986c412a6737/Carrera_GTS.jpg
A Porsche Carrera GTS is parked in the company's Exclusive workshop, awaiting its customization job.

Here we see the Porsche Exclusive showroom in Stuttgart, Germany.
 
 
 
 
 
[Date]2011-08-10    
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/223bd45d-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/672400c71325c6bd7337a3692a6a5cdb/Zeppelin_in_hangar.jpg
This is the Zeppelin NT flown by Deutsche Zeppelin-Reederei, a ZLT subsidiary.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/22351dbc-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/54acd3334c78af49a1c4cad81dabc5d0/Zeppelin_NT_hangar_exterior_with_Audi.jpg
Seen here in front of the Zeppelin NT hangar in Friedrichshafen, Germany, is the Audi A6 TFSI that CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman drove through Switzerland and Germany on Road Trip 2011.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/222ef47f-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/60805b5b44864aee427c2de87b8e23b9/Zeppelin_in_hangar_on_mast_w_truck.jpg
On a stormy day, ZLT's Zeppelin NT cannot take passengers aloft, so it sits inside the company's hangar in Friedrichshafen, Germany, moored to its truck-based mast.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/2229b450-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ac9916c5f62bb430e3aa0471c0a532a2/Rear_of_zeppelin_with_propellers.jpg
This is the rear rotor of the Zeppelin NT. Because of its internal airframe and flexible rotors, it is able to take off much like a helicopter, unlike a blimp, which has much less maneuverability. Rotors on the side of the Zeppelin can rotate up to 120 degrees, while the rear rotor can rotate 90 degrees. According to ZLT, "The capabilities of a vertical take-off, a precise landing, hovering on one spot and a backward flight are the unique flight characteristics enabled by the swivelling propellers."

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/22222602-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/aa67e86baf2435cc6247340312e57ccf/Zeppelin_truck.jpg
This is one of the mooring trucks inside the ZLT hangar.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/22181f6e-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f9867a5dfa3e029bc58991b58315759b/Zeppelin_scale_model.jpg
This is artist Josef Hawle's painted Zeppelin, "Luftschiff-Bodensee," created for the Zeppelin Parade exhibit in Friedrichshafen, Germany, and currently on display outside the offices of ZLT, in Friedrichshafen.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/22124084-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d725190e12e2e8ea0b1c9977d8326bef/Parts_for_new_zeppelin_wide_in_hangar.jpg
Seen here, against the wall of the giant Zeppelin NT hangar, are struts and internal parts for a new Zeppelin that is being constructed inside the hangar. The parts are actually from a decommissioned Japanese Zeppelin and the new airship is scheduled to fly in April of 2012.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/220e6cc3-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c7677319e0a9787b4f678aeec5fc5a4b/Parts_2.jpg
A close-up of some of the struts that will be used in the construction of a new Zeppelin NT.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/22047bd6-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/eeb45c489e96db74954510da66ccea44/Parts_1.jpg
A close-up of a second set of parts for the next Zeppelin NT.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/21fd7c48-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a5bc959efc8f79ca589e87bbfe09b4bd/Zeppelin_NT_parking.jpg
Those who have bought tickets to fly aboard the Zeppelin NT in Friedrichshafen park their cars near the ZLT hangar.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/21fa4f84-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/026367505834b0b7d33f5ba628aca6ca/Zeppelin_NT_hangar_exterior.jpg
This is the exterior of the Zeppelin NT hangar, just adjacent to the Zeppelin Luftschifftechnik headquarters in Friedrichshafen, Germany.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/21f1908a-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/057cd3556e91ca9c87f5bf17c503e18f/Zeppelin_NT_frame_illustration.jpg
This is an illustration of a Zeppelin NT, as seen inside the Zeppelin NT hangar.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/21eb2ea3-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e8598fa509f22a60fac91e409d6df5be/Photo_2_of_zeppelin_NT_interior_frame.jpg
This is a ZLT archival photograph of the interior structure of a Zeppelin NT. One of the major differences between a Zeppelin and a blimp is that the Zeppelin has an interior structure, while a blimp doesn't.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/21e37e60-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2701f6128529424f7d6e2e672717b3eb/Engine.jpg
This is a Zeppelin NT engine, as seen inside the Zeppelin NT hangar in Friedrichshafen, Germany. Every Zeppelin NT has three Lycoming IO-360 engines with 197 horsepower each.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/02/21d5e510-f0f3-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9978eaad1c6e3c8416b3c392331fe481/Cab.jpg
This is the cab of the Zeppelin NT operated by ZLT subsidiary DZR.








[Date]2011-08-18 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f3b549ae-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1eb0c325dd10211698b44097506b2b2f/DSC_4962.jpg
This is one of the earliest watches in the museum's collection, which dates back to 1500. It is the "Runde Halsuhr," which was made in southern Germany of gilded brass between 1530 and 1540. Made in the shape of a drum, it has a cover (seen hanging) and what the museum says is a "straight-line foliate" made of iron.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f3ac02af-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c00a31cf65a02707f6d51b3e372f3786/DSC_4969.jpg
This is a rectangular travel set that includes a table clock and two tools needed for writing with a fountain pen, a square ink bottle and a sand bottle. It dates to between 1550 and 1555.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f3a29067-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0db804a14d2c8c8dfd630805786330f4/DSC_4967.jpg
This is a sundial watch made in southern Germany around 1570. It is a drum watch with what is known as a "stackfreed." It features an hour-striking clock-watch with a cover, and has movement with straight-line foliate and power regulation by stackfreed. The watch is stamped "HK," for Hans Koch, and its inside cover has a sundial and a compass. It is signed and dated "MP/1592" for Markus Purman, who died in 1619.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f39bbd21-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c5746eb45363a9a3edf887ae58b02765/DSC_4978.jpg
This is a hexagonal table-clock, made in Aix-en-Provence, France around 1540. It is an hour-striking clock with circular foliate and alarm. It is attributed to watch maker Pierre de Fobix.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f39214f8-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ed951229554ec6c0db1eb11146447a8a/DSC_4981_1.jpg
In this picture, we see two spherical clocks. On the left in the back, there is a watch in the shape of a terrestrial globe, which was said to have been inspired by a heart-shaped world map published in Paris in 1526. It is made of gilded brass and features movement with circular foliate and an alarm. The watch is attributed to Jacques de la Garde. On the front and right, we see an hour-striking clock-watch in the shape of a terrestrial globe, also made of gilded brass, with a fitted leather traveling case (in the back right). This watch is also attributed to Jacques de la Garde.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f38ac15c-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9d88f79ebf9bad92e840b1a68438ded4/DSC_4991.jpg
This is an enamel watch with diamonds that would have been worn on a ribbon, hanging from the waist of an elaborate outfit, in the 17th century style. It is attributed to the Parisian School, and was made around 1655.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f37f565e-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/99232fbf4f633de3131267e62ccde747/DSC_4997.jpg
This is the "mon coeur bat," or "my heart beats" watch and case, made in Paris between 1660 and 1670. It is a square shaped watch. It is made of iron, gold, enamel, and rock crystal. Its original outer, protective case features gold pin work. This set belonged to New York businessman John Pierpoint Morgan.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f379acb4-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3d245d862c16ce9b3fc5725a14164499/DSC_4998.jpg
This is a square watch made in Paris around 1660. It has scrolled decoration, and was signed by Auguste Bretonneau. It is made from iron, gold, enamel, and rock crystal.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f3762910-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9092e4387fd554f79d978c5ef820daed/DSC_5003.jpg
This is the Anthony and Cleopatra watch, made in London around 1745. It is made of gold and enamel.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f371187f-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2ac14f0e0da64f8d5abf0eb6a062410d/DSC_5008.jpg
This is a set of tools worn by the "chatelaine," or lady of the house, in the case of this set, it might well have been a near-sighted lady, since a magnifying glass is included.
This elaborate pair-cased watch with its decorative clasp--which is also called a chatelaine--holds a perfume box known as a vinaigrette, a folding magnifying lass, a crank key, and a seal. It was made in London around 1770 of enamel on gold.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f366cf1e-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/42125b052146320a817d98fb6c03d891/DSC_5024_2.jpg
This watch is designed to look like a game board in the "draught-board" stayle, and is enameled using the "ors paillonnes" technique. It is a Louis XVI style center-seconds watch, made for the Chinese market. It was made in Geneva in 1785 out of gold, enamel, rubies, and pearls.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f362f488-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d86ea3bd00966a9ce9b3b547c5708924/DSC_5031.jpg
Here, we see a singing bird resting on a watch decorated in gold, enamel, rubies, and pearls. It is a Louis XVI style watch with a singing bird triggered upon request. It was made in Geneva around 1785 for the Chinese market.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f35e3b0b-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/63a16acda8880f00fa5fa5d038df5f56/DSC_5046.jpg
These butterflies, made of gold, enamel, pearls, and diamonds, feature compartments, including the one on the front right, that lifts to show a watch face. The others show automata and music. These were made in Geneva around 1815.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f358bd78-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9a2d6325d18a2720f59ed4719bef19a3/DSC_5047.jpg
This watch was elaborately made in the shape of a rose, with automata and music. It was made in Geneva in 1815 for the Chinese market. It is made of gold, enamel, and pearls.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f350e80f-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f02960ac128bd55c66e35fdcd0d00834/DSC_5055.jpg
This is one of a pair of ornamental watches in the collection that are in the shape of perfume-sprinkling flintlock pistols. They were made in Geneva in 1805 from gold, enamel, pearls, and agate.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f347efa7-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/76c0929c8a9f8eb51f88e5e6c4de9db3/DSC_5076.jpg
This is an "Empire"-style quarter-repeating watch with automata and music. It was made around 1815 of gold and enamel.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f340e539-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/02e09e4bfc5d12b3710555ad4fb26fbd/DSC_5084.jpg
This watch is made in a walnut shell and incorporates a magician automaton and music. It was made in Geneva in 1810 of walnut shell, wood, and enamel.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f33b1c86-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4749bbe6d560630c4967ec1a3c5ffe4a/DSC_5090.jpg
This watch, made in Paris in 1800, provided its owner with a tactful way to put a hand in the pocket in order to "feel" the time without having to take it out and read it. It is made of gold, silver, diamonds, and enamel.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f3337a34-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9e6fd08b5a8ab2fd7e9b10cc08ff2dbe/DSC_5104.jpg
This decorative pendant watch is done in the Viennese style. It is made of gold, enamel, and pearls, and was made in Geneva in 1830.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f322231d-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/145acf84d91282873a64732536716c8a/DSC_5120.jpg
This is an oval watch with expanding hour and minute hands. It was made in London around 1794 for the Chinese market.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f31b8dd7-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/259e6d31c68d477c5a1515eca8dba5fd/DSC_5131.jpg
According to a Patek Philippe Museum guide, this handmade wooden watch was not made by a professional watchmaker, but by herdsmen looking for a way to occupy a long winter.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f3153a81-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/590f7347b211307a2f316beae91d00be/DSC_5133.jpg
Here, we see a reflection of the face of the wooden watch. Its numbers are reversed because of the reflection.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f310a75a-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/416b083e5f9d98ac6c010f2ca7449412/DSC_5140.jpg
This elaborate ostrich feather fan is hiding a small watch. It was made in Geneva around 1845 of gold, enamel, and diamonds.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f30bc9e8-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fac2ae22c8d458064fe86358176e9c7e/DSC_5174.jpg
This is the Caliber 89, made for Patek Philippe's 150th anniversary in 1989. It is said to be the world's most complicated watch and features 1,728 components along with 184 wheels, 61 bridges, 332 screws, 415 pins, 68 springs, and 429 mechanical parts.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f300326d-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ec2146af6530c0218ed49f550c2cf9d3/DSC_5169.jpg
This is a close-up of the inner workings of the Caliber 89. The watch's "complications" include a record-breaking 33 different abilities, including the ability to figure out the date of Easter until 2017, as well as the time of sunset and sunrise, a leap-year correction, the age and phases of the moon, and more.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f2f93433-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/aba383ee71f0b294f1f33680ebaf5974/DSC_5172.jpg
The Caliber 89 is a double-faced watch showing a star chart on one side with the Milky Way and 2,800 distinct stars.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f2efc3ac-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a0c49a37dcd77306213d12a5677bea66/DSC_5204.jpg
Here, we see the inner workings of the star chart watch, including the functions of the sidereal calendar.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f2ea0b13-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f4c13d970e49f881967b95a6939d745e/DSC_5175.jpg
This is a Patek Philippe pocket watch with a pierced and engraved caliber. It is known as a skeleton watch. It was made in Geneva around 1975 from gold and enamel.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2011/08/15/f2ded9cc-f0f2-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/097ae84dfc070ab2fb72bafea519b8af/DSC_5210.jpg
Each of these watches were worn into outer space, according to the certification letter displayed alongside them.
 














[Date]2013-07-02  Heurtley House (Frank Lloyd Wright)
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/01/1185021d-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/615b1d664369840c88ecc64839b8faf2/Heurtley_house_side.jpg
This is the Arthur B. Heurtley House, built in 1902.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/01/118273f1-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1695fd112cc977e1fad4deb69a4d7b05/Walter_Gale_house.jpg
Just a few doors down from the Frank Lloyd Wright Home and Studio is this stunner, the Walter H. Gale house, which is one of a group of houses that he designed when he was still employed by Louis Sullivan. Because he was not allowed to moonlight, these houses are known as "bootlegs." Wright designed it in 1893.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/01/117feac7-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5e3c639e048fd962eaaf8e504983cfa5/Hills-DeCaro_house.jpg
This is the Hills-DeCaro house, which was built in 1906 and substantially reconstructed in 1977. It was designated a local landmark in 2002.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/01/117d7fe1-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9c41dfb50d10ea79a663477c196ca8a2/Frank_Thomas_house.jpg
This is the gorgeous Frank Thomas house, which Wright designed in 1901.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/01/117b06a3-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c498e175a318eacea33adb4fcc048850/Heurtley_house.jpg
Another look at the Arthur B. Heurtley House.

A Unitarian himself, Wright designed Oak Park's Unity Temple, which was built between 1905 and 1908.

A look at the Unity Temple from one of Oak Park's thoroughfares, Lake Street.

A look at the Frank Thomas house from the side.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/01/116f01b4-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0cc2013c41d3979eab0667b15b081025/Laura_Gale_house.jpg
Featuring some similarity to Wright's masterpiece Fallingwater, this is the Laura Gale House, just a few blocks from the Frank Lloyd Wright Home and Studio.

Just a block from Wright's house and studio is the Nathan G. Moore House, which the famed architect had built in 1895, and then again in 1923 after a fire gutted it.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/01/116792cf-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e27c06fa85ca3665e5003c61fce08337/Thomas_Gale_house.jpg
Another bootleg house just steps from Wright's home and studio, this is the Thomas H. Gale House, which Wright designed in 1892.

Yet another bootleg house, this is the Robert P. Parker house, designed in 1892.

This is the Frank Lloyd Wright Home and Studio, where the famed architect lived and worked from 1889 to 1909.
 
 
[Date]2013-07-03  In Chicago, the birthplace of the nuclear age
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0ea6e7cf-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f564132acc478d4584095dbadf7ad988/CP-1_drawing.jpg
This is an artist's drawing of Chicago Pile-1, which was created in about a month. It was a pile, 24 feet wide by 24 feet long, and 19 feet high, of machined uranium and graphite blocks.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0ea46b0d-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6822ada803d9e37a6154891a8b5c7b3d/Scale_model_showing_squash_court.jpg
This scale model, housed in the Nuclear Energy Exhibit at Argonne National Laboratory in Lemont, Ill., shows how the Chicago Pile-1 project was constructed inside the squash court under the bleachers at the University of Chicago's football field.
According to a  document (PDF)  hosted on the U.S. Department of Energy's Web site, that location was chosen in a hurry after the first choice, the Argonne Woods, was abandoned due to "slow construction." As a result, "the largest space available" was selected.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0ea1c0f6-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8ea57573067443d6976a18032eb5a7f2/Scale_model_with_person.jpg
This scale model of Pile-1 shows its size relative to a human being. All together, according to the document hosted on the Department of Energy's Web site, the pile contained 385.5 tons of graphite and 46.5 tons of uranium metal and oxides.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e9f2d10-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bf91a73083d7efda9cb93b20e8c0ea33/Scale_model_from_behind.jpg
This view of the scale model in the Nuclear Energy Exhibit at Argonne National Lab shows where the people who viewed the experiment watched from.
In addition, it also shows (if you look closely) that a man was stationed against the railing holding on to a rope that pulled a control rod. In an emergency, that rope was supposed to be cut, which would slide the rod into the core, absorbing neutrons and stopping the chain reaction.
As a backup safety measure, buckets of cadmium in liquid were ready to be poured over the pile, which would leak into it and shut down the reaction, according to Roger Blomquist, a nuclear engineer at Argonne National Lab.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e9cd487-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1a4d8a6f239f1cb10cc9d4c6dc79cbf6/Birth_certificate.jpg
This chart, known as the "birth certificate of the atomic age,"  according to Argonne National Lab, shows the neutron intensity as measured by a galvanometer during the world's first-ever controlled nuclear chain reaction on December 2, 1942.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e9a385e-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c7b90208147726ba349eeff947f3c836/Memorial_U_of_Chicago.jpg
Chicago Pile-1 was dismantled almost as fast as it was constructed, and it was quickly re-assembled in the Argonne Woods near today's Argonne National Lab. Today, all that remains of one of history's most important technological events is this memorial, found in the middle of the University of Chicago.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e978c7a-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/644c74eef7d84f5dafde92d0bf15fe0c/Plaque_2.jpg
A plaque near the memorial at the University of Chicago reads, "On December 2, 1942, man achieved here the first self-sustaining chain reaction and thereby initiated the controlled release of nuclear energy."
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e94be8a-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e9aacad8a1f010b52a9cd6eac3531e0b/Fermi.jpg
Enrico Fermi won the Nobel Prize in 1938. As the leader of the Pile-1 project, he was the man most responsible for demonstrating that man could tame nuclear energy. According to Argonne National Lab's Blomquist, nobody has ever combined a mastery of physics and mathematics at such a high level as Fermi.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e9217fb-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d6aaff80eb8467023e1d2b7ff5498c51/Packing_graphite.jpg
In this 1956 photograph, Walter Zinn, one of the leaders of the Chicago Pile-1 experiment, oversees technicians putting graphite from the CP-1 reactor into a storage facility in Building 24 on the current Argonne National Lab campus.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e8f9b4b-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/85d57990390a6aabd30d95bb3dec382c/Chianti.jpg
After the successful completion of the Chicago Pile-1 experiment -- which Fermi concluded after about 28 minutes of a self-sustaining chain reaction -- each of the 49 people on hand were given a small amount of chianti from this bottle, seen in a photograph at the Argonne National Lab.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e8d3413-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5df88152de69cc8186f87aae74165611/Signatures.jpg
The signatures, collected 20 years later, of most of the 49 scientists involved in the Chicago Pile-1 experiment. Notably missing is Fermi's signature.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e8a99f3-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/21ba456b6c1185fd9aee7ecc72bee9c8/Graphite.jpg
The pile, which was built on top of a wooden frame on the squash court under the Stagg Field bleachers, consisted of "alternate layers of graphite, containing uranium metal and/or oxide," according to the document on the Department of Energy's Web site, which "were separated by layers of solid graphite blocks to form a lattice structure."
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e880a9c-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1b75478909c48c38cbfe35471b14dd06/Were_cooking.jpg
This record shows the progress of the Chicago Pile-1 experiment, and at the bottom left of the right-hand page, "We're cookin!" was written to signify that the experiment was successful. That phrase was used, according to a placard at the Argonne National Lab's Nuclear Energy Exhibit, because "a nuclear reaction has been likened to the burning of a pile of oily rags that in certain circumstances can ignite spontaneously."
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e8577b9-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f452be6096e2aa82269463c50602951a/Photo_of_Stagg_Field_West_Stands.jpg
This photograph, on the wall of the Argonne National Lab's Nuclear Energy Exhibit, shows the University of Chicago's Stagg Field bleachers, inside of which the Chicago Pile-1 experiment was conducted in secret.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e8262e2-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0bd185d138e1350b5626d2c428f8139c/CP-2.jpg
This photograph, hosted by the Argonne National Lab, depicts Chicago Pile-2 in 1943. The so-called CP-2 was created "when Chicago Pile 1, the world's first nuclear reactor, was dismantled and moved to the Cook County Forest Preserve near Palos Hills, Ill.. At the new, isolated location, the reactor was reassembled with some refinements and modifications and renamed Chicago Pile 2. CP-2 had a thermal-power level of 10 kW and was fueled by natural uranium. A small laboratory atop the 14,000-ton reactor provided space for limited experiments using neutrons from the reactor's core. The reactor's face contained ports through which materials could be inserted into the core for irradiation."
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/02/0e7fe462-f07b-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/40374b4eee9456006576e48148ea2d3d/Site_A.jpg
Originally selected as the home for the world's first nuclear reactor and the first-ever sustained chain reaction, Site A, in the Argonne Woods, was host to a re-creation of Chicago Pile-1 immediately after the successful experiment conducted at the University of Chicago. CP-1 was dismantled and reassembled at Site A.
 
 
[Date]2013-07-04  Blue Waters  
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff3e7baf-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e674d0589a75e069962cf2086fde9a45/Inside_tape_storage_cabinet.jpg
This is a look inside one of the Spectra T-Finity cabinets that make up the 380 petabytes of long term storage.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff3c2fc3-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/84fec6f74901d0f5bb202b7175e91665/Blue_Waters_whole_system.jpg
The National Petascale Computing Facility was built with 20,000 of usable space for Blue Waters. The facility was built with biometric scanners for security -- so that it can compete for future applications with high-security operations elsewhere -- and can withstand an F3 tornado.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff34fb73-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5f448e2234a8f1d1e9bd0da64b394114/cables_above.jpg
It takes a huge amount of cables to power and connect all the Blue Waters nodes, as this look at the wiring infrastructure underneath the supercomputer reveals.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff32ad11-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/db9da06244e554bbfc417f55f81b3db7/Blue_Waters_rear_open_close-up.jpg
Blue Waters consists of 12 rows of 24 Cray cabinets, including this one, seen from the back with its wiring on display.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff3071e9-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4cfb08a0c50d7a0c268d21249a417f8b/Blue_Waters_blades.jpg
Each of the Blue Waters cabinets has 24 blades arrayed in three rows of eight blades each.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff2e2ffb-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5742d2a7d1fef3ec70dfff69b030beee/Blue_Waters_down_a_row.jpg
There are 12 rows of 24 Cray cabinets in the Blue Waters configuration.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff2c0379-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d69b8cd8e04b60e87e47312fd605c683/Cray.jpg
Cray has a number of employees on-site at the National Petascale Computing Facility in order to service and maintain Blue Waters.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff2a1eaa-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3c90f0e1ca1914fe59265b09f2d81aa3/Tape_storage_1.jpg
For long-term storage needs, the National Petascale Computing Facility provides Blue Waters users with 380 petabytes of tape storage.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff274b9d-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d608cb381436631ffee3e2e87a50b74b/Blue_Waters_cooling_piping_silver.jpg
Each of Blue Waters' rows has three cooling cabinets at the end, which are connected to the computing nodes via the silver piping on the top.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff24cc3d-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b36891f656baea83e86c833c6ff84ff9/Cray_Sonexion_Cabinets_-_closeup.jpg
 
In addition to the 380 petabytes of long-term tape storage available to Blue Waters users, there is also 26 petabytes of short-term, online storage available via these Cray Sonexion machines.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff225ba4-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bb10a1d5b4ca436e0ee598984825678f/Cabling_under_floor.jpg
Opening up the floor in the Blue Waters room reveals massive amounts of cables and wires that connect the various nodes.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff2009cf-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/826d078bb099c1d83a8202e42ec91495/Pump_room_1.jpg
In order to operate Blue Waters, the National Petascale Computing Facility maintains these massive cooling pipes, which altogether circulate 5,400 tons of chilled water with a capacity of 9,300 gallons per minute.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff1da5d8-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/63d5754fdd821617d7fb172b7454e4ae/Pump_room_with_heat_exchangers.jpg
Water is pumped through giant sumps and then to heat exchangers (on the left side, on the floor) in order to provide Blue Waters with enough cooling water.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff1b5e03-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8bf4b026af8959cdf4f2cd24349a6414/Transformer.jpg
In order to provide Blue Waters with enough power to stay operational 24 hours a day, the National Petascale Computing Facility has 20 transformers -- of which 14 are dedicated to the supercomputer. The system has a capacity of 13,800 volts coming in, and it is approved for a total of 24 megawatts, although it is currently only using about 13.5 megawatts. That means the facility can almost double in size if needed.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff19028b-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/972e69bc34efd71281e1b3b2d8cc2d9a/Electrical_room.jpg
The NPCF has a total of 20 giant transformers, 14 for Blue Waters, two spares, two which serve non-Blue Waters systems, and two which serve the facility itself.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff16cad2-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2db0f6523be1f91481416530385f7242/Sump.jpg
The NPCF cooling system includes several of these giant sumps.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff1481fe-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4a77907720c7af7b41ff55ce856f9c73/Tape_storage_cabinet_info_display.jpg
This display provides technicians with a quick view about the status of the 380-petabyte tape storage system.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff1227ef-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/61bb6bb7d631aba22c358be8fb55bf29/Cooling_cabinet_info_display.jpg
This display provides information about the status of the cooling cabinets.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff0fe925-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/bf77bc669523442f18af5c7c571f829a/Cooling_piping_under_floor.jpg
There is a six-foot deep well under the floor of the supercomputer room at the NPCF, inside of which is a network of these large cooling pipes.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/03/ff0da899-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8cd1b09c1ab9e2490951a37801e2af96/Whole_room.jpg
A look at the 20,000 square feet of space available for supercomputers at the National Petascale Computing Facility at the University of Illinois at Champaign-Urbana. Though Blue Waters is already one of the world's most powerful supercomputers, the facility has room to expand.
 
 
[Date]2013-07-05   The Bean
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/04/ff64c33c-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9f1f938b970e3354a4240472cf3b8ef1/IMG_5802.jpg
The Bean
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/04/ff625d94-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a792e8f9ff069881f8b508c10d9c6a6c/IMG_5826.jpg
The Bean is entirely reflective, and distorts what it reflects, so the incredible skies of a perfect July 4th are stunning when seen alongside Kapoor's masterpiece.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/04/ff5ffd98-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dc5b945a15ba64bc9474f89b07bc3e97/IMG_5823.jpg
Located in the heart of ATT Plaza in Chicago's terrific Millennium Park, The Bean is easy to see, and has something for everyone.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/04/ff5d8f33-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ea0d64b488dceb96dcd35950dc28f4fb/IMG_5808.jpg
The visuals underneath The Bean are unexpected and arresting.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/04/ff5b1e26-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/556ec63cf7a98d2826accc1270321798/IMG_5824.jpg
There's no right way to look at The Bean. It is meant to be looked at from any and every angle.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/04/ff58c38e-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9649a2c585c1a4aabff0f7cc999f79b9/IMG_5815.jpg
The Chicago skyline fills the reflection from The Bean's outside, while dozens of people inspect the view under its belly.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/04/ff563b42-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d4e08ac56e4de1312fc7d980381ff4a4/IMG_5814.jpg
A look at one edge of The Bean, with some of Chicago's tall buildings off to the side.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/04/ff53d9fe-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7bcd7f031602224c6ec782fe58bbb704/IMG_5816.jpg
Given its curved shape, and the way it distorts reflections, looking at The Bean through the fisheye lens of an Olloclip for iPhone 5 makes for a very interesting picture.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/04/ff5178c3-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e4615d43ea1ee5b21de8f85d1792b02b/IMG_5818.jpg
A close-up of one of The Bean's sides.

Can you spot CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman is this photo?

The edge of The Bean, which is metal, is entirely smooth, and almost looks like clear glass.

A look at The Bean from the side, using the Olloclip fisheye lens.

In this photo, The Bean almost looks to be holding up one of Chicago's many skyscrapers.
 
 
 
 
[Date]2013-07-08   Robie House, Frank Lloyd Wright's masterpiece, regains glory
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe835f6f-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c0f2e77b6d318bd15226de7e442f09a2/Robie_Hosue.jpg
With what is the quintessential example of Wright's Prairie Style of architecture, Robie House is a stunning work of art. Located at the University of Chicago, the house, completed in 1910 for businessman Frederick C. Robie, is in the middle of a major, multi-million-dollar renovation that should return it to its original glory.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe80cd32-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/55dd94577bfa1a9673aac30c6f748d05/Robie_House_looking_east.jpg
Robie House features Wright's signature geometric design, with long horizontal lines and striking angles at every turn. The house was a true collaboration between Wright and Robie, although the new owner only lived there for about 18 months before being forced to sell the building to help pay off his late father's debts.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe7e59e0-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7ad72c55f089e197aba66caecb508fa1/Robie_SW_corner_view.jpg
Robie purchased the lot for the house in 1908 for $13,500, when his family was still wealthy, and the house was finished in 1910.
The southwest corner of the house looks to many like a boat, and a lot of people refer to that corner as "the prow."
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe7bc376-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/195645554685665863c1f9f6fa77cdac/Roman_brick.jpg
Wright used Roman brick throughout Robie House because of their long rectangular, horizontal, shape, and the fact that he could use vertical mortar between the bricks that matched the color of the bricks, maintaining the sense that the facade was all horizontal lines.
Everything about the house instills the sense of horizontal conveyed by the flat landscape of the American prairie, which surrounded the house when it was built, and with which Wright was very familiar.
