http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe793ef6-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a8b4e31e876abfacc2eea7430893b7e0/Robie_private_entranceway_from_street.jpg
Wright enjoyed creating interesting entrances to his buildings and giving owners a sense of privacy outside their front doors. While it's not hard to find Robie House's front door, it is partially hidden away behind an outer gate.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe76d735-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/08bf9b062869b980efab953bed661ffe/Courtyard.jpg
Given that Robie House was built just a few years after mass production of cars began, it's quite possible -- although no one knows for sure -- that its attached three-car garage may be the world's first. The garage was purpose-built for cars rather than converted from storing horse-drawn carriages.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe732dfa-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2ac99041e924eb1fa30710a9bae655dc/Front_door.jpg
Robie House also features a wider-than-normal front door. Wright designed the building in order, like with many of his buildings, to bring the outside in, but maintain the privacy of those inside.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe70d2f6-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/662ae98d4394baa2ed212088b9c12e6e/Entrance_way.jpg
This is Robie House's entrance room. It features lots of natural colors, and a large carpet that George Mann Niedecken, who worked for Wright, designed.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe6e71cb-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1ccf492034fa1abb38b4a2149c451bb9/Entrance_way_hearth.jpg
Like so many of Wright's buildings, Robie House provides its residents with grand hearths. This one is in the house's entrance room.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe6c049b-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d03b411f0dcae1546fd5e7aee2a162da/Stairway_up.jpg
Wright enjoyed paths of discovery, and those could be stairways as well as hallways. This is the main Robie House stairway that leads up from the entrance room to the living room on the second level.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe698c22-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/66d3be8df9cbb42d2d98b37c3d19584b/Looking_out_over_interior_staircase.jpg
A look at the view from the top of the staircase.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe671dcc-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7b506f54fde9945e8a089e09f8a6e9a3/Living_room_looking_west.jpg
Those who climbed the Robie House staircase for the first time couldn't possibly have been prepared for just how grand the living room -- which they would encounter when they got to the top of the stairs -- was.
Designed with one of Wright's specialties -- the open floor plan -- the living room was a giant room featuring recessed lighting with what appears to be a Japanese influence, a common trait in Wright's houses.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe640eaf-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f4f19ed5abc4981c8ec449bdab23e925/Living_room_west_end.jpg
The west end of the Robie House living room.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe61a8b2-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/56e9979681277d9dcae01a5bba5de12a/Looking_back_at_house.jpg
A veranda on the west side of the house allows those inside to step outside where they have a lovely view of the surroundings, and a veiw back at the house, protected from the weather by the building's grand cantilevered roof.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe5f415b-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/179b7a2863da44d417bc4d4c161d6b1e/Balcony_and_cantilevered_roof.jpg
The balcony offers a look at University of Chicago buildings across the street, as well as protection from one of Wright's signature touches, a large, cantilevered roof.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe5ce1f8-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e2948b5b272519677691f561a46b4349/Living_room_balcony_south_side.jpg
A long narrow balcony abuts the south side of the Robie House's living room.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe57ddbc-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c0c01bcd1c1250f6f719e066e36d05ed/Robie_W_view_straight_on.jpg
Today, after having gone through many different owners -- including several institutions -- Robie House is owned by the University of Chicago and managed by the Frank Lloyd Wright Preservation Trust, which got a $5.5 million grant to do the renovation.
Twice, in 1941 and again in 1957, Robie House was slated for demolition, and both times Wright stepped in personally to save his masterpiece. Wright argued in 1957 that, "Why destroy this building any more than you would destroy an important painting? Because it's more important than any could possibly be."
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe557921-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/05c75341b4f0495acec1af8c5326013c/Living_room_art_glass.jpg
Like so many of Wright's buildings, Robie House features windows with what's known as "art glass." The house has 175 art glass windows.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe530d5b-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5f10d48b7df581f9d8605b416040be37/Living_room_art_glass_tall.jpg
These doors open onto the south-side balcony from the living room and feature Wright's famous art glass design.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe50ae88-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1992ff44213b9c8810b70ff56a6acb39/Art_glass_south_side.jpg
Wright brought the outside in through the use of dozens of windows throughout, but maintained the privacy of those inside, in part by using art glass instead of clear windows.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe4e4840-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d460fd553f4b4a170b13e5c9533c62cf/FLW_cornice.jpg
Another Wright signature was the use of cornices -- essentially rain gutters -- on the underneath edges of the roof.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe4bf42e-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/24a1286a77b743954e2fa9843cb1e6a6/Sconce.jpg
Robie House still has one original sconce, a light that casts beautiful shadows and indirect lighting, a favorite design element of Wright's.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe498cd0-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b70e0be8054df33ea94fca82bf805d82/Living_room_long_looking_east.jpg
A look back into the main part of the house from the west side of the living room.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe471997-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/608b426e1d4ba2cfa0b561a381c7f7f6/Living_room_hearth.jpg
The living room features another hearth, which in keeping with one of Wright's favorite design elements, has open space directly above the fireplace, and two flues on both sides.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe44765b-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4fd34f1c3720ee4314a9a3ac0ea2ed6a/Living_room_light_screens.jpg
A look at light screens on the ceiling of the Robie House living room.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe4202c2-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4bdf53912a1e27785cd7b8b69e77a8f1/Dining_room_built-ins.jpg
As he often did, Wright designed built-in furniture into the Robie House dining room. Doing this allowed him to maintain more control over the interior living space than many architects enjoy.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe3f9e7c-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2c82f5a0efd6a4c8febfbd43500b72b1/Breakfast_nook.jpg
Wright liked designing rooms within rooms in his buildings, and Robie House is no different. This is a breakfast nook found at the east side of the house's open dining room.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe3d4ac1-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3bf526dbbcca3169214a66be3b2cdef5/Staircase_to_private_quarters.jpg
This is a look down on the staircase that leads up to the house's private quarters from the main level.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe3adfd3-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4749b5ac3dd7a6af0cc8c60953323546/Master_bedroom.jpg
This is the Robie House master bedroom.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe3870d5-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d670a25e25aaf7ac0503a0624b8d429a/Master_closet_entry.jpg
The master bedroom had a grand master closet that included built-in drawers, meaning that residents didn't have to have bulky chests of drawers.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe360fea-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d9973b085fb555a257ccc510c7b05914/Master_closet.jpg
The closet doors in the master bedroom were faithful to Wright's love of geometric patterns.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe33a03a-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a1f267965e2adfb7e4c8ea3c9b6b01e1/Courtyard_looking_west.jpg
A look to the west from the main Robie House courtyard.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe3140f8-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e6762d4e56a7651594784c413ee6cec5/Wall_safe.jpg
The master bedroom had a small safe built into the wall.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe2ed94d-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9fe1a7c467bb461533b2321296eadf8a/Shower.jpg
The master bedroom featured a very modern -- for the time -- shower with two heads.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe2c3eb8-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e2c589bdaf9a68098a6609c115ddc7ab/Robie_House_from_north.jpg
A look at Robie House from the north.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/06/fe29d41d-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/08db9463ab76dda9f8bb22e1e51e252f/Upstairs_bedroom.jpg
Some of the house's art glass is seen leaning against the wall in one of the upstairs bedroom. The glass is being repaired as part of the major renovation project.
 
[Date]2013-07-09  How the Wright brothers won the race to invent the airplane
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f6459452-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4982c1f72e1f0529151d432fc01dbce0/Wright_Flyer_610x405.jpg
The original Wright Flyer, with which the Wright brothers made the world's first airplane flight on December 17, 1903, is permanently housed at the Smithsonian's Air  Space Museum in Washington, D.C. The Dayton Aviation Heritage National Historic Park has a replica of the aircraft
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f64304b3-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0d47b021b5b704fe7f649aaf4b6d2a1c/Wright_Flyer_III.jpg
Although the original Wright Flyer gets most of the ink -- and why not, given that it made the first-ever manned, powered flight, which was captured in a perfect photograph -- some would say that the brothers' Wright Flyer III, which they used to perfect their airplane design, is more important in the annals of aviation.
Today, the plane, which on October 5, 1905, made a 24-mile, 39-minute flight -- more than all other flights made by anyone up to that time combined -- is permanently housed in the Wright brothers exhibit at the Carillon Historical Park Museum.
The main reason this plane may be more important to aviation history is that, because the Wrights used it to demonstrate that they'd solved most of the technical problems they'd been working on for years, they were now able to market the machine. And that meant, primarily, that they could seek out a contract with the U.S. Army. This formed the basis of the Wright Company, their airplane business, which made them rich, unlike their previous printing and bicycle businesses.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f6406275-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b9d9c9548e7613d2c6d0290864ef6c6b/Wright_Flyer_III_pilot.jpg
As with the Wright Flyer I, the pilot of the Wright Flyer III lay down horizontally at the front of the aircraft and flew alone. But before it would sign a deal with the brothers, the U.S. Army insisted that the airplane be able to take a passenger, and that both pilot and passenger sit upright.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f63db3ab-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e20b391ecdb444d161b5df946b43538f/Military_Flyer_diagram.jpg
Based on the Army's requirements, this was the Wrights' new design for their airplane, the Wright Military Flyer.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f63aff15-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/691752a53e047239af2fa6d0021413d2/First_flight_camera.jpg
To some, this camera may be one of the most iconic in history, given that it was used to photograph the Wrights' first flight at Kitty Hawk, N.C. The Wrights turned to local photographer John T. Daniels to document the flight. Daniels was fortunate to click the shutter with the Wright Flyer in the center of the frame, and with Wilbur Wright running alongside as his brother Orville flew for 120 feet at an airspeed of about 34 miles an hour.
The Wrights had set the camera up to point at the spot at which the plane would go aloft. It was in focus, and all Daniels had to do was click the shutter. But in the excitement of the moment, Daniels couldn't remember if he'd managed to do his job. Fortunately for history, he did.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f6384e01-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ac4ce9315c308a230d22398958e93bf3/1911_test_engine.jpg
This is a 35-horsepower test engine built for the Wrights in 1911 by their mechanic, Charles Taylor, who had built the 170-pound, 12-horsepower gas engine that the Wrights used to power the Wright Flyer I for the December 17, 1903, first flight.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f634f3b8-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/df69302ce1756631a54fd8426ed5f4a5/Wind_tunnel.jpg
Another one of the Wrights' innovations in 1901 was using a wind tunnel to figure out the effectiveness of wind foils of a number of shapes. They used the wind tunnel to confirm results they had made earlier including one testing a rotating airfoil they mounted on one of their own bicycles.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f6321071-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d7c931463bd87d9f9af7861fa540a9b4/Wind_tunnel_rudder.jpg
As part of their experiments, a wind foil like this one would be placed inside the wind tunnel and the Wrights would record the results.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f62f6ba3-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/34c29382b4a77a98d2ff950579387504/Bicycle_airfoil.jpg
Before using the wind tunnel, the Wrights had come up with an ingenious way to test various airfoils by mounting them on the handlebars of one of their bicycles and recording the results as they rode the bike.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f62ccc96-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d0eb61cdfa9fa21b14605218f4ea7a8f/Huffman_catalpult.jpg
Although the Wrights made their first flight in secrecy at Kitty Hawk, N.C., as they progressed in perfecting the airplane, they needed to be able to do much more frequent testing back home in Dayton as well.
As a result, they chose a cow pasture in a flood plain on the edge of town now known as the Huffman Prairie Flying Field.
There, the Wrights conducted countless tests of their various planes, and even set up a flying school there. It was here that the Wrights conducted their 39-minute flight that convinced them they were ready to patent their work and get their airplane business of the ground.
Although the Wrights had long experience with bicycles, they somehow hadn't originally come up with the idea of putting wheels on their planes. That meant that in order to get the planes in the air, they needed to catapult them. This is a replica of the catapult they installed at Huffman Prairie. The plane would be thrust down the rail and it would vault into the air when it hit the right speed.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f62a22b3-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/11f7465293bd61a8e76d277d225026fb/Huffman_hangar.jpg
This is a replica of the hangar the Wrights used to store their equipment at Huffman Prairie Flying Field.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f62729c2-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6bf82a58a028809ceff02acc88895586/Patent.jpg
While there were numerous challenges to their patent for what they called their "flying machine," the Wrights maintained their rights in large part because their patent attorney, Harry Toulmin, wrote a very broad application that included the concept of three-axis flying. Though the patent was granted, there were many who didn't accept the Wrights' claim to having invented the airplane, and even the Smithsonian Institute, which has been a big supporter of the Wright brothers story, didn't acknowledge their claim until 1942.
This is a replica of their original patent.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f6249b36-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/634d18e6ff5334a5992ebca11010a5c8/Wright_Cycle_sign.jpg
Before inventing the airplane, the Wright brothers had run two other businesses, one a print shop, and the other a bicycle-making company.
They sold two types of bikes, the Van Cleve, and the St. Claire.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f621e93b-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/cd84504705460414ca298abd01fb58de/Wright_Cycle.jpg
There are very few original Wright brothers bicycles left in existence. But the Dayton Aviation Heritage National Historic Park has one, this St. Claire.
The Wrights were likely able to take years to perfect their airplane design not because of the success of their bicycle business, but because their father had money and the brothers lived at home, meaning their costs were far lower than would have otherwise been the case.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f61f1cde-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f12fe6aa481097744b2acd9eb74f6025/Model_gliders.jpg
On display at the Dayton Aviation Heritage National Historic Park are these three models of the Wrights' early gliders.
The one of the bottom is a model of the Wrights' 1900 glider, which had a wing span of 17 feet. They made their first flight using this aircraft, which was made from wood and cotton sateen fabric. The glider did prove that their basic design was air-worthy, but they had difficulty getting it aloft with the weight of a pilot in normal winds. The glider weighed 190 pounds.
The middle aircraft is their 1901 glider, which had a wing span of 22 feet and weighed 98 pounds. They built this hoping that they had solved the lift problems with a forward elevator meant to control pitch. However, it was still difficult to control, despite the fact that the Wrights used this glider to break a number of distance records.
The 1902 glider, on top, had a wing span of 32 feet, 1 inch, and weighed 112 pounds. They installed a movable single rudder to handle yaw problems, and the glider became the first aircraft to be totally under the pilot's command, according to the Dayton Aviation Heritage National Historic Park. They made more than 250 glides with the aircraft, some nearly 600 feet.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f61c27f4-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/43cf3f46af35c84f06713bd307c21aa2/Broken_engine_part.jpg
This is a broken piece of the mounting foot from the Wright Flyer I. On December 17, 1903, they made the first-ever airplane flight at Kitty Hawk, and over the course of the day, they made three more flights, the longest traveling 852 feet. A gust of wind subsequently flipped the plane, heavily damaging it.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f619933d-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7a7b20271ba290fd379d7925e8e15913/Harpers_cover_NYC_flight.jpg
In order to gin up potential investors, the Wrights flew the Wright Flyer III around New York, which Harpers magazine photographed and put on its October 9, 1909, cover.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f616e4eb-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/46e3124bf90d15705ad39de23b410d27/Wright_Aviation_factory_buildings.jpg
The first two buildings on the left are the original Wright Company factory. Over the years, the buildings were used in auto parts manufacturing, and now, they are in the process of being decontaminated and added to the roster of locations included in the Dayton Aviation Heritage National Historic Park.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f61449cb-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/206c12269741e630f3427e7a253e66c6/Pontoon.jpg
In order to fly a Wright Model A up the Hudson River, Wilbur Wright lashed a canoe to the bottom of the plane. This is that canoe.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f610a44f-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5706fe6404656eb7131328edc80d1ca5/Wright_Flyer_replica_Dayton.jpg
Though the original Wright Flyer is in the Smithsonian's Air  Space Museum in Washington, D.C., the Dayton Aviation Heritage National Historic Park has a replica of the aircraft.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/08/f60dff7f-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6b7c7601ce9ed3b3c70f0724f211c574/Wright_house.jpg
With the riches they earned from their airplane company, the Wrights purchased this mansion atop a hill just outside Dayton.
 
 
 
 
[Date]2013-07-10  Building the U.S. Army's M1A2 Abrams tank
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f31a1243-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/d5f8a9ac8e4d2b05afcb71ad9815d3cb/Tanks_on_railcars_1.jpg
In 1996, the plant stopped making brand-new hulls and turrets for the U.S. Army's Abrams tanks, instead choosing to recondition older M1A2 systems that were still in working order.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f317794b-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/966fc2d1b933d0c7a402a712c8b5dc1f/Rustys_on_rails_2.jpg
Before the production of a reconditioned M1A2 Abrams tank can begin, the plant first receives rail cars full of decommissioned tanks, known as a "rusty."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f313090f-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1ba823340f686bf2d63938d4e95928a4/Rusty_and_shot-blasted_hulls_1.jpg
After the plant receives the "rusties," workers take the old hulls and turrets and put them through a shot-blasting process, stripping them of years of rust. On the left is a rusty, and on the right is a hull that has been shot-blasted.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f31030d2-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/af54e4048a7748a5e63024ec8c888306/Rusty_hull_1.jpg
A look at the interior of the hull of a "rusty" that was first built in the early 1990s.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f30d5ed0-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/273e38013eaf142cef0bb89f22fd0335/De-masked_hull_1.jpg
After the hulls and turrets have been shot-blasted and painted, they then go through the process of being de-masked. This hull has already gone through that step,  meaning it is now ready for the assembly line.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f30a7885-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6272dca9cb54b2f8edef5f157f91d0e0/Cannon_assembly_1.jpg
Once the turrets have been shot-blasted and de-masked, they have a set of so-called ammo doors added to them. After that, the cannons are mounted on them. But first, workers test the cannon recoil to ensure that the shock of firing it is properly absorbed inside the turret.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f3077afb-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ff3f7d72cda01c484845cfd58aa2e735/Completed_turret_lifted_up_1.jpg
After the cannon is installed on the turret, the turrets are moved to the assembly line for the addition of interior equipment and systems. CNET was not able to see this part of the assembly process due to its sensitive nature.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2fdc3b1-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4e8faa0ad44fec7c18086aecab3d73ed/Abrams_Turret.jpg
This is a completed turret coming off the assembly line. It will be set aside until later in the process, when it will be married with a completed hull.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2faf5ed-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a63ede998ec3a4d87f03c16c67b786be/The_basket_1.jpg
This is what's known as "the basket." Essentially, it is the pod inside of which three of the tank's crew members -- the gunner, the commander, and the loader, as well as 2,000 pounds of electronics and gear -- will sit. The basket is loaded up from underneath the turret and installed.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2f82e81-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/75d353f0c77ca1ab096a2d00ca3e655f/Station_3_1.jpg
This is Station 3, where an Abrams hull that has had many of its internal equipment and systems installed, is lifted off the ground in order to have its suspension components installed.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2f55ff1-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f9a4ee229d2a0bfb6d1fbf4bf97c3805/Road_wheels_1.jpg
These are what are known as "road wheels," which are mounted on the Abrams before the tank's track is installed.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2f296b4-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1afadf8b89386a98534b82cef894aaa5/Hull_assembly_line_1.jpg
This is the Abrams hull assembly line. Workers systematically install equipment at a series of stations along the line. A drag line pulls the hulls forward, since they don't have their suspension installed at this point.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2eebbc6-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/57c4018bde341e2f61232a537019dd27/Ammo_rack_1.jpg
Most of the tank's ammunition is stored in the turret, but some is kept in the hull, in ammunition racks like this one.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2ec1261-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3250d5e4e9b9b30f27335507d819bb7b/Fuel_cell_1.jpg
This is one of the Abrams tank's fuel cells, or gas tanks. The Abrams is not particularly fuel-efficient, getting 1.9 gallons to the mile.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2e95bc8-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1ee687f7d82a4be79861eed1d17246a9/Slip_ring_1.jpg
Installed at the interior base of the hull, this is the slip ring, a cylinder that allows the tank's turret to turn 360 degrees without its wires ending up tangled.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2e6d0a5-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/262fd34960c8fb6684f6ff9703406c5c/Fire_suppression_system_1.jpg
This is the tank's fire suppression system. If a fire breaks out inside, it is designed to automatically pour fire-resistant chemicals into the affected areas.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2e4027c-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/7d57eec7c78a7577ef967eb66a5377e0/Hulls_lined_up_1.jpg
A group of Abrams hulls are lined up as they move through the assembly process. At this point, they have their suspension system installed.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2e1536c-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4a54b59ca130bba09097304a5b0da50c/Resting_on_its_own_wheels_1.jpg
Early in the assembly process, the hulls rest on the ground as they don't have their suspension installed. But at this point, they have had their wheels mounted and can now rest on those wheels.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2de954b-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/008db01f33bec3ff5c70b73fa981571f/Systems_inside_hull_1.jpg
Midway down the assembly line, a look at some of the equipment and systems inside the hull's interior.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2dbd5f6-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/de4719284f70aa1fb554b985e3491eeb/Final_drives_1.jpg
Part of the tank's suspension system, these are known as final drives. Essentially, these are gears that transfer power from the transmission to the tank's track.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2d943ff-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/48cee784055f359c01d1bc95d06ed6d5/Sprocket_bolted_on_1.jpg
A large sprocket is bolted onto the final drives, and this is what moves the tank's track.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2d65f0a-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2f4c917b7b2fe93b56d5d3285c87476d/FUPP_1.jpg
This is the tank's full-up power pack, or FUPP. It includes the Abrams' engine, transmission, and all cooling apparatus -- the oil coolers and the exhaust. The turbine engine has 1,500 horsepower, and is multi-fuel, meaning that it can work on many different kinds of fuel, from diesel to jet fuel to gasoline to kerosene, and even peanut oil. That gives the tank a great deal of flexibility, which is a major advantage during combat or other activity. 
The FUPP is designed to be "plug and play," meaning that if it is damaged or dysfunctional, it is pulled out in its entirety and replaced with a new one.
On the battlefield, this is thought to be a more efficient way of repairing systems. In a case like this, the defective FUPP will usually be taken away to a maintenance area. In combat zones, replacement FUPPs are carried around by heavy trucks, or possibly delivered by aircraft.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2d39122-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8c820ddf5421604d99a4345d9feb143f/Rolled_up_tracks_1.jpg
Rolls of tank tracks sit on the floor alongside the Abrams assembly line.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2d0fe0a-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1aead75d4ff03052f598d8f2bec2ad7b/2_small_1.jpg
In order to install the tank's tracks, they are bolted down at one end, and rolled down on the floor. Then the drag line pulls the tank forward until it sits on top of the track.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2cd2dbf-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c124b47d6425208adcf7c927862e3a61/Track_about_to_be_joined_1.jpg
In order to complete the track installation, a pulley system with winches brings the two ends of the track together, where it is hooked together. Here, we see a track with two ends about to be joined.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2ca789a-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5963cc5742816db62fe3df597a8f4cbe/Track_joining_by_worker_1.jpg
A worker in the Lima plant kneels alongside an Abrams tank to make sure the track joining process goes smoothly.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2c7b95d-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ba0ba552da4064964e68e7cf186af016/Convertible_1.jpg
This is a "convertible," an Abrams hull that has made its way fully through the assembly process and is now awaiting being married to a completed turret.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2c51bad-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1507f7cbfee442fad77d2813767bc22c/1turretlifting_1.jpg
A completed turret is hoisted onto a completed hull. Afterward, workers will add armor and skirting and tighten the turret properly.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/09/f2c24d6b-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/431df3a366409c522f13e37b4dbb2fc0/Fire_control_systems_1.jpg
These are the tank's fire control systems, which are tested after the hull and turret are married.
 
[Date]2013-07-13   Making Goodyear's next-gen blimp
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e6588954-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0d15633bbbe92346eeddb9cd628c3b22/Skin_full_over_front.jpg
A firsthand look at how the new zeppelin is coming together.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e65584f3-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/433882164f3512018f0d01ecba86c5b1/Full_width_with_skin.jpg
Goodyear is building the new zeppelin -- which it will still market as a blimp -- inside its Wingfoot Lake airship hangar near Akron, Ohio. The company hopes to formally unveil the new airship later this year, and will begin making the second of three zeppelins in 2015. It should complete construction of the third by 2018. The three zeppelins will replace the blimps that Goodyear currently bases at Akron, Carson, Calif., and Pompano Beach, Fla.
Goodyear purchased the design, and the components for the new zeppelins from Zeppelin Luftschifftechnik, which is based in Friedrichshafen, Germany. But Goodyear is building the airships in its Akron hangar, and making several modifications to the standard zeppelin NT design.
Currently, construction of the first new blimp is well under way, with the ship's frame already put together and its skin slowly being stretched out over its full length.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e6528c67-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/260d194e7b80d8cc73ffc42add19d57d/Rear_frame_from_platform_close-up.jpg
A look at the rear end of the frame of the next-generation Goodyear blimp, which is a zeppelin NT designed by Zeppelin Luftschifftechnik (ZLT). The new airship is being built jointly by Goodyear and ZLT personnel inside Goodyear's 800-foot-long Wingfoot Lake hangar.
Unlike a traditional blimp, which is essentially just a giant inflated bag, a zeppelin has a rigid frame.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e64f9f90-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3fa5700dab7a693402571235e92f0891/Taking-off_550x367.jpg
Goodyear is replacing its familiar blimps with zeppelins like this one, seen in 2008 and flown by California's now-defunct Airship Ventures. The zeppelins are designed by Zeppelin Luftschifftechnik (ZLT), from Friedrichshafen, Germany, and feature three propellers instead of two, and a design that allows the airship to hover like a helicopter. That's one of the primary reasons Goodyear is switching airship platforms, given that being able to hover over a sporting event should allow event TV directors to order specific shots.
Compared with a traditional blimp (see next photo), a zeppelin is longer and has a slightly less oval silhouette.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e64cece3-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8b6812d4fe7c69b01dcabfc0cf091857/Blimp.jpg
The Spirit of Goodyear, one of the tire giant's existing three blimps, seen flying near the company's Wingfoot Lake hangar, outside Akron, Ohio. A blimp has a rounder, shorter silhouette than the zeppelins that the company will begin flying later this year.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e64a3cf3-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/73447af466b4d80858a361bdbda40277/Eureka_gondola.jpg
The gondola on a zeppelin -- as seen on Airship Ventures' Eureka -- is larger and more comfortable than that of Goodyear's traditional blimps. In addition to seating 12 people instead of 6, the zeppelin's gondola is also much quieter because the engines are not directly outside. That means passengers can talk normally and don't need to wear headsets to hear each other.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e6477b7d-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3ca8a162d90083fee738128511f98aa2/Blimp_gondola.jpg
The gondola found on Goodyear's traditional blimps is smaller and more cramped, and requires passengers to talk to each other using a headset, because the engines are directly outside.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e6443d4d-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/79d6d6972cce7bda61b20882b5784539/Skin_full_over_front_right_side.jpg
A look at Goodyear's new blimp, which is being built inside its Akron, Ohio hangar. The tire giant is switching from traditional blimps to zeppelins, which are longer, faster, quieter, and can hover over events instead of having to circle around them.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e6412b61-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fc7fe157b5d5f8bf09d8ff4f73bd389c/Straight_on_front.jpg
Though Goodyear's new zeppelins will be the world's largest airships, they're dwarfed by the size of the tire giant's hangar outside Akron, Ohio.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e63e0990-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8bb8304b1207f024a2dce341d8bd66cc/Rear_frame_from_platform_close-up_underneath.jpg
A look at the underside of the frame of the new Goodyear blimp, which is being built at the tire company's Akron, Ohio, airship hangar in conjunction with personnel from Germany's Zeppelin Luftschifftechnik, which designed it.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e63b07e9-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3d1f085d0c6777d70fa88ad1df744db1/Skin_held_aloft.jpg
Because the zeppelin has a rigid frame, it's built by first constructing the frame and then slowly stretching the skin over the top. By comparison, a blimp is made by starting with the skin on the ground and slowly inflating it.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e637ddd6-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c5edb64f76f263c5481030ce7ed290a5/Weights.jpg
These weights are being used to hold down the ropes that are holding up the skin as it is slowly and methodically pulled over the zeppelin's rigid frame.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e634e12e-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c8113f55428d86498efe84936aead382/Internal_frame.jpg
The zeppelin features these triangular trusses, made from lightweight composite materials, that help maintain its structure.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e6320058-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3a53c841997bcff9f8e3f4a488360cf5/Underside_skin.jpg
Though it appears that the yellow frame is the zeppelin's bottom, it is actually only its middle. The fabric seen underneath the frame will be pulled all the way to the airship's far (rear) end, and once fully enclosed, the zeppelin will be inflated using helium, which will raise the frame up well off the floor.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e62eb06d-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a35f8dfb6d6061cbd1cfd34ccd635a6b/Rear_frame_from_platform.jpg
The full Goodyear zeppelin construction, as seen from the rear from an elevated platform at the tire giant's Akron, Ohio, airship hangar.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e62bdca4-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2d84a2f5186e1d770af14a490d9874a1/Fins.jpg
The three fins for the new Goodyear blimp sit on the floor alongside the airship.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e6291714-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/1686f629c190ebdda54261f665c18f1b/Eureka_gondola_interior.jpg
The interior of a zeppelin's gondola is spacious, airy, and comfortable. This photograph was taken inside the gondola of the Eureka, which belonged to California-based Airship Ventures, which has gone out of business. The Eureka was dismantled and its components were sent back to Friedrichshafen, Germany, home of Zeppelin Luftschifftechnik, the company that made it, and which designed and is helping Goodyear construct its new zeppelins.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e6263f1e-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/dc62267356dabd3c4e21bdfc5dfc0a12/Loo-with-window_400x600.jpg
Perhaps the most important part of a zeppelin NT is its bathroom, which features a window from which to enjoy the view.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e623539d-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4081823a8dfdc3bb683697066228d8fc/Rear_of_zeppelin_with_propellers_610x405.jpg
Unlike a traditional Goodyear blimp, which has only two engines, mounted just outside the gondola, a zeppelin has three engines, two of which are on either side of the airship's center, and a third at its very rear. Because it has three engines, a zeppelin can hover like a helicopter.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e620af21-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/04f60b23c36f2961aecdf4955e504740/Side_rotor.jpg
Another advantage of a zeppelin is that its engines can rotate, allowing for much more agile movement.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e61db464-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/33c70bf9c67b93f0a3e34069366beafb/Zeppelin_in_hangar_on_mast_w_truck_610x405.jpg
A zeppelin, seen in the Friedrichshafen, Germany, hangar of  Zeppelin Luftschifftechnik, mounts to a mast that is attached to a long arm off a special truck.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/11/e61a60fa-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c6ce317889233c1dae0002bdcfa1f89f/Blimp_mast.jpg
A blimp's mast, by comparison, is a much larger, more cumbersome construction, as seen here, with the Spirit of Goodyear linked up to its pyramid-shaped mast at Goodyear's Akron, Ohio, airship operations headquarters.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2012/05/24/e616fab8-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/3ee75b481ada63e8fbebc26e12540847/Golden_Gate_Bridge_and_Airship_tail_1.jpg
Another advantage -- at least to the passengers -- of a zeppelin, is that its gondola has a large rear window, which allows those on board to see behind the airship. That's how CNET's Daniel Terdiman photographed the Golden Gate Bridge from the now-deconstructed zeppelin Eureka in 2012.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2012/05/11/e6136e52-f07a-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ada1c6739fb732910bf1aa7e1fe31c4b/Eclipse.jpg
The silhouette of a zeppelin reveals a much slimmer airship than a traditional Goodyear blimp.
 
[Date]2013-07-15  The Memphis Belle comes back to life
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7aa5f785-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/7fa0f31eee3d2ba4ffe256245bf102f5/Memphis_Belle_from_front.jpg
Now, the plane is being meticulously worked on by the experts in the museum's restoration shop, and CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman stopped by to check out the work during the trip. It is expected that sometime late next year or in early 2015, the plane will be moved inside the museum itself, where the general public will be able to see the final restoration process.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7abc024a-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/0bf0dffd37ea1274243a6c7fbc8cf5dc/Memphis_Belle_in_flight.jpg
In this Air Force photograph, dated June 9, 1943, the Memphis Belle is seen flying over England on its way back to the United States.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7ace34ff-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/3e987df176905a437f3e1b0811644650/Unloading_the_Memphis_Belle.jpg
After arriving at Wright-Patterson Air Force Base, home of the National Museum of the U.S. Air Force -- and its restoration program -- the Memphis Belle is carefully unloaded in the hangar where the restoration work is done.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7adf5e00-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/1824c417b8c896eac3da24919d466141/Memphis_Belle_from_behind.jpg
The Memphis Belle is seen from behind on July 10, 2013, in the restoration shop at the National Museum of the U.S. Air Force, at Wright-Patterson Air Force Base, outside Dayton, Ohio.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7af33a7f-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/c65e214d4e3f50653b12722554b6d53a/Memphis_Belle_from_front_with_right_wing.jpg
A look at the front (and the right wing) of the Memphis Belle inside the restoration shop.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7b08450f-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/3ec0ea0d846e74df2ff87130025252e4/Norden_bomb_sight.jpg
This is a Norden bombsight, mounted in the nose of the Memphis Belle.
According to the National Museum of the U.S. Air Force, "The Norden bombsight functioned as a part of a whole system. As the bomber approached its target, the bombardier entered data about wind direction, airspeed and altitude into the bombsight's analog computer, which calculated wind drift and provided the correct aim point. An internal gyroscope provided the stability necessary for using the telescopic sight at high altitudes. When connected to the Sperry C-1 Autopilot, the Norden bombsight provided unprecedented accuracy."
Although not original to the plane, this Norden bombsight is representative of what the B-17 flew with in World War II.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7b186463-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/6d97be9f8511aeb8d87c1bb6345346c0/Back_from_the_25th_mission.jpg
In this 1943 Air Force photograph, the crew of the Memphis Belle returned to England after completing the plane's 25th mission -- the accomplishment that gained it lasting fame in the annals of U.S. military aircraft.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7b289a6a-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/900ec0420d746f9b961971cb07d0bdf0/Nose_art.jpg
In its days barnstorming around the U.S. on a war bond tour, the nose art of the Memphis Belle added the plane's navigators name, as well as eight swastikas representing the Nazi planes it was said to have shot down. But those elements -- unlike the single swastika under the window -- were added after the bomber left Europe.
As part of the restoration, the nose art will be returned to its state when it was flying bombing missions.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7b384223-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/cae27182f7fbbd6f52908786bd2836f2/Memphis_Belle_crew.jpg
In this Air Force photo, the crew of the Memphis Belle pose alongside their famous plane. Though the photo shows nose art featuring a series of swastikas, the National Museum of the Air Force's restoration supervisor, Greg Hessler, said that that art was added only once the plane was in the U.S. and was on its war bond tour.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7b47ef1f-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/745168f0eebacdc45e2c1d52ef4a8a73/Right_wing_engines.jpg
The Memphis Belle flew with four engines, like these two mounted on the plane's right wing. These are not its original engines, but they are authentic.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7b5a49cb-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/0ae6706752a3f5a33b07a9ad1778d485/Propellers.jpg
These are the four propellers that will be mounted on the plane's four engines once the restoration project is done. They are authentic but not original to the plane.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7b6ba665-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/3e610be10e7f07fcef140c507cf83af0/Etchings.jpg
Everywhere the Memphis Belle went on its war bond tour, people would scratch their names into the rear of its fuselage. Today, many of those etched names are still visible, but when the restoration is complete, and the plane is painted as it was when it was still flying bombing missions, the names will be painted over.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7b7b4a18-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/c2364ece22e140719284531539a68940/Lt_V_McMahon.jpg
One person to etch his name on the side of the Memphis Belle was Lt. V. McMahon, of New York.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7b8bd2ab-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/146a5342cd2ec457d3819cd3c23b1ce2/Tail_gunner_position.jpg
This is the tail gunner's position, which was mounted at the very rear of the Memphis Belle. A gunner sat there with two .50-caliber machine guns.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7b9c6344-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/78ba1ffce56551fbeb2c5abf06981b23/Tail_gunner_position_from_behind.jpg
The tail gunner position is seen from behind.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7bb6c8c1-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/2870e71fd1c5015875f3a6717acb204f/Ball_turret_from_side.jpg
This is a ball turret, which would have been mounted underneath a B-17 bomber like the Memphis Belle. A gunner jammed himself into the turret and fired two .50-caliber machine guns.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7bc8d66e-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/54bca4e889065e2fb96d2243a626a813/Ball_turret_drawing.jpg
This drawing depicts the gunner inside the ball turret. It's clear that this was not one of the most comfortable jobs in the Air Force.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7bd8f107-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/9c26b1b25c11766840ec673845a41ef4/Ball_turret_inside.jpg
A look inside the ball turret.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7be934b5-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/f6c15a7efc35a1558951d67db5749649/Machine_gun.jpg
A .50-caliber machine gun, like those used aboard the Memphis Belle.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7c02361a-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/6cdda5323e248dbed435214584961c0a/Top_turret.jpg
This is the top turret, which was mounted behind the plane's cockpit. A gunner sat in the top turret and fired two .50-caliber machine guns.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7c11f255-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/aa745b893c64efdd1c8c5a7025b53dbd/Bullet_hole_repaired.jpg
One of the Memphis Belle's bullet holes is covered up by a small piece of metal.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7c21b8f4-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/be3c9769726d3031422b281a42f0865e/Belly.jpg
A look at the belly of the Memphis Belle. The restoration crew is working to make the plane look as authentic as possible, and the process is likely to end up taking eight years or so to complete.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/14/7c3182b7-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/c338c833ce85565406d23235f7d96289/Heat_exchanger_in_wing.jpg
Though no one will ever see this heat exchanger, because it will be closed up inside the Memphis Belle's wing, the restoration crews are bringing the plane's systems back to their original condition -- minus its ability to fly.
 
[Date]2013-07-16 Fixing the smake unit Lionel
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/709a1955-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/29562bae297a4ee0bef5c2199a28754d/Working_on_smoke_unit.jpg
One of the most common repairs is fixing the smoke units on Lionel locomotives, which can easily get damaged due to leaking oil.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/70b0de8b-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/c8d9a7eb41d0989fc92f0d8c998ee411/Pulling_apart_kits_for_parts.jpg
Between 40 percent and 50 percent of all repair work done at the facility is due to some sort of failure by the operators of the trains. Often, owners will demand returns on trains with minor chips, like on the corner of this shell.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/70d43409-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/ca9c9616eca196fec5c882f67284276c/Bins_and_bins.jpg
The Canfield repair facility has about 120,000 parts in its inventory, meaning that it can replace almost any part on any Lionel train made from the mid-1990s and later.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/70e401dc-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/ad1b6c7c74fa0da273a93e4173ca7797/Parts_from_kits.jpg
A worker piles up parts he's gotten from tearing down returned subway cars.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/70f64a87-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/0a015f377aaeb879178dc93dfb95f4d7/Plastic_trucks_grid.jpg
Surprisingly, the Lionel company didn't have a complete register of all the trucks it used in its many sets, leading it to sometimes make brand-new trucks that duplicated ones that already existed.
One of the special projects the Canfield facility has been working on is creating this physical record of every rail truck used on a Lionel train so that the manufacturer can avoid creating new duplicate parts.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/711125a7-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/c2c51ada370baea22838fb06f1214b4b/Locomotive_taken_apart.jpg
Sitting on a repair technician's desk, this locomotive has been taken apart in order to work on its wiring.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/71209ac2-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/41ad912ae208b293883e044d2fc7ae90/Test_track.jpg
When technicians finish a repair, they will usually put the now-fixed product -- be it a locomotive, or a car -- on the testing track and run it around the track for a couple of hours to be sure everything is working properly.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/71305c0a-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/75546c29acde67ddda9a6fd25851b528/Collecting_for_refurb_locomotives.jpg
The repair facility ends up with many locomotive chassis that come from returns. They then hold on to the chassis and use them to build refurbished models, which they sell at a discount.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/71416e89-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/39a4f5f901b6548bf2109a4076f78136/Shelves_of_refurb_locomotive_bodies.jpg
Shelves full of locomotive chassis await being put together for refurb models.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/7151d830-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/cea05a088e8df9d8260fb10bd74f7498/Inspecting_coaling_tower.jpg
A technician in the Canfield repair facility works on fixing a coaling tower. He concluded it had been dropped due to a small separation in the plastic. Often, what could take a customer quite some time to fix -- or be impossible for them to do -- can take a technician just minutes.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/7165030e-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/ef87c2ecb0a6fc18e9cac15b3044d70e/Testing_the_smoke_unit.jpg
After fixing the smoke unit on a locomotive, a technician watches it go around the testing track to make sure that it's working properly.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/7174b54c-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/34f494bd0ea9b1e81ce431af9a8a87d9/Testing_the_smoke_unit_up_close.jpg
A closer look at the locomotive with the repaired smoke unit as it rounds the testing unit.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/71870496-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/c7fdbbfec986f9f7483d9c2c45f00311/Shelves_of_shells.jpg
The facility has shelves full of train shells that have been pulled off extensive repairs. The shells are then kept and paired with chassis that need only simple repairs. Often, Lionel can offer someone a shell like this at a much cheaper price than they would pay for a brand-new one.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/7197df77-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/77f58f8cae2f0f9f375c2899b71a2524/Shelves_of_shells_close-up.jpg
A close-up look at some of the shells the facility keeps for future use.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/71b5257c-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/a84482e4a0392abf0ee0bd9628e58d66/Paints.jpg
Just about any paint color that might be needed is kept on hand at the repair facility.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/71c792b5-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/1f0f67985fba100703e5507358330917/Tugboat_sound_board.jpg
One of the 120,000 parts in the facility's inventory is this one, a tugboat sound board.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/71d9254c-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/b7c977fa80a62af912cc49b3a50925b1/Slow_season.jpg
Summer is the facility's slow season, since many train enthusiasts are doing other things. If it were high season, these shelves would be full of what are known as "major" projects, which are repairs on high-end, expensive locomotives. Lionel promises that it will take no more than 21 days to complete such repairs, but in practice it usually never takes more than six.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/71ea13e3-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/6d2412f8493ace2bc5394c9f11478087/RC_bases_in_box.jpg
A box of 990 Legacy Command Set bases, which hold Lionel's $400 remote controllers.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/71fa9545-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/a7ab761abceeefd53617342f62d60fc3/Bins_of_small_parts.jpg
Hundreds of small bins hold many of the smaller parts.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/720a9ea5-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/3af82bd0ca79b7c6a32125dc036c9c27/Bin_of_bells.jpg
This bin is full of small bells.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/15/72217a2c-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/9c00abf0807dd917dac119de9de3da45/Bin_of_motors.jpg
Another bin holds motors for the locomotives.
 
 
[Date]2013-07-18   With Superhero museum, who needs Comic-Con
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/5959cceb-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/d7988684bd1f685ea3852c05521dc8e5/Batman_suit.jpg
The suit was a gift to the museum this year from a man who had purchased it at auction years ago and who was liquidating some of his collection.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/596d42d9-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/3a916740f22082a319ac6d8b7fa2ac41/Hall_of_Heroes.jpg
Opened six years ago, the museum was designed to look like the Hall of Justice from the Superfriends. Much of the collection belongs to Stewart, whose interest in superheroes dates back to the mid-1970s.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/597d2f75-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/a8e2b96d8ebc5a3c3ab6565fde906c4a/Amazing_Fantasy_No_15_-_1st_Spider_Man.jpg
Stewart says that the museum holds 55,000 superhero comic books, including every Marvel book ever produced and every DC book since 1956. So while the museum doesn't yet have an Action Comics No. 1 (the first appearance of Superman), it does have an Amazing Fantasy No. 15, which was the first appearance of Spider-Man.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/598ea73a-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/770cc842d1fe10ca9a7ae270df0ccbac/Spiderman_Twin_towers_poster.jpg
Another rare treasure in the museum is this Spider-Man poster, which depicts the webbed hero and the Twin Towers in New York. The poster was for 2002's "Spider-Man 2" but was recalled after the terrorist attacks of September 11, 2001.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/59a4c774-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/4cfa7c5378c9370d99c6718717a38391/Captain_America_No_1.jpg
A personal favorite of Stewart's -- which is also quite valuable -- is the museum's copy of Captain America No. 1, in which the superhero is introduced on the cover smashing Adolf Hitler in the face. The book is part of the museum's "golden age" section, which showcases superheroes through comics and other collectibles between 1938 and 1955.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/59b51d93-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/c03f248bb9cf8b31ab1b8a68d160aaea/Batman_No_15_with_gun.jpg
Though Batman is famously anti-gun, that wasn't always the case, as this Batman No. 15 illustrates.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/59c53ce1-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/40b0937dccc029eb41b2455eb8472038/First_Superman_toy.jpg
This is the world's first Superman toy, Stewart said, from 1940. It's a wind-up toy, and when wound, Superman picks up the airplane.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/59d4c585-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/e75872b07c91dd9149de981ccb3ad9ae/One_wall_of_comics.jpg
With 55,000 comic books in its collection, the museum devotes a great deal of space to housing the books, which are presented in alphabetical order. This is just one wall of four of dozens upon dozens of boxes of superhero comic books.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/59e43948-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/1b1a086d8712ec606992d0e54dd0cdb8/Superman_section.jpg
The museum devotes special sections to a few famous superheroes, like this one for Superman.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/59f457f8-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/ef234582e20086b5841e1030f88268f3/Spiderman_section.jpg
There's also, of course, a Spider-Man section.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/5a195252-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/c594e8be8b4bd5dcecab3b2597dfccc0/Wolverine_exhibit.jpg
The museum usually has a special section set up to celebrate something in the superhero universe. Right now, it's a Wolverine section set up because of the forthcoming "Wolverine" movie.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/5a29e038-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/a7d75fb4b36ae3e899199c905f442e02/Comics_boxes.jpg
The collection features countless boxes like this one, which all together hold 55,000 superhero comic books, including every book from a major comic book publisher since 1956, Stewart said.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/5a3d91f4-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/83f499bef2783e443003a1474bc0ca95/Hollywood_heroes_wall.jpg
Another special section depicts superheroes in Hollywood.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/5a4e9469-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/e2e32b8244fb10a449f5925e16251016/Greatest_American_Hero.jpg
Though not as valuable as the original Batman suit, this is an original Greatest American Hero suit from the 1980s TV show of that name.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/5a5e2e99-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/aec7baf323e82d540db7e01975e38768/Batman_pedalcar.jpg
This is a Batman pedalcar from England. Stewart said it is the only one of its kind in the United States.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/5a6dd91b-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/ab825923ccb0b534dbe763c5ef344649/XMen_No_1.jpg
Another important comic book from the collection is this copy of X-Men No. 1.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/5a7d61da-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/609449c399689200c136a6098532b809/Hall_of_Justice.jpg
Tucked away on a shelf is this Hall of Justice toy set from the Superfriends show. The museum's facade was designed to look like this building.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/5a8cfdee-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/ac9986de0bb17ede79f8bfca1b0069bd/Green_Lantern_section.jpg
Another superhero with a designated section is the Green Lantern.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/5a9db341-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/f3a03640d7ba0a2593fe646708c30335/Hot_Wheels_Invisible_Jet.jpg
One year, Hot Wheels pulled an April Fool's Joke by issuing a press release that it was releasing a Wonder Woman Invisible Jet toy. Later, it put out a small number of the toys.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/17/5aadce3c-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/525b660a412219067c03bd5a2bb55fe1/Captain_America_shield.jpg
This is Stewart's Captain America shield. Every year, he dresses up as Captain America in Elkhart's Memorial Day parade, and said that he is usually swarmed by World War II veterans who were often given copies of that hero's comics in their war-time care packages.
 
 
[Date]2013-07-22 A century of the best of the U.S. Air Force
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/1f982eb0-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/a5d24735a128a90da68c95973be37dd2/B-2_from_side.jpg
"With a crew of only two -- the pilot in the left seat and the mission commander in the right -- a typical combat mission consisted of a non-stop flight from Whiteman Air Force Base to the target and back. During these missions, normally lasting more than 30 hours and requiring numerous aerial refuelings, each B-2 delivered up to 40,000 pounds of precision weapons."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/1fab91d2-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/04673215ea19581e239fa1cc0dc0ab50/B-29_Bockscar.jpg
This is Bockscar, the B-29 Superfortress that dropped Fat Man -- the second atomic bomb ever used in warfare -- on Nagasaki, Japan, on August 9, 1945, effectively ending World War II. The Smithsonian's Air  Space Museum in Washington, D.C. has Enola Gay, which dropped Little Boy on Hiroshima three days earlier.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/1fc207fd-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/6310b0ed61c6fe5c13acf0246152b4fe/SR-71_Blackbird_from_SAC_museum.jpg
This is an SR-71 Blackbird, seen at the Strategic Air Museum in Ashland, Neb. The National Museum of the U.S. Air Force also has a Blackbird, of which there were 32 built. According to the Air Force museum, the SR-71 was "a long-range, advanced, strategic reconnaissance aircraft developed from the Lockheed A-12 and YF-12A aircraft. The first flight of an SR-71 took place on December 22, 1964, and the first SR-71 to enter service was delivered to the 4200th (later 9th) Strategic Reconnaissance Wing at Beale Air Force Base, Calif., in January 1966.
"The U.S. Air Force retired its fleet of SR-71s on Jan. 26, 1990, because of a decreasing defense budget and high costs of operation.
"Throughout its nearly 24-year career, the SR-71 remained the world's fastest and highest-flying operational aircraft. From 80,000 feet, it could survey 100,000 square miles of Earth's surface per hour. On July 28, 1976, an SR-71 set two world records for its class -- an absolute speed record of 2,193.167 mph and an absolute altitude record of 85,068.997 feet."
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/1fd20803-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/eb59b0db48ae7f543ef26dc6a82ff746/Observation_balloon.jpg
This Caquot Type R observation balloon from World War I, allowed American military personnel to see up to 40 miles behind enemy lines in a bid to see troop movements or other activity. It was a hydrogen-filled balloon with a gas capacity of 32,200 cubic feet, a length of 92 feet, and a diameter of 32 feet.
It could "lift two passengers in its basket, along with charting and communications equipment," according to the Air Force museum, "plus the weight of its mooring cable, to a height of [up to] 4,000 feet....During WWI, American balloon observers directed artillery fire and targets such as troop concentrations and supply dumps. They noted more than 11,000 enemy airplane sightings, 1,000 instances of military traffic on railroads and roads, and 400 artillery batteries."
A great deal of the Caquot balloons were produced for WWI.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/1fe1d706-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/3566e80109605206703a562f09ebdd9c/Wright_Flyer.jpg
The airplane became "the first military heavier-than-air flying machine," according to the museum. "Upon purchase by the Signal Corps for $30,000 on Aug. 2, 1909, the U.S. Army designated the Wright 1909 Military Flyer as Signal Corps Airplane No. 1, and it remained the only Army airplane for nearly two years."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/1ff19539-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/4cfe037e3d20f5e199b4dd42508e3442/Wind_tunnel.jpg
This 1918 wind tunnel was built at McCook Field in Dayton, Ohio. "Using a 24-blade fan of 60 inches diameter," according to the Air Force museum, "the tunnel developed a maximum air speed of 453 miles per hour at its 14-inch diameter choke-throat test area.
"It was used for calibrating airspeed instruments and testing airfoils. The item to be evaluated was placed in the choke-throat and viewed through the glass door. During operation, the air was drawn into the small end of the tunnel and exhausted from the large end where the fan was located."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/2001bc06-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/337c398f36ed3be026a1c57e1e34ce0d/Spad_XIII.jpg
With the Germans threatening to win World War I on the strength of their new fighter planes, the American military turned to the French Spad XIII, a small plane with a 220-horsepower engine that, according to the Air Force museum, had a top speed of 135 miles an hour, about ten miles an hour faster than the German fighters. "It carried two .303-cal. Vickers machine guns mounted above the engine," according to the museum. "Each gun had 400 rounds of ammunition, and the pilot could fire the guns separately or together. Technical problems hampered production until late 1917, but nine different companies built a total of 8,472 Spad XIIIs by the time production ceased in 1919.
"Since the United States entered World War I without a combat-ready fighter of its own, the U.S. Army Air Service obtained fighters built by the Allies. After the Nieuport 28 proved unsuitable, the Air Service adopted the Spad XIII as its primary fighter. By the war's end, the Air Service had accepted 893 Spad XIIIs from the French, and these aircraft equipped 15 of the 16 American fighter squadrons."
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/2012206a-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/6b70b2045cee232321f4450febd108e1/Sopwith_Camel.jpg
This is the famous British Sopwith F-1 Camel, which was used to shoot down more enemy planes than any other World War I fighter. The plane went into action for the first time in June 1917, with the British Royal Flying Corps and Royal Naval Air Service, and two U.S. Army Air Service squadrons also flew the Camel in combat, according to the Air Force museum.
The plane was also used, according to legend, to shoot down the ace pilot Red Baron.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/202434e3-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/93cdae03fe83cc2a63fa824cd0355aeb/Bug.jpg
This is the Kettering Aerial Torpedo, otherwise known as the "Bug," the earliest-ever cruise missile.
According to the museum, it was "launched from a four-wheeled dolly that ran down a portable track [and its] system of internal pre-set pneumatic and electrical controls stabilized and guided it toward a target. After a predetermined length of time, a control closed an electrical circuit, which shut off the engine. Then, the wings were released, causing the Bug to plunge to earth -- where its 180 pounds of explosive detonated on impact."
However, although the Dayton-Wright Airplane Company (founded by the Wright Brothers) made nearly 50 of these weapons, they never saw combat. This is a replica.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/203522bc-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/0cebfa4900e098c1a855b31a87b862f5/MB-2.jpg
The Martin MB-2 "was the first U.S.-designed bomber produced in large numbers," according to the museum. It was ordered in 1920 and replaced British- and Italian-made bombers made in the U.S. under license during the first World War.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/20458b6c-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/a886d70f791e02ee37a9a07a3b5bde17/P-26.jpg
This is a replica of a Boeing P-26A Peashooter, the American military's first-ever monoplane. The planes first flew in March 1932. Eventually, the Air Corps ordered 111 of the Peashooters.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/2055f442-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/feed64d45c910589df9cbdce4f5d9606/B-10.jpg
The Martin B-10 was known to be America's best fighter plane. It was a big leap forward for the military because it had a retractable landing gear and variable pitch propellers. It also had enclosed gun turrets, an enclosed bomb bay, and was faster than any production fighter in the world. This is the last B-10 in the world.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/2067440c-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/c79d47219a84860418d92b1bc700aa72/P-36.jpg
The Curtiss P-36A "Hawk" "was first procured for the Air Corps in 1938," according to the museum. The Army Air Corps purchased a total of 243 P-36s, but by the time World War II began, the plane was outmoded, and was eventually used mainly for training and courier duties at home. This is the Air Corps' first-ever P-36A.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/2076f29e-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/a1fbad49d751ded63a99aeb7a906a529/Doolittle_Raiders_goblets.jpg
"In January, 1942, Gen. Henry 'Hap' Arnold selected Lt. Col. James Doolittle to lead Special Aviation Project No. 1, the bombing of Japan," according to the Air Force Museum. "Although the Doolittle Raid of April 18, 1942, caused only minor damage, it forced the Japanese to recall combat forces for home defense, raised fears among the Japanese civilians, and boosted morale among Americans and our Allies abroad."
In December 1946, Doolittle and the other Raiders met to celebrate his birthday, in the process starting an annual tradition. These 80 silver goblets were presented to the Raiders in 1959 by the city of Tucson, Ariz., each representing one of the men. At the reunions, the surviving men turn their deceased comrades' goblets over, and as of July 2013, there are just three remaining unturned goblets. When only two remain, the final two Raiders will drink a last toast.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/2089ea75-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/792c3bbb9ea93ff6682de76780449f12/Doolittle_B-25.jpg
This is a rebuilt RB-25D, the plane that Lt. Doolittle flew in his raid on the Japanese.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/2099f385-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/bc2e284b24e6863b2437fb7ed3652560/Beaufighter_MK1C.jpg
When the Americans formed their first radar-equipped night fighter squadron in January 1943, they did not have an effective night fighter, according to the museum, so they relied instead on the British Bristol Beaufighter until a plane could be produced at home.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/20a98fc4-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/67d84775c864c73258013d49175d6ec6/B-24.jpg
This is a B-24 Liberator, the most-produced military bomber in history. At its peak, a B-24 came off the assembly line every 56 minutes, and in total 18,000 were built. It was a mainstay for the American in World World II, which used the plane in every combat theater.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/20c01d87-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/cfb5e1a154506d356106a4f1100cb927/BF-109_German.jpg
This a German World War II-era BF-109, which, with 36,000 built, was the most-produced fighter of all time.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/20d07d34-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/20ba17ab991b7fb1506f05c8c5315712/B-17_Flying_Fortress.jpg
The B-17 Flying Fortress, from World War II, "is one of the most famous airplanes ever built," according to the Air Force museum. It first flew in July 1935 and eventually flew in every World War II combat zone. "It is best known for the daylight strategic bombing of German industrial targets." All told, 12,726 of the planes were built.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/20e0be6f-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/7d0323bd3db98c1c4e7eaab4b5d23f41/ME-262A_German.jpg
This is the German ME-262A, the first operational jet fighter in world history. However, though it could fly very fast, its engines were temperamental and it was slow to accelerate, so the Allies would simply fly large numbers of fighters over German airfields and attack the planes on takeoff. The ME-262A was not destined to help the Germans win the war.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/20f9ed54-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/c074ae1415c954c7cf7a806569946c61/Fat_Man_bomb.jpg
This is an actual Fat Man atomic bomb, found in the National Museum of the United States Air Force. The American military produced several Fat Mans, although just one was dropped, on Nagasaki, Japan, on August 9, 1945.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/210a3bb0-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/ba18b5eb4ca30213a711b06802460e6a/B-29_Bockscar_from_front.jpg
This is Bockscar, the B-29 Superfortress that dropped Fat Man on Nagasaki, as seen from the front.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/2119d7c0-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/21937837593606866fc5f5506ae59227/F-86A_Sabre.jpg
This is an F-86A Sabre, which was the U.S. Air Force's first swept-wing jet fighter. It made its initial flight in October 1947, and the first production model flew in May, 1948, according to the Air Force museum. In September of that year, it set a world speed record of 670.9 miles an hour.
The Sabre was a day fighter and was used in the Korean War, battling Russian-made MiG-15s, eventually shooting down 792 MiGs with a kill ratio of 8:1, according to the museum.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/21299b63-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/e8542c2b63444a3c8f55a20a0964b852/B-52.jpg
This is a B-52 Stratofortress. According to the Air Force museum, the B-52 became operational in 1955 and for years was the Air Force's main long-range heavy bomber, especially during the Cold War. Indeed, the plane is still operational today with some potentially flying until 2040. Nearly 750 were built, with production ending in 1962. A B-52 dropped the first airborne hydrogen bomb on May 21, 1956, over Bikini Atoll, and in 1965, the planes entered combat in Southeast Asia. "By August 1973, they had flown 126,615 combat sorties with 17 B-52s lost to enemy action," according to the museum.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/213ac907-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/b166988f8b1e0a7e78dff86286b21bb4/UC-123_Ranch_hand_Patches.jpg
This is "Patches," a UC-123 Ranch Hand. The planes were used in Southeast Asia, and according to the museum, Ranch Hand crews denied the enemy the ability to ambush vehicles and boats in the jungle by spraying herbicides and defoliating the jungle. "To accomplish the mission, Ranch Hand crews flew [the planes] on straight runs at very low altitude over a well-armed enemy," according to the museum, and the planes were hit regularly, leading to a reputation as the most shot-at Air Force plane in Southeast Asia.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/215c368a-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/82c145ee6b723cded7adbbbf4a26e9a4/UC-123_Ranch_hand_Patches_nose_art.jpg
This plane, known as "Patches," was shot almost 600 times, as its nose art suggests.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/216cfdc5-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/2ab4fce108cd94aa14a9bdd0479fb2a9/F-111.jpg
The F-111A was developed to meet the Air Force's need for a new air superiority fighter. The plane had variable-geometry wings, which could be swept forward on takeoff or landing, or during slow-speed flight, according to the Air Force museum.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/217c8901-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/5808395705e5136868a46b2c1759bae2/B-36J_Peacemaker.jpg
The B-36J was known as the Peacemaker. It was designed during World War II, and the plane went operational with Strategic Air Command in 1948. Some of the planes "served as photographic reconnaissance aircraft, and others were adapted to launch and retrieve specially modified RF-84F/K reconnaissance planes."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/218d5ce2-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/21a297910621db982660a82f7b0ac90d/RF-84K_Thunderflash.jpg
