This is an RF-84K Thunderflash, which would have been dropped from a B-36J. The Thunderflash was a reconnaissance and nuclear strike fighter. It had a short range and could not be fueled in mid-air, so being dropped from a B-36J was a way to extend the plane's range, according to the museum.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/219debc2-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/1368375168347b8d3c59933ea7421afd/F-84_Thunderstreak.jpg
The F-84F Thunderstreak saw duty during the Berlin crisis in 1961, the year that saw construction begin on the Berlin Wall, with 200 of the planes involved in that pivotal Cold War event.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/21ae000d-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/02f963a1fe684721d69d076cbb70b177/B-2_from_front.jpg
The Air Force museum's B-2 bomber, as seen from the front.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/21be1132-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/73bcdd0e6400703e346566a98ec1bb68/B-1B.jpg
This is a B-1B Lancer, a multi-role, heavy bomber that first flew in 1984. "The B-1B's blended wing body configuration, variable-geometry design, and turbofan engines combined to provide greater range and high-speed (Mach 1.2) at sea level," according to the museum. "The B1-B employed forward-looking radar and terrain-following radar. it also had an indicator for moving ground targets. Its extremely accurate [GPS], inertial navigation system, Doppler radar, and radar altimeter enabled aircrews to navigate accurately around the world without ground-based navigation aids." The plane was used in combat in 1998, and was also used in Kosovo and Afghanistan, and in 2002, the Air Force began cutting the number of B-1Bs as a cost-saving measure.
 
 
[Date]2013-07-23 from Doomsday plane to
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/179b0f89-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/4158c3b45bbbf8840601de0b86fec582/Alert_plane.jpg
OFFUTT AIR FORCE BASE, Neb
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/17af3776-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/e448252661028f94aef4e7cdd015d3fb/Three_out_of_four.jpg
Though there are four NAOC planes in the military's fleet, all four are almost never operational at one time. Usually, at least one of the planes is being worked on. But even having three together at once, as seen here, is a somewhat rare event.
The E-4B in the foreground is the one that, when this photograph was taken, is known as the "alert" plane. That means its crew is on a week-long alert and ready to go airborne within minutes, should such a situation arise. Which plane is on alert depends on many factors, among the most important of which is the maintenance schedule.
The planes are also sometimes used to flying the U.S. secretary of defense on international trips.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/17c0ef2d-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/1eca9a4683ce5a4f44860935d8967132/Welcome_aboard.jpg
Anyone going aboard one of the Doomsday planes is greeted with a sign belying the plane's sombre raison d'etre.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/17d0d6e5-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/1e8ebf2b0ca124963fa69c05111d8dd7/Communications_chart.jpg
The NOAC planes are similar to the president's Air Force One but actually have a more sophisticated communications package, including a few more antennae. This chart shows the full range of communications that are possible on board the plane. Essentially, it is outfitted to communicate across the entire range of electromagnetic capabilities in order to ensure that the command and control leadership are able to talk to their forces, regardless of the situation.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/17e444ac-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/cd2f905cada849640ae83abd9a8f1e30/Nose.jpg
The nose of the E-4B.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/17f5fe90-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/499e85280021a1f75c20d1a8b442d9d7/Hump.jpg
This hump, on top of the plane's fuselage, gives those on board the ability to communicate via super high frequency and Milstar systems. According to the Air Force,  Milstar  "is a joint service satellite communications system that provides secure, jam resistant, worldwide communications to meet essential wartime requirements for high priority military users. The multi-satellite constellation links command authorities with a wide variety of resources, including ships, submarines, aircraft and ground stations."
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/1806e674-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/528101d93662e04954b6c53e1551d9dd/Cross-section.jpg
This chart shows the various sections and compartments aboard the Doomsday planes.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/181734a4-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/b49a05c018c102fb96d69a5df7c2f123/Battle_staff_room_through_window.jpg
This is the plane's battle staff room -- seen through a window, as CNET was not allowed to takes photographs while inside the room. When the plane is on alert, the room would be full with officers from each of the United States' military services. The goal is to staff the room with officers who understand the country's big-picture strategic situation, as well as national, state, and local infrastructure, including power grids.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/1828e7e8-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/3384793012f7bc2cfc9495a30cedb414/Briefing_room.jpg
Because the plane sometimes carries the secretary of defense or other senior military leaders, it has a briefing room for traveling press.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/1838e4cb-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/396b4d41e18e27d35cb46e02f4703b64/Radio_compartment_-_Secure_side.jpg
The plane has a compartment dedicated to processing radio communications. This is the console for processing secure communications.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/1848de2a-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/caf3f52daefc79b31450f6f08c8bfda5/Phone.jpg
The planes have many phones like this one, although at least one of the planes is having its phones upgraded.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/185a93b6-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/66a268dc8549edb923bc3b4e9cee5924/Radio_compartment_-_Clear_side.jpg
This is the other side of the radio compartment. It handles non-secure communications from the ground or other aircraft.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/186c0b83-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/4161019a3effc88902bc82f39010f080/Power_and_cooling_console.jpg
In the plane's technical room, one console is dedicated to handling the plane's on-board power and cooling functions.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/187da8ac-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/dcf86c37e69f79f039aa8cce886752b9/Satellite_line_console.jpg
Also in the technical room, this console is dedicated to routing communications that come in through satellite to the appropriate phone or radio on board the plane.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/18976886-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/9315da6fb568401d180b4a315d0de515/Air-to-ground_line_console.jpg
This console is dedicated to routing air-to-ground line communications.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/18aa58f4-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/9faf1342ddb37e33112fbe36b15880dc/VLF_antenna_monitoring_station.jpg
Among the plane's many communications tools is a very-low frequency antenna that can be trailed out the back of the aircraft. Depending on need, the antenna can be as much as five miles long. Technicians can monitor the antenna from this station, and see it physically through the white periscope that is on the left side of the station. It is vital that the technicians monitor the antenna when it is outside the plane because it is fragile and can be damaged or destroyed in rough weather or turbulence.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/18bb94d6-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/2326413364704527e5b3453109e78879/VLF_antenna_spool.jpg
This is the very-low frequency antenna, wound up in a giant spool.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/18ce6fe5-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/89b5402681c15e855d114b5e00f1e22f/Crew_bunks.jpg
The plane has a number of bunks for crew rest. If necessary, the Doomsday planes can be refueled while aloft, extending their flying time to well more than ten hours.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/18e294e4-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/af52d7eb473486a78c036c79c2306c73/SecDef_quarters.jpg
While the NAOC planes are designed to provide airborne command and control functionality in case of a major military crisis, they are also used to fly the Secretary of Defense on international missions. When the Secretary is aboard, this is his dedicated compartment. It includes a bunk, and several chairs, a desk, and video screens.
If the plane is flying without the Secretary aboard, the compartment can be used by other senior officers.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/18f5dea3-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/452bf9a5f747f22cb2a313aa367f3c61/Conference_room.jpg
Located just outside the secretary of defense's quarters, this conference room is set up to facilitate live video conferences, as well as all the other forms of communications the plane provides and enables. When aboard, the secretary will often use the room for long meetings.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/1909685b-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/b9e8954eac2d44a7620714ff26761f59/Logo.jpg
This insignia, depicting the National Airborne Operations Center logo, and the term "Nightwatch" is found on the wall of the conference room.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/191da093-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/b2692c68b4a1ac605126b0bfcc38851d/Cockpit_empty.jpg
This is the plane's cockpit. With a few minor differences, it is essentially the same as on any Boeing 747-200.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/19366bd4-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/d8e9887892817e73ce4c2258b450640d/Flight_engineer_console.jpg
This is the flight engineer's console. On Boeing 747-400s and 747-8 Intercontinentals, there is no flight engineer, with most of that role's functions having been automated. Air Force One is also a modified 747-200.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/1949df78-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/b92142e38ce2556ba46df6f3e8790501/Generators_panel.jpg
One major difference between the NAOC planes and a standard 747-200 is that the Doomsday aircraft have eight generators, with two for each engine. A normal 747-200 has only one generator per engine. This area of the flight engineer's console shows the status and provides control over those eight generators.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/195bc0c4-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/9bc72e201c997948c87a786e8437d141/Alert_plane_from_cockpit.jpg
The NAOC plane that is on alert is seen out the cockpit window of one of the other Doomsday planes.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/196e104e-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/2727ac597ecf33e872d30286eaa07641/Navigators_station.jpg
Located at the rear of the cockpit, this is the navigator's station.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/22/197e2c36-f693-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/255eb85664a7f58d70fb4efbbb184ea6/NAOC_taking_off.jpg
One of the Doomsday planes is seen taking off over Bellevue, Neb., just outside Offutt Air Force Base. Though designed to provide full command and control functionality during the most serious of military crises, the planes are frequently flown on training and other missions.
 
 
[Date]2013-07-26 2013  Why Frank Lloyd Wright piled 60 tons on a lily pad
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e3ec596f-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/723671d31ea1cc205f6c4da578202efb/Great_workroom_from_floor.jpg
The great triumph of the columns, which are also collectively known as a lily pad, is that they effortlessly hold up the roof, allowing for walls made partially of glass tubes -- a design that allows a great deal of natural light to flood the space during the day.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e3fd797b-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/bf13265ec149e986fe8dd0b3ed51f910/60_tons_on_column.png
Though the SC Johnson company was fully on board with Wright's design, there was just one problem: the Wisconsin Industrial Commission couldn't believe that the 21-foot-tall columns meant to hold up the building's roof, which were just nine inches in diameter at the base, could possibly carry such a load. As a result, they denied permission for the great master to proceed.
 
Seventy-four years after the building's opening, one might wonder how Wright convinced the commission to change its mind. The answer: He had workers pile as much as 60 tons of sandbags on top of one of the columns, which only needed to hold 12 tons under his design.
 
By the end of the demonstration, seen here in a photograph from 1937, the commission reversed its decision in less than 24 hours, and the building became part of American's great architectural heritage.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e40dc233-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/94f3343dce3bb61d2e91e50c2775493c/Great_workroom_natural_light_1.jpg
Thanks to the masterful design of the columns, the roof and walls of the SC Johnson administration building didn't need to have structural supports spaced along the walls, as most buildings did. That allowed Wright to build in long sections high on the walls with glass tubing that let in natural light from the outside.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e41dd94d-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/4ce73b4bb24ddfd908c3eb03465ad9c0/Walkway_to_R_and_D_building.jpg
Another highlight of the SC Johnson headquarters is this walkway, which connects the administration building -- opened in 1939 -- with the research and development building, which opened in 1950 as Wright's second major commission for the family-owned company.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e42e0ea1-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/a873d5a39c184ce7c9963775aa8aff94/Sitting_area_and_column.jpg
The administration building is full of aesthetic surprises, like this second-floor seating area, which sticks out over the main floor, and which is underneath one of the signature lily pad-like columns.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e43df33b-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/7d49418e4a25a7bdf9d126c6d0d69521/Great_workroom_from_2nd_level.jpg
A look at the great workroom from the administration building's second level.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e44d90ce-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/7aee9015d72d8c35cbc179b43b86ef7f/Glass_pattern_in_Admin_building.jpg
Wright is famous for the art glass he often used in houses he built in urban environments, glass that let in natural light, but which provided privacy for those inside.
 
At the SC Johnson headquarters in Racine, Wright used a similar concept -- artistic glass, made out of tubes, that let in natural light, but kept what went on inside the building private.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e45dc322-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/2da6590c10cb2707bad1acb0789fdc72/Second_level_lightwell_long_view.jpg
The open space of an oval-shaped interior passageway in the administration building is interrupted only by the signature columns.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e46dd602-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/c0837e47b955ea8bddbf0167f972257c/Spiral_stairs_to_bathroom.jpg
Bathrooms are located below the Great Workroom, meaning that employees who need to use the restroom must climb down this spiral staircase.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e47d90ce-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/a6e3720147801cae2f3716fb5294f04d/Elevator_cage.jpg
Though some use the stairs, employees in the administration building can also take the elevator. This is the Wright-designed elevator cage.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e492af97-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/92713cd0ebba4b35a7bbd8256804d8e0/Elevator_open.jpg
A look inside the elevator cage in the Frank Lloyd Wright-designed SC Johnson headquarters administration building in Racine, Wis.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e4a28572-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/1872cacf63ca62bf4312a9f85d919e75/Tube_pattern_in_ceiling.jpg
Natural light flows into the building through a skylight made with interconnected glass tubes.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e4b20f04-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/606d0ae3b41f89f2669115397bf45f01/Desk_with_mailslots_in_background.jpg
To this day, employees who work in the SC Johnson headquarters administration building sit at desks designed by Frank Lloyd Wright, like this one. The desks have three levels, like the building, and have Wright's famous linear aesthetic.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e4c1e5ed-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/12e89abcf9890d9eaee1da015741a5d0/Desk_with_swing_drawer_pulled_out.jpg
A look at one of the drawers in the desks, which is designed to swing out, allowing full access to its contents.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e4d1c8e6-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/2ab100fc71f9c62e4344267620c8d05e/Three-legged_chair.jpg
Wright also designed three-legged chairs for the company's employees, but after many workers had trouble keeping from tipping over, Wright agreed to design a four-legged version.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e4e1caec-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/ab6a6e503adf5323b47b19f12b8d5443/Great_workroom_lighting.jpg
Though the Great Workroom is flooded in natural light during the day, it does require lighting when it gets dark.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e4f15f51-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/d3938e14231ff0534eaabab6ef751eba/Research_Tower.jpg
Today, the R&D building is being fully restored, after years of use took a heavy toll on the building's brick facade and the glass tubing that was used to let in large amounts of natural light. This is what the building looked like before the restoration began.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e50b30b4-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/56c541b3d4ce301556a1c24833b45bd1/Tubes_view_and_no_view.jpg
The RD building was designed with glass tubing around its entire exterior, allowing natural light to flood in. But the years have taken their toll on the building, and now, its brick and glass facades are being restored and replaced.
 
Here, we see a section along one wall where some of the tubing has already been replaced with all-new glass, while some has yet to be fitted. That means that, for the moment, there is a view out of the building that will never again be possible once all the new tubing is installed.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e51bdd86-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/5b05c103eccb156f2b76541025db55e0/Tubes_cleaned_in_place.jpg
The key to installing the new -- and restored -- glass tubing is mounting them on special gaskets along the building's side, as seen here.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e52bbd32-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/769b77803213c04db8dc37a3173c72ff/Old_gasket.jpg
This is an original gasket from the building, which has been taken off for cleaning. Once cleaned, it will be remounted in order to have tubing fitted over it.
 
2013-07-26 last two replace with these two
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e53c38fb-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/95fa34c3861fb8fdb341cbe41706489b/Tubes_stacked_for_cleaning.jpg
Though much of the straight tubing on the building's sides is being entirely replaced, SC Johnson decided to keep the curved tubes that were mounted on the building's corners. As a result, workers are meticulously cleaning these tubes. This is a stack of the curved tubes, which are awaiting cleaning.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/25/e54c1a97-f68d-11e2-8262-0291187b029a/thumbnail/770x578/70abd19b3d1e0fcbf49adbea1d26c0f3/Cleaned_tubes_numbered.jpg
These curved tubes have already been cleaned and are now marked so that workers know exactly where they are supposed to be re-mounted.
 
[Date]2013-07-27   Trains for miles! Inside the world's largest rail yard
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/0eb25956-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0ff8d72f72adce3676bcef04d8c5160b/Coal_trains.jpg
NORTH PLATTE, Neb. -- The Union Pacific's Bailey Yard
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/0f20f490-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0f328491c0719f84b06e285be83392f5/East_Hump.jpg
A major component of Bailey Yard is the East Hump, where every day, more than 1,500 rail cars are sorted, taken off one train, and re-routed to another. Locomotives push the cars up a 34-feet-tall mound, where they are then separated and sent downhill, one at a time, onto one of dozens of "bowl" tracks -- each of which represents a specific eastern destination.
A corresponding West Hump is also used to used to sort cars heading west, but because most traffic heads east, the bulk goes through the East hump. In addition, the West hump mound is only 20 feet high.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/27/0f89ad84-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2b4ce50886e3d95b2a13343881cf30b3/Screen_shot_2013-07-27_at_10.49.48_AM.png
A look at the aerial view of Bailey Yard, from Google Maps. The yard covers 2,850 acres, and has 114 tracks designated for specific destinations.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/101af415-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/30e0b401e38b0e32deb5a04f02b82cde/Cars_for_west_hump.jpg
Rail cars headed to the West hump to be separated are pushed uphill.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/10864019-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c33bd6ffcd57974db38a2d35f375b61e/Y823_pushing_cars.jpg
Locomotive number Y823 slowly pushes rail cars uphill towards the West hump. Because many westbound cars are empty, or at least carrying lighter loads than their eastbound counterparts, less locomotives are needed to push the cars towards the West hump than for the East hump.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/11661db7-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/fe4b32bc9742734f6865a5d5b54845d5/Disconnecting_at_East_Hump.jpg
A Bailey Yard worker manually pulls a handle to separate a car from a train at the top of the East hump.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/11f47e2a-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8d831bcce5375c008a8e9302a7dd1a5a/Car_at_retarder.jpg
After the cars are separated, they roll gently downhill using only gravity toward their designated bowl track. When they pass a so-called "retarder," their wheels are squeezed hard enough to slow them down so that they roll easily and safely onto the proper track for their eventual destination. A computer routes the cars onto the proper track.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/12addd8b-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ded52cef3388374009f008f37d06f600/Retarder.jpg
A closer look at a retarder, which is used to slow the railcars down as they roll unimpeded downhill towards their designated bowl track where they will connect with their intended train at 4 miles an hour or less.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/133ab18d-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a6317721c993687fb7bdf86e6ecd1b57/East_Hump_incline.jpg
A look down the East hump incline toward the 64 bowl tracks below.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/143d7ce6-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/17b44be54be9406c9b9592803d7e0ac2/East_Hump_incline_cars_going_down.jpg
One by one, cars roll gently down the East hump incline.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/14c8b44c-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/cf1c5e23d4b4e4e00e93c8aeb99f3bdb/Working_East_Hump.jpg
A Bailey Yard technician monitors traffic passing through the East hump. He and other technicians use sophisticated remote controls to operate the locomotives that push the rail cars up to the humps.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/155b8c80-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0eba8f371aba982cb54ca8077e5b9be1/Remote_control.jpg
The technicians use remote controls like this one to operate the locomotives as they pass through the humps. The remotes give them all the control they would have if they were aboard the locomotives.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/15c1d4ae-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/98889df003744a7982371e165e758c26/Coal_trains_2.jpg
Long coal trains sit idle on the tracks at Bailey Yard while a multi-modal train heads in the opposite direction.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/16bd649a-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/309837e0048d191b9c67e82268e6edc7/Locomotive_service.jpg
Often, when trains come into Bailey Yard, their rail cars are separated from the locomotives and are sent off to one of the humps to be re-directed to trains heading to their eventual destination. While that happens, the locomotives are brought to the diesel yard to be serviced.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/174988ce-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5036c3f696f9248cbb8a4c855693e3a9/Train_cars_as_far_as_you_can_see.jpg
Though Bailey Yard is off limits to the general public, visitors can climb the Golden Spike observation tower, where they can get a panoramic view of the world's largest rail yard. From the top of the tower, they can see rail cars all the way to the horizon.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/17d4f48e-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/86ee4ff470612385f512c6b8c6d97639/Bailey_Yard_from_plane.jpg
Flying to Chicago to begin Road Trip 2013, CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman flew directly over North Platte and got a view of Bailey Yard from more than 30,000 feet.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/1954791e-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/15a5e72e07cbea0b25d918165bd68e3d/Axles_in_the_maintenance_yard.jpg
Many rail cars that roll through Bailey Yard have damaged wheels that must be replaced and repaired. Here, near the maintenance area, dozens of train wheel axles are lined up in preparation for being used as replacements.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/19e2b5a9-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/184a837361ba17a91f05938874084574/Bad_order_tag.jpg
Rail cars that need maintenance are identified at a location a few miles outside North Platte and tagged so that workers at Bailey Yard know that they need attention.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/1a93ece7-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4fcef211ec90f8b902b57edab80e2129/East_service.jpg
Many locomotives heading east are sent through the East service track, where they are loaded up with, among other things, sand that they will use to gain traction when their steel wheels slip on the steel tracks. The sand is shot onto the tracks using nozzles mounted at the wheels.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/1b49e6ff-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/16a23e071def8e82f3957fa3bd609db0/East_service_sand_tower.jpg
This large tower along side the East service track is filled with sand, which is loaded into special tanks on the locomotives using cranes.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/1bb2473d-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/611a8a6a2c4fc9a66a5ec3557c2a759c/East_service_locomotive.jpg
A locomotive getting serviced at the East service track.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/1c3eeeed-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/96ae41bb35b359225f7d4bc9242d455a/West_service.jpg
The West service track is much smaller than its eastbound counterpart given the lighter trains that go from east to west.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/1ca716dd-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f066496eec0042b536fbc521fef81895/Golden_Spike.jpg
Many people visit North Platte every year just to climb the Golden Spike observation tower and get a panoramic view of Bailey Yard.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/1d3b894f-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9ed2bf2ade312c3647bfe67f78726b16/Ultrasonic_wheel_repair_facility.jpg
This special facility -- the only one of its kind in the world -- is designed to inspect railcar wheels for small defects that would otherwise be invisible to the naked eye. Special ultrasonic sensors inspect each wheel to determine if there are problems that need maintenance.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/1e1889f5-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/75afb12319a5721eae3bd4df114ae3ef/Axles_on_a_car.jpg
Dozens of train wheel axles roll through Bailey Yard on top of a rail car.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/1ecf0809-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/785d41819c68567f92d98ff8b580929c/Shrink-wrapped_locomotive.jpg
A locomotive, most likely for a passenger train, sits idle and shrink-wrapped in plastic at Bailey Yard.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/26/1f351b7c-f7f0-11e2-8c7c-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a2e5c09ff6ee3b028091bfd1538f05b1/East_hump_locomotive.jpg
A pair of locomotives that have just pushed dozens of rail cars to the top of the East hump reach the top of the 34-foot-tall incline themselves.
 
[Date]2013-07-31 Motor Speed way
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/9365d2e8-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/234573b237853fe1e8cf736606c147f4/Scanning_machine.jpg
One recent innovation is this laser scanning system that is used to take precise measurements of each car that will compete in a Nascar event in order to ensure that race teams are adhering to strict regulations about how the vehicles are engineered for a race.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/936708ce-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/49bbc2bcdacdff9b92385e504e8f5943/2_car_on_the_scanner.jpg Car No. 2, which is driven by Brad Keselowski, gets scanned on Nascar's laser scanner at the Indianapolis Motor Speedway prior to the Brickyard 400 on Sunday, July 28, 2013.
If Nascar officials determine that a car doesn't meet race standards, the offending team is given a short amount of time to make the required modifications.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/93682c55-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/c4aff17b03523d4cb9b77897099b357d/Official_under_car_at_scanner.jpg
Hours before the Brickyard 400, one of Nascar's biggest races, each of the 43 cars that were taking part in the competition lined up to be scanned. Once a car passes the examination, it has a sticker placed on it, and a Nascar official stays with the vehicle until the race begins to ensure that no one tries to modify the car in any disallowed way.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/93694fab-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/1cc3b02b32837f4b9c9aa2140b8b4bc1/Calibration_plate.jpg
When a car is on the scanner, it has plates like this one placed on each of its wheels. These are used in conjunction with lasers in order to ensure that the measurements that are made are highly accurate. Because Nascar aims for a level playing field, it wants each car to hit the race track with specifications that meet the sport's standards. Essentially, it wants the drivers to be the difference in who wins or loses, not the cars.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/936a713b-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/e1a4650529911ec0e62d783a6465cfde/Laser.jpg
A look at one of the lasers that's part of Nascar's pre-race scanning system.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/936b9d1d-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/14618b5d16fc1b7a59252ab14917e7d4/DEJ_88_coming_off_track.jpg
After completing his qualifying run the day before the Brickyard 400, Dale Earnhardt Jr.'s No. 88 car is pushed off the track and taken back to his team's garage.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/936cb340-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/3076274de08d6110388f43e178954dbe/RN39_record_breaking_qualifying.jpg
Ryan Newman, who eventually won the 2013 Brickyard 400, takes off on his qualifying run, which set a new track record and earned him the pole position for the race.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/936dcbd6-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/e5c63181fef78d7e549287637e2c6bd9/Screen_shot_2013-07-30_at_11.38.35_PM.png
This Nascar chart spells out the Gen-6 car's newest features.

http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/936eea47-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/3e4e380c9251656344ff896afb3fb4aa/Anatomy_of_a_Gen-6_car.png
In 2013, Nascar rolled out its so-called "Gen 6" car, an all-new vehicle that aims to be lighter and safer than the previous version, which was used from 2007 to 2012, while also doing away with the uniform look of all manufacturers' cars that fans didn't like.
The new design has 17 main elements, not all new, as demonstrated by this Nascar chart: A radiation air intake; brake air intakes; hood pins; cowl induction; body panels; a roll cage; a jack post; double frame rail and energy absorbing material; a window net; roof strips; a TV camera; roof flaps; jacking bolts; cooling vents; Goodyear Eagle racing tires; rear deck fins; and a rear spoiler.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/9370153d-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/a5e68479cdf02a2977490be81e5dc802/6_generations_poster.png
This poster demonstrates the look of Nascar's six generations of cars.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/93712e6a-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/8c35ea674bdc891a3cf898c655fdcb20/Cockpit.jpg
Nascar drivers must endure several hours of driving at break-neck speeds crammed inside a narrow cockpit like this one.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/9372501f-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/5238c29823bd8a0dda896d51aae120d7/Tear-off_windshields.jpg
Rather than having teams spend precious racing time cleaning off windshields that have gotten messy between pit stops, they can simply tear off a layer and expose a fresh windshield. The cars are fitted with seven layers of tear-off windshield before the race.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/93736dad-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/9237ea46fac11943fa4475be02d22ac5/Tear-off_windshield_torn_off.jpg
One of the tear-off windshield layers is seen crumpled up in pit row after being ripped off during a pit stop.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/93747da2-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/16fabad5bfeec16652b48135094c8a86/Roof_flap.jpg
This roof flap is a design meant to help cars remain stable even in a spin-out. Previously, Nascar vehicles risked taking air if they spun out at full-speed, and while that's still a danger, the roof flaps are meant to help reduce that risk.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/93758fac-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/05973421afcb7b8fe7ed3327c5a6f194/Clear_spoiler.jpg
Another aerodynamic feature of the cars is this clear spoiler on the side of the rear-hood.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/9376b5cc-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/751d9f5757505ce534057698f7c4411c/Tape_for_engine.jpg
If a car's engine is running too hot during a race, the team can rip these strips off the radiator air intake, letting in more air and cooling the engine down.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/9377c94e-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/bace48b767a115aa65d22620dd422de2/RN_and_JJ_in_front.jpg
Ryan Newman (No. 39) and Jimmie Johnson (No. 48) at the front of the Brickyard 400 field at the beginning of the Sprint Cup Series race on Sunday, July 28, 2013. Newman won the race's pole position with a track record speed of 187.531 miles an hour, not even a tenth of a mile per hour better than second-position starter Johnson, who had earlier set a track record of his own with a qualifying speed of 187.438 miles an hour.

http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/9378e0b1-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/e30464a557bc965e57e8883b3f62329e/JJ_qualifying.jpg
Jimmie Johnson, who was considered the favorite at this year's Brickyard 400 after having won four previous races at the Indianapolis Motor Speedway, tears around the track during his qualifying run on Saturday, July 27, 2013. His speed of 187.438 miles an hour set a track record, breaking one that had lasted since 2004. But Ryan Newman broke Johnson's record less than an hour later, besting his speed with a 187.531 mile an hour performance.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/9379f987-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/ad8ea956367ea63071f2a0547d36d79e/DEJ_88_pit_stop.jpg
Dale Earnhardt Jr., who races car No. 88, comes in for a pit stop early in the Brickyard 400. Earnhardt finished sixth but would have finished higher had he not had to come in for an early pit due to a loose wheel.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/937b0ec7-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/3a493a34bf5b7ebf397741fb37abccea/Pole.jpg
The pole at the Indianapolis Motor Speedway, which shows the leader board during the Brickyard 400.
