After Ryan Newman earned the Brickyard 400 pole position with a record-setting qualifying speed of 187.531 miles an hour, he was mobbed by the press.

http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/937d42d5-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/008f64f36270c308d9d3994a314e0345/Official_prowling_the_pit.jpg
A Nascar official prowls the pit during the race.

http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/30/937e5dae-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/7da3c104171fa750dc03c46fe5dade0a/FanVision_chart.png
Nascar fans can keep up with the race action by renting or buying a FanVision, a device that gives real-time leaderboard statistics, as well as live video and audio from drivers' cars.


[Date]2013-08-01  Flying car
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/91f5cb8e-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/d95bdaa5d5fd3d5da88f43fc1ecf4060/Wings_up.jpg
Flying Car
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/91f6fa17-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/1b64823868c23f27c8c8eac101d91bbf/Taking_off.jpg
Here is Terrafugia's promotional blurb for its $279,000 hybrid:
"The Transition is the transportation of the future today. A street-legal airplane that converts between flying and driving modes in under a minute, the Transition brings a new level of freedom, flexibility, and fun to personal aviation.
"It gives the pilot the option to land and drive in bad weather, provides integrated ground transportation on both ends of the flight, and fits in a standard single car garage at home. The Transition can fly in and out of over 5,000 public airports in the U.S. and is legal to drive on public roads and highways.
"It is the only light aircraft designed to meet Federal Motor Vehicle Safety Standards, and it is also equipped with a full-vehicle parachute for additional safety."
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/91f81fb3-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/2c581bbba882dd41779806a487e2bd4b/On_tarmac_wings_down.jpg
The Transition sits along the flight line at OshKosh, preparing for its demonstration flight. Its wings unfolded, as they must be for flying.
The plane has a top speed of about 100 miles an hour, and has a range of about 410 miles. It gets about five gallons per hour in the sky, and 35 miles per gallon on the highway.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/91f941ee-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/7c4fc0286f08a1f86884dc804f39fa0a/Catching_the_sun.jpg
As the Transition flies above the crowd of thousands at sunset, it catches a few last rays of the gorgeous Wisconsin sun.
The Transition features automated electromechanical folding, rear wheel drive on the road, and automotive style entry, exit, and seating. It doesn't need a hangar since it can fit in a standard house garage. It also has both a steering wheel and stick and rudders.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/91fa5904-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/399cdf59fbaaa1635d103ebb56976c65/Straight_on.jpg
The Transition flies directly toward the AirVenture air show crowd.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/91fb6b37-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/c0f8b7fbb5a00d731db2e6decec19bd1/Right_side_in_air.jpg
The Transition is seen in the air with its right side to the crowd.
The flying car can be driven to a location if it can't fly because of inclement weather, and has a 100 hp Rotax 912iS engine. It also features a vehicle parachute, and modern glass avionics, Terrafugia says, as well as standard automotive crash safety features including driver and passenger air bags.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/91fc81ee-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/714b22274591dbcf95d8ab3ee169744c/Banking_to_right.jpg
The Transition is seen banking to the right at the EAA AirVentures air show in OshKosh, Wis.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/91ff1d0d-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/06b76c8ad4a1ff1bfd587dddaac52105/Underneath_overhead.jpg
A look at the underside of the Transition as it soars overhead at the air show.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/920181c9-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/1459ad0057ce3400737b756ffe86c9e4/Taxiing_in_after_landing.jpg
After completing its public performance, the Transition taxis in from the air strip.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/9202aa2b-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/7d0e8970033c3293b5fe7eed78efaab7/From_front_wings_down.jpg
Taxiing in, the Transition's wings are still down.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/9203c9d3-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/cfdc2a407e87750971097fd1d4510f91/Wings_up_from_front.jpg
Ready to drive, the flying car's wings are automatically folded up at the push of a button.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/9204e0b2-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/d44711f7a2422f468c3c914fdd663b64/Driving_away.jpg
Just minutes after landing, the Transition is street-legal and driving away.
 
[Date]2013-08-02  (is 8-1, but conflict with Flying car)  Inside U.S. Strategic Command
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/922ebd72-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/1c8a37c0e005c1dbb3e353761fd45a33/Strategic_Command_sign.jpg
OFFUTT AIR FORCE BASE, Neb. -- The scope of the entire U.S. military is vast, yet there is not a single military mission on Earth that is not supported in some way by U.S. Strategic Command.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/922fed31-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/b66220ff853c8c4fecf55d2d3ecf3f18/Minuteman_and_Trident.jpg
Formerly known as Strategic Air Command (SAC) and based at Offutt since 1948 (after being founded in 1946 at Bolling Air Force Base in Washington, D.C.), Stratcom is a joint command, meaning members of all American military services are represented.
A Minuteman missile (right) and a Trident missile stand outside Stratcom headquarters as a reminder of its past and current missions.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/923106cb-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/7bedf905e879e89feb104da32a644883/Operations_floor.jpg
A look at the operations floor at Stratcom in a photograph provided by Stratcom.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/92322c28-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/40deb016d58bbd36638ae429736f0f4c/Commanders_situation_room.jpg
A photograph, provided by Stratcom, of the commanders' situation room at Stratcom headquarters at Offutt Air Force Base.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/9233614a-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/797ac76ca1dccb85b034d40e90b0b483/Legacy_SAC_logo_on_floor.jpg
As reminder of Stratcom's legacy, the entryway to its headquarters features a U.S. Strategic Air Command (SAC) logo in the floor. SAC was deactivated in 1992.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/92347984-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/f61ed774ebe974e8b3ac61295e3fe86c/Doomsday_plane.jpg
One of Stratcom's many missions is the National Airborne Operations Center, known colloquially as the Doomsday plane, which military leaders would use to conduct operations in case of a crisis such as a nuclear war. There are four NAOC planes, which are operated by the 55th Air Wing at Offutt Air Force Base.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/92359403-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/767df335cea5367f07ef093b2d38e5ff/Looking_Glass.jpg
An EC-135 known as "Looking Glass" that was a precursor to the current-generation Doomsday plane.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/9236c0a0-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/269f63992fa6ea0bf92d03fbe72f90f5/SR-71_Blackbird_from_SAC_museum.jpg
This is an SR-71 Blackbird, housed at the Strategic Air Command museum in Ashland, Neb. The Blackbird was operated by SAC.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/21/9237d012-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/3eecdf8c3ea194c6e194b6ab55622b3d/U2_from_SAC_museum.jpg
A U2 spy plane that flew high over the Soviet Union during the Cold War, housed at the Strategic Air Command Museum.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/9238e470-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/494bbe287f6ff5fa466fcef93cdb5837/Stratcom_chapel_stained_glass.jpg
The Offutt Air Force Base chapel features many stained glass windows adorned with Strategic Command insignia.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/9239f5d0-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/f8d1c1e2194dc9cf4cd3066cf28e2668/Stratcom_chapel_stained_glass_2.jpg
Another stained glass window at the Offutt Air Force Base chapel.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/923b142c-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/c769f638b2bf2aee0e82dbab1b3f2aa4/Enola_Gay_and_Bockscar_modified.jpg
Now a huge military gym, this field house was once a factory where Enola Gay, and Bockscar, the B-29 bombers that dropped atomic bombs on Hiroshima, Japan and Nagasaki, Japan during World War II, were modified to be able to carry those nuclear weapons.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/923c251d-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/11d3c081f6040b6af4b72f87be241ec0/Stratcom_building.jpg
The front of current Stratcom headquarters at Offutt Air Force Base. Stratcom is currently building a huge new, $1 billion headquarters nearby.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/923d35ce-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/3a2346e39d3934af91b17882f6731f8e/Command_section_entrance.jpg
The entrance to the Command section at Stratcom headquarters. This is where the command's leadership, including current Commander C. Robert Kehler, work.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/07/31/923e4d59-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/5e2f9386f86e703fc24a5e6429b0aab7/Stratcom_intelligence_many_doors.jpg
There are several different security-enabled doors at the entrance to the security directorate at Stratcom headquarters, signalling that what goes on behind the doors is worth protecting.
 
 
 
 
[Date]2013-08-06   Cool Museum
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/0603ad6e-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2055f54e96a3ee606265d71a3c3d50d7/Bus_over_the_edge.jpg
ST. LOUIS -- It's a rare museum that has a school bus hanging off the roof, let alone a bus that kids can climb on. But at the City Museum here, that's just one small piece of the fun.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/0607bbf0-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ffb1fbb57d26d56f6063f1cf95aa64b6/Climbing_high_overhead.jpg
With the school bus hanging off the edge of the giant warehouse building, the City Museum is impossible to ignore, even from a block away. Cassilly's vision included giving visitors a chance to go beyond the usual things they're allowed to do in public places and to have a great time doing so. After all, who else would allow visitors to climb on a bus sticking out into open space or through a tunnel high over the heads of others?
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/0609abfc-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/0145c8ebd9a4951a2236a22f4f859dde/Ten-story_spiral_slide_from_top.jpg
In the 1920s, the warehouse that is home to the City Museum was the world's largest shoe distributor, and it used these spiral slides to send shoes from the upper floors to those below. Today, it's a slide used by endless numbers of kids -- and adults -- to gleefully shoot from the upper floors to the bottom.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/0617c076-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/56fe6902e7012e046a82e7f5c839eb2b/Ten-story_spiral_slide_from_bottom.jpg
A look back up at the 10-story slide.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/061996bf-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ca51f058c4288c848865932dce204caf/City_Museum.jpg
Who wouldn't want to spend a few hours at a "museum" that announces itself with a huge airplane on a tower and a long open-air tunnel that anyone can crawl through -- if they're comfortable crawling through a tunnel high over the heads of other visitors.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/061b6b5b-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/42495f602c718e2bea78cae936f6923e/Rooftop_tower_and_elephant.jpg
Everything in the City Museum is reused in some way, and its director, Rick Erwin, is constantly making trips to salvage sales in St. Louis and other cities on the lookout for unwanted goods that would make for exciting new elements of the City Museum.
This tower -- and elephant -- are on the top of the building's roof, and have quite a view of downtown St. Louis. y
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/061df046-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/e316e467b5efe94f08aeb9c769870d5b/Rooftop_ferris_wheel.jpg
Another attraction on the City Museum's roof is this Ferris wheel, which both kids and adults can ride. The museum is planning to install a kids-only Ferris wheel inside a dome on the roof of the building.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/061fd79e-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/828200d75183212eb7c1167171acd7e9/Egyptian_entrance.jpg
Rick Erwin, the City Museum director, said that at any given time, there are several new projects under way, including six at the moment. This Egyptian sarcophagus is one of them. Erwin said that he decided that a new Egyptian-themed area had to look like a sarcophagus, and so his builders made the entrance to the space have five-foot-thick walls.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/06211abc-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/17581d53a284f2e52ac249efa7db222e/Worlds_largest_pencil.jpg
If a giant needed to take the SAT, he or she might use this pencil, the world's largest. At 76 feet long and a weight of 21,500 pounds, this pencil can actually write -- and erase. And since it used No. 2 lead, it could be used, if someone could lift it and write with it, to take a standardized test. It was created by artist Ashrita Furman.
All told, this mammoth pencil contains 4,000 pounds of Pennsylvania graphite and is the equivalent of 1.9 million normal pencils. Its eraser is made of rubber and weighs 250 pounds.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/0622d3f9-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/55add99e714d34ab61df64dbe5234a8f/Pencil_lead.jpg
A look at the sharpened lead of the world's largest pencil, at the City Museum in St. Louis.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/06241ab0-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/9d1366c9ec0ae6d85732d7fb8baac8c0/Insect_room.jpg
Among its many varied galleries and exhibitions, this is the City Museum's insect room, which presents visitors with a stunning collection of butterflies, moths, and other bugs.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/06256329-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/f053a55fa16d288d09bbe8c93adcfb6e/Worlds_largest_underpants.jpg
This is the world's largest pair of underwear, found in the City Museum's area known as the Museum of Mirth, Mystery and Mayhem. Not long ago, the underpants were stolen, only to reappear about a month later, washed, and accompanied by a matching woman's pair.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/06268eda-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a78488e0d8b0aa0ef4734d00fb8ade1e/Kids_climbing_1.jpg
If the City Museum isn't filled with the screams and shrieks of happy children running around and climbing up into various tubes and tunnels, or disappearing into mysterious holes in the floor, then it isn't open for business. Everywhere you look there are different passageways and compartments for people -- kids and adults -- to crawl through, over, below, and into.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/0633f174-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a60058afd1456615028199cbb4dce3d8/Shoelace_factory.jpg
One interesting element of the City Museum is that it invites a number of entrepreneurs to open up small businesses inside. Those can range from small eateries to this, a shoelace factory. Using equipment from the building's past, the factory's owner makes brightly-hued laces to sell. Visitors can buy a pair, or just watch as the machinery, which once made laces for World War II soldiers, turns out pair after pair.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/063544cc-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/c4f56f8f74a8c7d77dce6341f22953d1/Kids_sliding_down.jpg
There are probably too many different slides and tunnels to count at the City Museum. That's no doubt true because builders there are always looking for places to add new tunnels or passageways.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/06367abb-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/b5f4a365b6d5860c2d0dfb1fd459c864/Vault.jpg
This bank vault, complete with the fronts of hundreds of safety-deposit boxes, opens up to a hall of mirrors, among other attractions.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/0637b70c-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/2c06f84a1f03a6cd7579dbce69315c0d/Hall_of_mirrors.jpg
A look down the hall of mirrors.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/0638f0a2-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/ddc18d2170d8c64b7fb3d53a564cebe6/Hamster_wheel.jpg
What kid wouldn't want to run around inside a life-size hamster wheel?
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/063a27f5-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/6884f45568e0254d6a7476aa1b91eeb3/Kids_climbing_through_coil.jpg
The City Museum transformed old beer cooling coils from the Anheuser-Busch brewery in St. Louis into a climbing tube for visitors.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/063becb2-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/8b1be627f63b5f66bd48fabf0d73967d/Ceiling_made_from_airplane_fuselage.jpg
This ceiling installation -- made to look like the crystals inside a cave -- are actually made from the material in a Boeing airplane fuselage.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/063d355e-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/a6e269244b75fee1d69ae8cdf15a9c29/Flying_dinosaur.jpg
What museum would be complete without a pterodactyl?
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/064deb30-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/5f324e4c81648be6749cdd15012d7326/The_whale.jpg
The City Museum's first floor features a full-size bowhead whale for visitors to explore, and find their way around.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/06504f15-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/4af57223b7e48349798824fd995c2831/Dinosaur_and_passageways.jpg
Anyone wanting to climb the stairs from the first floor to the second would have to pass this giant dinosaur and look up above at climbing cages, most likely filled with kids, or kids at heart.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/05/06518853-0627-11e3-bf02-d4ae52e62bcc/thumbnail/770x578/93918cc827a2490198d14d9deae1b441/Ball_pit.jpg
MonstroCity, a special outdoor section of the City Museum, is "a captivating collision of old and new, architectural castoffs and post-apocalyptic chaos....It is is at once interactive sculpture and playground. Comprised of wrought iron slinkies, fire trucks, stone turrets, airplane fuselages, slides of all sizes and shapes, and a pair of ball pits modeled after the Thunderdome, MonstroCity inspires a child-like sense of bravado in guests of all ages."
 
[Date]2013-08-08  Road to Indy: The life of a Goodyear racing tire
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f4fb97a-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/34412172456322ca9589257792ac4b94/Indy_-_pit_stop_rolling_tire_off_car.jpg
When you buy a set of new Goodyear tires for your car, you have every reason to expect that they'll last you 80,000 miles. But when a Nascar team gets its hands on a set of Goodyear's racing tires, the expectation is a little lower: just 100 miles.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f50e2a6-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/25d8fbab2af4f73bf5973acd022030a1/Pressing_rubber.jpg
In order to produce a racing tire's tread, Goodyear workers run what's known as green rubber through a machine called an extruder. There, the rubber is heated and blended to the point that it's soft enough to formed. At that point, it's run through a die, which pushes out a long, continuous strip of rubber.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f5203bf-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/706556cee9f90a804fff7b75ba70ca99/Sheets_coming_off_machine.jpg
Heated rubber, forming a continuous strip, is pushed through the die. After making its way through the extruder, the rubber is laser-measured to make certain that its shape meets engineers' specifications. It's then sent down a conveyor belt to be cooled and cut to the desired length.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/07/8f5322ec-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/58b1953be6641f4cab1f537aabc72524/M-building_edges.jpg
Additional components of a tire, like its belts, are manufactured in a machine known as a calender. There, large rolls of nylon, aramid, and fiberglass are fed into the machine, where they have a rubber coating added to each side and between each individual cord. The goal is to completely encapsulate each cord in rubber.
This is the bead, a hoop-shaped tire component, two of which go in each tire. The bead is the rigid part of the tire that keep it held firmly to the wheel. The bead also gives the tire its round-shaped opening. The bead is made by encapsulating high-tensile steel wire in rubber and winding it around what's known as a mandrel into a hoop shape.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f5443a8-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/e21d663c621f2efbe7825e2974ca32fd/M-layering.jpg
After making all the different tire components, they are moved to a tire building machine, where highly-trained builders assemble the tires by hand. Known here as "green tires," they are constructed in two separate sections -- the carcass, which provides the load-carrying capability of a racing tire; and the belt package, which is responsible for the shape and footprint of the tire at high speeds.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f555cdd-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/bb0685d9eab09056fe5efede60005ebf/M-Adding_thicker_outer_layers.jpg
In order to make a carcass, the builder begins with an interliner made of rubber, which is designed to keep air sealed inside the tire when it's inflated. After that, the rubber-coated fabric piles, which were manufactured at the calender, are added to the inner liner. This gives the tire its shape.
Next, the beads and other components are added, stiffening the sidewall.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f567f52-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/e3ff66ca108335de87529df52311b4f7/M-inflated.jpg
Next, an airbag in the machine inflates, giving the tire a shape nearly identical to what its final dimensions will be. Doing this makes it possible to put the tire's many components in the proper position before the tire is cured.
A tire builder will also create the belt package at this point, adding two belts applied at diagonal angles, and folded over each other to increase strength. As well, a small fabric strip known as an overlay, is wound around the tire, helping to reinforce the tire's shape and structure at high speeds.
Lastly, a one-eighth of an inch-thick strip of tread is added. The tread is that thin because a racing tire is designed for heat resistance and grip rather than a long tread life.
Finally, the carcass and the belt packaged are put together, and an automatic roller presses them together. It is now a "green tire," and a barcode sticker is applied.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f57a1b2-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/21efd22bb07b61c78b1708060e1a694f/Applying_RFID.jpg
Nascar tracks tires at every step of the way, from delivery to the teams to the race, and beyond. To do so, they track RFID tags that are applied as the final step in the green tire's assembly.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f58b1f7-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/52eac77c88a55c13b5ae201ba2bcfbaf/Curing.jpg
The green tires are then taken to a curing press, where a hot mold closes around the tire, and an airbag inflates insides, providing the tire's shape. This process vulcanizes the rubber through the application of specified and tightly-regulated amounts of heat and pressure.
Lastly, the curing press opens and the tires are taken out and moved to a conveyor that takes them to an inflation machine. There, it is inflated for 30 minutes in order to retain its shape and size during the cooling process.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f59cbda-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/cc1255d6faff4ae01776393753de2103/Race_tire_components.jpg
A look at the wide variety of components that go into a Goodyear racing tire.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f5aebcb-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/6abaf2756a36a56b275f4ebff60d968e/Indy_-_Wall_of_tires_ready_for_teams.jpg
After the manufacturing process, the tires are eventually shipped to one of the many Nascar races held throughout the United States each year. Generally, there's about a 30-day lag between production and the race.
According to Nascar rules, each team participating in a Sprint Cup Series race -- which is Nascar's major leagues -- can use up to 10 or 11 sets or tires, depending on the race. The tires are not all the same, however. Right-side tires are about an eighth of an inch larger than left-side tires and usually have higher pressure, which helps cars in the turns on a racetrack stay stable
This wall of tires is outside the Goodyear garage at the Indianapolis Motorspeedway prior to the Brickyard 400. Typically, Goodyear brings about 4,500 tires to each Nascar race.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f5c098f-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/fcb476aa12888ca64231cbd75ee47c5c/Indy-Fitting_tires_on_wheels.jpg
Goodyear delivers all its tires to the racing teams mounted on wheels. The goal, as with much of what Nascar mandates when it comes to racing specifications, is to ensure that each team is on a level playing field, regardless of its financial resources.
Here, Goodyear crew members mount the tires on wheels using special machines.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f5d1b98-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/84a0d85b255c01e079f48a098d623968/Indy-inner_tire.jpg
On any racing track longer than a mile, Nascar requires that cars use tires featuring a tubeless inner tire designed to allow the driver to make it safely back to the pit in case of a flat.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f5e2c49-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/b54a77e5f76d43f933bfe397e6b39601/Indy_-_2_car_tire_on_white_wheel.jpg
While most Nascar teams use black wheels, the No. 2 car, driven by Brad Keselowski, sports white. Keselowski's tires for the 2013 Brickyard 400 are seen piled up outside Goodyear's garage at the Indianapolis Motorspeedway.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f5f3c94-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/50c0cc9a0f04276fea2cbde2aa25559d/Indy_-_2_valves.jpg
On one of Keselowski's wheels, you can see the two valves that belong both to the main tire and to its tubeless inner tire.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f604e1e-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/aa197204f441bb91766c135e255981f8/Indy_-_2_car_racing_on_white_wheels.jpg
Keselowski races through his qualifying round on the white wheels on which his team has its Goodyear racing tires mounted.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f616592-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/b43e81207540d74bf15d8ba5fb014795/Indy_-_tires_on_DEJ_car_88.jpg
Nascar favorite Dale Ernhardt Jr.'s No. 88 car is seen being pushed back off the track after he completed his qualifying run the day before the Brickyard 400.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f62772e-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/8764ddad222b7528b0261e99a8a8b49d/Indy_-_left_and_right_tires_laid_out_pit.jpg
In the pit area, a racing team has laid out two sets of Goodyear racing tires, with the right and left tires designated by different color strips. Goodyear's right-side racing tires are slightly bigger and often are filled to a higher pressure than the left side tires. That's helps keeps the cars stable as they go left around every curve on the track.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f639083-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/a20227408f97581ac50b0ce7f106573a/Indy_-_worn_tire.jpg
Most Nascar teams replace their cars' tires every time they refuel, which is about every 100 miles. This is what a tire looks like after being pulled off a race car at a Nascar event. Note the rough surface, which used to be very smooth and unmarked.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f64a002-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/b883f87144ea3119aae90bcb5c48514c/Indy_-_rolling_worn_tires_out_of_pit.jpg
A team member carts a load of tires out of pit row after they were pulled off a car during the Brickyard 400.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f65c467-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/60e850ad1ccfb8dbcc03ebc7f9501392/Indy_-_putting_new_tires_on_88_pit_stop.jpg
Earnhardt's crew races through a pit stop, replacing No. 88's tires. During the Brickyard 400, Earnhardt lost his position early in the race due to a loose wheel. But he eventually managed to make up most of his lost time and finished in sixth place after falling all the way to last place.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f66d98b-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/5733db3961120ef1419ad72d47ae5ee6/Indy_-_measuring_wear.jpg
A team member uses a special tool to measure the wear on a tire that has been pulled off a car during a pit stop. The teams -- and Nascar -- keep close track of data like this because it tells them a lot about how the drivers are performing, as well as how the tires do under the extreme conditions of a Nascar race.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/06/8f67ffb7-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/2bba1e8ffe3b76ad705df55d9a33c806/Indy_-_burning_worn_rubber_off.jpg
A pit crew member uses a flame to burn off worn tread in order to measure exactly how much wear the tire took while it was on the car.
 
 
[Date]2013-08-17   Taliesin: At home with Frank Lloyd Wright
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b92506a-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/608d0057957b35e742cf49a7f61c2a2b/Courtyard_fountain.jpg
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b9384be-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/43a29e83dd994a5f8c7091899d907292/Exterior_on_approach.jpg
Wright lived in Oak Park, Ill., where he had set up his architectural firm. But when he fell in love with Mamah Borthwick, the wife of a client, he moved -- along with his mistress -- to Spring Green. He built the main house on the Taliesin estate -- which means "shining brow" in Welsh -- for Borthwick, completing it in 1911.
 
Though the house was first completed that year, it has been evolving ever since, in part because Wright had to contend with two major fires, but also because even after his death, Taliesin has continued to evolve as a showpiece of architecture and of the famous architect himself.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b9491c1-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/ef64ccf1923d862c34ff1d357f93d232/Long_cantilvered_walkway.jpg
The main house at Taliesin is full of trademark Wright touches, like this long walkway that sticks out off the side of the house.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b95a4db-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/127bc7e48190987e0ff2aa663de9ef7b/View_of_cantilevered_walkway.jpg
A view of the walkway that allowed those inside to walk well out away from the building and into what would appear to be wide open space, despite the walkway resting on a stone pillar below.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b96b78d-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/67dc7f18b078f7530a5c014b72183871/Look_at_garden_from_passageway.jpg
A look at the main house's garden courtyard from underneath an entry passageway.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b97c829-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/7667b033c967cb740d84cd680bdc9502/Courtyard_from_fountain.jpg
The main house features a small courtyard, garden, and fountain just below a set of stairs that lead to the rolling hills and fields of the Taliesin estate to the south.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b98da6d-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/3711df22d06c2ba78a10cd1076a18cee/House_from_above.jpg
The main house at Taliesin was built to blend into the landscape, as so many of Wright's buildings were.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b99eb4b-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/8adda6f30aadfbfb29796690c42d5ad8/Lamp.jpg
A signature Frank Lloyd Wright lamp inside the main house.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b9afc62-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/4c8097785a8278aac602ef49c5f989a9/Living_room.jpg
One of Wright's most exquisite rooms is the living room in the main house at Taliesin, a room that is at once vast and intimate. What's not evident to the naked eye is that Wright expanded the room at the point of the pillar on the far wall after a fire. Originally the pillar was where the wall was.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b9c0b9d-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/bd353dc60e5026647426eae00807fba1/Living_room_view.jpg
One of the most elegant elements of the living room is the wide open view of the peaceful and stunningly beautiful local landscape, hills, and valleys. Taliesin was built in the middle of a large, untouched area of nature, and Wright wanted everyone who visited -- or lived there -- to be able to enjoy that nature from the house's main living space.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b9d23b8-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/02b347dcfe78f7f47277869e2735b055/Courtyard.jpg
A look back at the main house across the courtyard and its gardens.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b9e3a77-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/2967eb479de16659545b4cedb478ae79/House_into_the_hill.jpg
Wright fans may at first think that the living room is cantilevered over the hillside, which almost seems to disappear as it drops below the house. But in fact, Wright built the house into the side of the hill, cleverly using the shape of the landscape to serve his architectural and engineering purposes.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8b9f56c8-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/46fed6ed569d2fb9570a9993f3cff851/Wrights_bedroom.jpg
A look at Wright's bedroom.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8ba068d0-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/adf803ad7e09bccf5fd97622a309f35a/Mrs_Wrights_hearth.jpg
After Mamah Borthwick died in the 1914 fire, Wright married twice more, finally taking Olga Ivanovna Lazovich as his wife. Her tastes were different than those of Wright, as her bedroom shows. Where Wright's style was complex and layered -- as evidenced by the masonry on the room's back wall, her aesthetic tended to more simple style, as the hearth in her bedroom shows.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8ba17a4b-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/634735896d1f156fb59b08eb8857df13/Grave_1.jpg
Although Wright died in Phoenix, Ariz., he was buried in the small cemetery on the Taliesin grounds, along with other family members. This is his grave.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8ba28d3e-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/1bf58faaf58a308ebd657c7162351fbf/Grave_2.jpg
Rather than a headstone, Wright's grave is marked by a piece of metalwork reminiscent of the geometric design style familiar to those who know his art glass windows.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8ba39bbe-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/a7388bb22a2ae32335d3046509e3692e/Tower.jpg
This is the Romeo  Juliet tower on the Taliesin estate. It was the first structure that Wright ever designed. Built in 1896 for his aunts Jane and Nell Lloyd Jones, it was only supposed to last for 25 years. Instead, it stood until 1990, when it was significantly rebuilt.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8ba4b34c-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/7e9cf4be9d73b3bb0cd90a9902375ae8/Architecture_school_interior.jpg
As at Wright's Arizona home Taliesin West, he maintained an architecture school at Taliesin in Wisconsin. To this day, students study at the school.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8ba5cde8-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/faccc020ba5e22f6195be8f06c6b7f2e/Long_hallway.jpg
As in so many of Wright's buildings, the main house at Taliesin features a long, narrow hallway that opens into a large room at the end.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8ba6ed01-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/d8c59c5e1529a6cfb4e39ce83778fe33/Entry_area.jpg
The entry way into the main house at Taliesin.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8ba7fb5e-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/7768b62d5fe160e6fcd082d6e8a45587/Inside_the_arbor.jpg
A walkway through an arbor near the main house at Taliesin.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8ba909a1-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/d13de8b91c374a2358d1afdd56492960/Steps_up_from_courtyard.jpg
At the edge of the garden outside the main house, a set of steps go up and meet the edge of the rolling hills of the 600-acre Taliesin estate.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2013/08/16/8baa1cff-6788-11e3-846b-14feb5ca9861/thumbnail/770x578/da62d488196b6b6a62b6d67b843fdfd6/Rolling_hills.jpg
A look at the estate's beautiful rolling hills and valley.
 



[Date]2014-06-17
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/63638a3b-5e54-437a-8e67-5df57932cd3d/thumbnail/770x578/2b3cec9b8f6256c113799ced20013d7b/trik-truk.jpg
This is Darryl Starbird's Trik Truk, built on a 1980 Chevrolet van base. Entirely handcrafted in sheetmetal, the van has a mid-engine blown injected 350 Chevy V8. It's painted in "House of Color" Candy Brandywine and Pearl White paint.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/d7ec7b11-7ec6-46fa-b428-03a5bc4f0de9/thumbnail/770x578/50f9584bc5b830b6a6f1e92050618407/spaced-out.jpg
The museum inducted 13 Hall of Fame members when it opened in 1995. Every year, it adds two more members. The museums board issues several other awards and honors, including Builder of the Year, Lifetime Achievement Award, top Auto Journalist, and top Auto Designer.This is "Spaced Out, designed by Darryl Starbird, based on a 1934 Buick 4-door. It's all metal and has handmade bubble top, custom fenders, running boards, hood, nose, rear end, grille, and bumpers. It runs on a 350 Chevrolet Crower Injected, and has a Corvette rear and Jaguar front suspension. It's painted with House of Color Candy Cobolt Blue.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/dfb5c112-fc78-46e6-9e82-046200a84ecd/thumbnail/770x578/2db59181465acb332341a2ca96a70b29/predicta.jpg
Said by the museum to have been voted one of the three most famous custom cars in history, "Predicta" is a 1956 Ford Thunderbird rebuilt and redesigned by Starbird.According to the museum, it's the first-ever bubble top custom car. It has a 1957 Chrysler Hemi engine, and a Chrysler automatic transmission. Its body is made with all sheet metal and has a chrome undercarriage.Kits based on this car sold more than half a million units.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/b0f61ece-0845-4722-987f-60af16017cab/thumbnail/770x578/95f41b6ed33f4c4f4c9993986b20fedd/predicta-interior.jpg
A look at the interior of "Predicta," which features a custom Darryl Starbird design with dual controls, stick steering, and even a TV.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/fa7444fd-aab0-4e87-9fa9-2bc953910563/thumbnail/770x578/865d37df9853d0b33b149e67b6cec6a3/vantasta.jpg
Based on a simple 1972 Ford Van, this is Starbird's "Vantasta." Made from hand-shaped metal, the vehicle was among the very first to feature a futuristic nose. The van has a mid-engine 350 Chevy engine. Inside, there's a stereo, a TV, a bar, a bed, and a two-person cockpit. It's painted in House of Color Pearl and Graphics.Starbird got his start building custom cars in 1954 at the Star Custom Shop. His first project was a 1947 Cadillac. Right off the bat, he got his work in a magazine. His career took off from there.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/66e05fd9-cf5d-4168-a61c-e0839f1c0a33/thumbnail/770x578/eb6e64727be8ab1db47579344c26e0e2/project-6-shooter.jpg
While more than half of the vehicles in the museum were designed by Darryl Starbird, it also showcases the work of many other star hot rod and custom car builders.When the museum opened in 1995, it inaugurated 13 Hall of Fame members and has been inducting two more every year since.This is "Project 6 Shooter" by John Farr. It's based on a 1980 Dodge D-50 pickup.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/0906482f-72af-47b5-8a2a-1506e981c59b/thumbnail/770x578/933477a957a25e62a666ed3fd06a7897/project-6-shooter-cab.jpg
The cab of the "Project 6 Shooter."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/4a581e08-28bf-4022-8c4f-6feefe79b81e/thumbnail/770x578/8d0c1409a8f975578771262b0c0d08fc/project-6-shooter-bike.jpg
A bike on the back completes the look of the "Project 6 Shooter"
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/0aeb24f1-7d04-4ab3-8f71-5e2c44b0560e/thumbnail/770x578/35ef179c0bba42d2e8c0d89b872c6e44/museum-from-drone.jpg
An aerial view of Darryl Starbird's National Hot Rod &amp; Custom Car Hall of Fame Museum, outside Afton, Okla. Shot using a DJI Phantom 2 Vision +.In the beginning, the heart of the hot rod community was on the West Coast. Folks like Batmobile creator were getting Hollywood excited about the culture. But out in Kansas, Starbird was quietly becoming one of the most important voices in the community. In 1959, he produced his first car show. Today, he said hes done 440 around the country, most of which have been in middle America, although he also produces them in places like San Francisco and Oakland, Calif.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/d795bd00-7f5a-46fc-b317-9d7a3ac5f01d/thumbnail/770x578/4a643f44e8dfb30438da64166d59e63c/vibrasonic.jpg
This is Vibrasonic hand-built by Elden Titus.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/144e2a52-c75e-462a-85ea-10aee3265f15/thumbnail/770x578/7d92725b1f3d7365b9befa26d7f5d5ff/vampyre.jpg
This is "Vampyre," built by Elden Titus and Gary Meyers, and owned by Meyers.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/9626b31c-118e-426d-b70f-0112a601f5f9/thumbnail/770x578/a8cc16824e7d59a6bcb6dc3381b2ee20/the-undertaker.jpg
This is the "Undertaker," built by Doug Weigel. It's based on a 1913 Model T C-Cab Truck, but contains no original Ford parts inside its fabricated all-stell body. It features a blown 350 SB engine, and custom intakes by Weigel.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/bbb711f3-e8e4-4fbd-8659-67e00f91b331/thumbnail/770x578/769268047e20e53ad4c71341a801c4ae/phaze-ii.jpg
This is Dave Puhl's "Phaze II, which was built in 1966 and later restored by Darryl Starbird. It features a handmade mid-engine metal body, and was painted with House of Color Candy Gold.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/49140c3b-d2c2-41e4-93d9-107317607b67/thumbnail/770x578/eec7d05294fce3a1c343660bcb7842f4/outlaw.jpg
This is "Outlaw," by Ed Roth. It was eventually "cloned" by Mark Moriarity, according to the museum.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/b931240c-89e8-4afe-add0-3d75d5b12eca/thumbnail/770x578/7eee506bd071b3c23ce55098dce24489/lil-coffin.jpg
This is Monogram's "Lil Coffin," an all-metal 1932 Ford sedan. The custom car was built in 1963 by Dave Stuckey. Starbird later resurrected it. The car has a 1955 De Soto hemi engine.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/91cb6392-f32e-4351-ba71-0a07c3642c57/thumbnail/770x578/5bc38d736ed0881cec3b586d9e79b541/voodoo-spider-front.jpg
This is "Voodoo Spider," handbuilt and designed by Elden Titus.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/a951a808-a904-43c0-bb38-6b06517dffb7/thumbnail/770x578/71e55083397d5561ed1cd5d43018b0cc/voodoo-spider-rear.jpg
A rear view of "Voodoo Spider."
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/942cb667-2f8d-418a-bae2-a247922cb1f7/thumbnail/770x578/a83412bf4d76007682c2cd3ab7fbcf55/the-starbird.jpg
Built by Chuck Mathis as a tribute to Darryl Starbird, this modified 1960 Ford Thunderbird is known as "The Starbird."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/d5cd4cc4-d935-4d85-bdbb-46e8985ba740/thumbnail/770x578/bdb911aa958eb6bdd67093ff1b860047/big-t.jpg
This is Monogram's "Big T," owned by the museum, and built in 1961 by Darryl Starbird. It features a Model T Roadster pickup, and a 283 Chevy engine. Its undercarriage is all chrome, and it has a white naugahyde interior. It was resurrected in 2005 by Predator Performance.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/e38e0433-aada-434e-95af-2f4da92792b1/thumbnail/770x578/e47d4bb766301d0510fb32b756ceacfe/cecil-the-diesel.jpg
Designed and built in 1975 by Starbird, this is "Cecil the Diesel," a "wild cartoonish Model T" body that was entirely hand-crafted in metal. It features a Mercedes 4-cylinder diesel injected engine. It has a chrome-plated frame, and front and rear Jaguar suspension.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/eb504368-fc17-420e-acdf-869b8e84fcec/thumbnail/770x578/e5af43c56929b4d5438cf476778abf75/j-bird.jpg
This is "J Bird," a 1978 Jaguar XJS base that features a V12 overhead cam engine. It has an all-steel body, and handmade front and rear ends and runningboards. It was designed and built by Darryl Starbird.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/7082b44f-4c97-4c76-ba00-c73049a91c3a/thumbnail/770x578/f3c8025d3512ae52585b7bb9ed0d2b0f/deelegance.jpg
Designed and built by Darryl Starbird, this 1979 Buick Riviera was re-built in 1980 and styled to look like a 1930s car. It has a Buick V8 4.0 engine with front-wheel drive.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/07/16/8fb9a735-e068-4919-9ebc-49aeb6ef94c7/thumbnail/770x578/967a30fac2ed620d075b91c586fd353d/frankenstein.jpg
A serious monster truck known as "Frankenstein," outside the museum.
 
 
 
 
 
 
[Date]2014-06-18  Dalas JFK
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/e74b18dd-0a91-4f6c-badd-9dcb9b3f1d9b/thumbnail/770x578/49488732992866df34d8d4bd550da464/fbi-model-wide.png
Among the compelling exhibits is the above FBI-created model of the
president's motorcade moving down Elm Street at the moment Kennedy was shot.
Made to determine whether it was possible JFK had been shot by presumed
assassin Lee Harvey Oswald from the sixth floor of the book depository, the
model establishes that the official explanation is at least plausible.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/b61918ae-5920-48f9-8fae-712800aaef4c/thumbnail/770x578/2bd227e5135866a0c6e4edbddf333229/6th-floor-window-view.jpg
Although the trees along Elm Street are taller than they were in 1963, a look out the sixth floor window of the book depository is a chilling experience. From the window, it is possible to visualize Kennedy's motorcade route, with his limousine first moving slowly down Houston Street (on the left) and then making the sharp turn downhill on Elm, where he was eventually shot.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/ee0c4514-775e-45de-9c33-fff80273596a/thumbnail/770x578/249c4468f7e8257b5d8f124d674492b3/dtxmarksspot.jpg
A look up Elm Street toward what was in 1963 the Texas School Book Depository, and which is now the home of the Sixth Floor Museum (left). The X on the pavement is said to mark the exact spot where Kennedy was fatally wounded.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/04e13d61-1ad3-43d3-bf5d-c0b97a323287/thumbnail/770x578/a4e558628fe24235eca609e45dd283bc/dtzapruder.jpg
Called the most famous home movie in history, the short film shot by Abraham Zapruder on November 22, 1963 was the only known footage to show the assassination. It was used as evidence in many of the investigations surrounding Kennedy's murder.This is the exact view Zapruder had on that fateful day.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/dd8a1a7b-8c89-4a42-b933-5226bdebf244/thumbnail/770x578/07993c9db6dce697409c55a83f0cfe45/exhibitrifle-location.jpg
This is a replica of the 6.5mm Mannlicher-Carcano rifle said to have been used by Lee Harvey Oswald to kill Kennedy. Located in the Sixth Floor Museum next to stacks of replica boxes meant to depict the scene at the spot where Oswald allegedly fired the killing shots, the weapon is one of the most startling artifacts in the museum.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/ce60b332-45c8-4091-9ebd-6447a028fd83/thumbnail/770x578/c7b7bb1a200c5afcdb39c3ede17143d0/dealey-plaza.jpg
A wide-angle photograph of Dealey Plaza taken looking up the hill toward Houston Street. On the left is the former Texas School Book Depository and Elm Street where Kennedy was killed.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/7b79ca0e-c7e4-4a63-9c17-88bef7febc9c/thumbnail/770x578/68cbb94ff4762b7086dff0ec83e959a1/exhibitsnipers-perch-2.jpg
A re-creation of the so-called "sniper's perch" in the northeast corner of the sixth floor of the former Texas School Book Depository, where Oswald was said to have hidden in order to shoot Kennedy.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/ecfee5c3-b80c-474d-9c94-6f8004b19565/thumbnail/770x578/40c2eabcdf0345b20f2ba8ff727eb0f9/dtbookdepository.jpg
A seven-story brick building that would otherwise have no notoriety, the former Texas School Book Depository is now one of the most infamous structures in American history. Today it houses the Sixth Floor Museum at Dealey Plaza, a history of President Kennedy and his assassination.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/f7d7593f-279e-489b-9005-f5c90a1140dd/thumbnail/770x578/3be37ca1388291d4146cc871b03f5ceb/fbi-model-close-up.png
A close-up look at the FBI's model re-creating the supposed wound and kill shots said to have been fired by Lee Harvey Oswald from the sixth floor of what was in 1963 the Texas School Book Depository. The white wires are meant to establish that there was direct line of sight to Kennedy for both shots.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/6761d183-3b25-47eb-a14d-4ac9ff9e586a/thumbnail/770x578/a1b8701191dc463c1f0b6638e806a9de/dtgrassyknoll.jpg
Many people refuse to accept the official theory that Oswald was the lone assassin. A number of witnesses said they heard shots from what is now known as the "Grassy Knoll," this spot alongside Dealey Plaza on the west side of Elm Street.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/316aaff9-38ba-4171-a7e3-f84486873aae/thumbnail/770x578/998cba2a0c8522db8705616328e7c5d0/exhibitbob-jackson-camera.jpg
This is the camera used by the Dallas Times Herald's Bob Jackson to capture the famous photograph of Jack Ruby shooting Lee Harvey Oswald two days after the Kennedy assassination. The picture won the Pulitzer Prize.The camera is on display at the Sixth Floor Museum in Dallas.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/101f01d3-cb12-44d3-8d30-ee4dff18c735/thumbnail/770x578/71cd3ab87271c12512f2ca73685f38f0/exhibitleavelle-suit.jpg
Almost everyone will recognize the photograph of Oswald getting shot, with Dallas Police homicide
detective Jim Leavelle recoiling as Ruby fired the killing bullet.The Sixth Floor Museum has the suit Leavelle was wearing that day on display.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/16/1ff93b0f-c894-4dec-b230-59c012d535d8/thumbnail/770x578/1f8c0589d8075448f52a32e7ab496604/exhibitthe-1960-campaign.jpg
The Sixth Floor Museum attracts thousands of people annually with a wide variety of exhibits about the life and presidency of John F. Kennedy.
 
 
 
[Date]2014-06-19     South West Airline headquater, Dallas
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/dc00db4c-9bab-4fe6-b032-72092e6e32c1/thumbnail/770x578/158454b79228bb533a2d30a8742793de/poolie-with-smoke.jpg
At its Dallas headquarters, Southwest runs this cabin trainer, known as the Poolie, which lets the flight crew practice what to do in a crisis
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/b0a54086-df28-40cd-a9da-7e3154563ce2/thumbnail/770x578/164261ad16c427515e44175773f564b3/poolie-from-side.jpg
Southwest bought the cabin trainer, which mimics part of the interior of a Boeing 737, from Carnival Airlines in 1985. The airline repainted the trainer with its new livery last year.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/71daf5cf-e57f-4b10-a262-127e85e788c1/thumbnail/770x578/4f503a3496c29fa463f4aa43ce1604f6/poolie.jpg
The interior of the Poolie just prior to the cabin filling up with fake smoke. As you can see, it looks very much like an older Southwest 737.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/a5e0058e-4e3e-4c1b-a9aa-57286d2fb3a7/thumbnail/770x578/51ac5fc816a7aff92e16071348655156/poolie-with-slide.jpg
Part of the emergency exercise for those training in the Poolie is to guide "passengers" down the slide that inflates in the case of a crisis. Here, we see the trainer with the slide extended after a demonstration. The exit door had been open during the demo.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/5c1daa9b-059f-4aa3-9e67-c8af57979d41/thumbnail/770x578/4a866258ad1689932346f8d7dbfef9aa/line-of-flight-simulators.jpg
Southwest maintains nine flight simulators at its Dallas headquarters. All first officers and captains must train in them once a year. The giant simulators are meant to pitch and roll just as a plane would, depending on the scenario. The nine simulators are almost always all in use.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/a23afeea-55b6-49ad-a944-ef6421d52692/thumbnail/770x578/39dee4293d44dde3dd15e55f1c281ed6/all-electric-flight-simulators.jpg
While most of the flight simulators use hydraulics to move, the airline's two newest are all-electric. All nine of the trainers simulate Boeing 737s, although two of them (not pictured here) represent the oldest 737s in the airline's fleet.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/64e4f7aa-5dc8-4d84-8645-7460e98d8e5f/thumbnail/770x578/f46c9849aaa44d30b7d813cab37520cf/the-chicken.jpg
Each of the nine simulators has a rubber chicken hanging from it somewhere. Though not an airline industry standard, at Southwest, the practice is thought to bring pilots good luck.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/22598e81-2d8e-4f3e-b967-9d0a26b812d3/thumbnail/770x578/c0652d579d198e7ab5b06706de508569/dt-in-the-flight-simulator.jpg
The author sits in one of the pilot's seats in the flight simulator, ready to take on the virtual skies.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/0b7c4a24-e6ea-41ec-9392-e689c796c51d/thumbnail/770x578/783bf7bb2cc76c2bf21ac8d8fa5139c1/flight-sim-prepping-for-takeoff.jpg
A look out the "window" of the flight simulator cockpit at the virtual runway. The simulator can test any number of scenarios, including taking off with a burning engine, landing in low visibility, approaching big airports, or just about anything else.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/17a2be04-c994-49b9-bd3b-e5b12e516c32/thumbnail/770x578/6fa505a7937e86b8ecfcb465b43ab4f4/flight-sim-banking-over-seattle.jpg
CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman banks to the right over "downtown Seattle" in the Southwest Airlines flight simulator.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/2b9b7962-fc6b-44ac-9d0b-24838687d6e8/thumbnail/770x578/86cf94e7061ac890f12419352c708dd1/models-with-current-livery.jpg
In the lobby of Southwest's main headquarters building, a set of scale-model Boeing 737s hangs from the ceiling, each representing a livery the airline is currently flying.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/a0bf9caf-5a09-4745-94f4-8291776d7384/thumbnail/770x578/18015fc74705d584172eebcb05fb6dae/nose-photo-op.jpg
Scattered around the airline's headquarters are a set of "photo ops," like this one, where employees and visitors are invited to stop and pose for a photograph.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/45a1b20e-6dcf-441e-9d31-4547a0bdf335/thumbnail/770x578/593f38b24c9e3c7d1fa58ca0396c96a0/working-on-the-bridge.jpg
One of Southwest's big new initiatives is this giant center, the NOC, or network operations center. Here, representatives from most of the airline's departments work in small teams to dispatch and monitor each and every one of its daily flights. Here, we see supervisors monitoring flights on the "bridge," the raised area in the middle of the center where senior members of the teams sit and work.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/ea9aa7ad-0edc-4fc4-8387-d5ba08a7fc64/thumbnail/770x578/3da0c0b876f497d354876849c7133610/noc-wide.jpg
A look at the NOC from the rear of the room. Southwest built the center with plenty of room to expand.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/07c100e3-d2e5-4b3e-af8e-bc4d11ff8771/thumbnail/770x578/5e77773e6a8c9e42a06aef93369d5495/trying-to-route-around-weather.jpg
A dispatcher's computer screen shows that the intended route for a Baltimore to Salt Lake City flight would take it through a giant set of thunderstorms. His challenge is to find an efficient route around the 55,000-foot-tall thunderheads.

Southwest recently opened a giant new facility across the street from its main headquarters. Known as TOPS, or Training and Operations, the building is home to the airline's entire training program, which used to be distributed around the country. This large scale-model Boeing 737 hangs in the TOPS lobby.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/3b350930-217c-416b-9372-81c33c5e4b63/thumbnail/770x578/16db8b6f0ea1d30aa46fbef3caa13da9/listening-center-word-cloud.jpg
Recently, Southwest opened what it calls its "Listening Center." Designed to help its communications department monitor social-media channels throughout the day, the idea is that it can react to many types of customer complaints or questions, or to unfolding events much faster than using traditional methods.One way it does so is by looking to see what the most common phrases being used in conjunction with the airline's name are at any time. The result is this word cloud.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/17/f3acbbda-b5d2-4da6-9c50-b556ec7ee261/thumbnail/770x578/fbc7468e4d32dfc22b097a5678cd95a9/listening-center.jpg
A look at the listening center, which has room for several employees, and several different kinds of information being represented on its many screens.

It may look just like an airport's Southwest Airlines check-in counter, but in fact this is a mock-up of one in the airline's training center.

Groups of trainees are taught how to open the plane's doors in an emergency using this mock-up, in one of the training rooms.

[Date]2014-06-21                 Making the world's most efficient locomotive 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/18/28850589-4455-4574-b881-e3466a0780bb/thumbnail/770x578/4f66eaa499fea65254616524631caee9/locomotive-in-the-paint-shop.jpg
Here, a locomotive is inside the paint shop. Currently, all locomotives made
in the Fort Worth factory are being sold to BNSF Railway, so this machine will soon
sport the orange and black livery of BNSF.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/18/0fafdd36-73e8-46e4-a4b8-2fd8ed81a898/thumbnail/770x578/4ff4a570171bb7e200882003e80a7b1d/operator-and-auxiliary-cab-on-platform.jpg
A completed locomotive weighs in at 440,000 pounds, and the platform it's built on alone weighs 120,000 pounds.Here,
GE staffers work to assemble a locomotive. The platform has been
mounted on the trucks (wheels) and they've added the operator cab and
the auxiliary cab -- the section where all the electronics are
installed.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/18/0db0b3f6-1048-44f5-9569-d5f4a31389d0/thumbnail/770x578/e740ebcd205dc51e0c42980693a5ffdf/engines.jpg
Two giant diesel engines sit on the floor in the Fort Worth assembly facility, ready to be being built into locomotives. The engines are built in Grove City, Pa., and weigh 34,000 pounds. A train pulled by a couple of locomotives can haul a ton of freight 500 miles on a single gallon of fuel.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/18/7be0b8e2-4c67-4c32-96d4-31bdd98b2159/thumbnail/770x578/bf5709197fa586f0463fd9c15c5c335f/bnsf-final-assembly.jpg
A finished locomotive sits outside the Fort Worth facility. GE delivers to BNSF twice a week, and the locomotives are put into service immediately.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/18/ee079250-ee86-4e2e-a30e-a8679406733d/thumbnail/770x578/5c2ab3d88fb2abe89274ecf6c9cf5a20/truck-frames.jpg
A line of truck frames sit on the factory floor, awaiting their turn to have wheels and other systems installed.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/18/bb4d0fab-cb22-4221-9a4b-40eb0499cbcd/thumbnail/770x578/b0b1d0b038a3bcd11a24534eed715ed4/trucks-in-progress.jpg
Locomotive trucks sit on the factory floor in various stages of build-out. Some have already had sensors and other systems added, while others haven't yet.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/18/e476003c-a128-4c4b-9327-18a8e4786571/thumbnail/770x578/f58a55c581dd6f1f14788f0603fdd7bd/truck-with-speed-sensor.jpg
A close-up look at a truck, including its speed sensor (the yellow cable).
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/18/d5ee51f1-c6e6-4f03-87aa-7eddd8317f44/thumbnail/770x578/d42e2bebaab64adcec08957dd8c9e644/truck-assembly.jpg
A worker puts together the various elements of a truck assembly.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/18/bd4dadb9-aa6c-4169-9baf-f4582210f23a/thumbnail/770x578/cfcbb6f8dadd2f8432b40fc626bde94f/painted-trucks.jpg
Two finished truck assemblies sit on the floor. They've already been painted and are now waiting to be integrated with a platform.
 
 
 
 
[Date]2014-06-24            How robots can help soldiers haul their heavy gear
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/a1ae3876-9db6-4db3-b782-f45043ef0ed3/thumbnail/770x578/05bf2c05ea1c761dd1e5ddf1bd1965ff/smss-jgh9285.jpg
Robot Carrier
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/d1851e63-13eb-4951-a476-51683167cf8e/thumbnail/770x578/05e3359c1b069108e36b2bde8e28edac/smss-on-dirt.jpg
Lockheed's SMSS was designed to be able to navigate nearly any kind of terrain. One example is dirt. The vehicles can be remote controlled from nearby, can be be programmed to run autonomously, or can be controlled from hundreds of miles away by satellite.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/847839f9-b13d-4fb2-aadd-378abc4ef0ef/thumbnail/770x578/995b81fcebab2ca3b277f0e3e0270654/smss-climbing-rocks.jpg
At Lockheed's robotics facility in Grand Prairie, Tex., near Dallas, the SMSS easily climbs over a pile of rocks, demonstrating its prowess on multiple types of terrain.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/1e54c0b3-f3d2-478f-8db7-ae1116cc0346/thumbnail/770x578/983b1ec154a1cf2f602e2be540dcc290/smss-cied-down-wadi.jpg
Although the military hasn't issued a contract for an autonomous ground vehicle like the SMSS, it's clear that among many things that will be required if, and when, it does, will be the ability for the vehicles to rake the ground for potential land mines or other explosives. Though the machines may be destroyed or damaged by such weapons, no soldiers or Marines would be harmed.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/42a17197-7571-4a77-adae-dcc7d28c310b/thumbnail/770x578/8632c7320bd67db63209254361e66f20/following-while-walking.jpg
Another capability Lockheed has built into the SMSS is for the vehicle to autonomously follow an individual. Once set to do so, it can distinguish between multiple people, and will continue to follow the person it's supposed to. Here, an SMSS drives a short distance behind a Lockheed employee as he walks across a field at the company's robotics facility in Grand Prairie, Tex.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/106d9a90-1d76-4fd6-9203-93723cf67b07/thumbnail/770x578/0d7ffe7280ee82a7286d176645bae9b9/following-while-running.jpg
The SMSS has no problem keeping up with the person it's supposed to be following when he's running.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/682cea3c-3e72-4383-8fe2-fe9b3dd80a1e/thumbnail/770x578/35b3a2377e974b8866b7e0a761078f0d/smss-with-cameras.jpg
This version of the SMSS has been outfitted with a gyro-cam and satellite equipment that allows it be controlled by personnel far away from the field. Lockheed recently conducted a test of this system in Michigan, and plans to do another test at Fort Benning in Georgia in August.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/edb0dee9-24c4-47c0-b86c-e331d9d98706/thumbnail/770x578/76cb476ade33aef05f1654e89d14e073/smss-jgh8369.jpg
The SMSS was designed to fit on a small military aircraft for transportation into the field. Here, an SMSS is loaded onto a military helicopter.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/9151a3b9-5118-4d40-b500-e23fedf7a82d/thumbnail/770x578/77b0eba659453abb8c48dcc0f04a9b49/following-while-going-over-rocks.jpg
The SMSS doesn't care if the person it's following goes over rocks.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/eb26ef6c-672d-4a6a-8335-956d4145b555/thumbnail/770x578/55a8c9de143b26d1b277c281ab9ac11b/driving-smss.jpg
A Lockheed technician drives the SMSS using an off-the-shelf remote control system.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/3ebe09c1-9e8f-4870-9b0a-a07da64f0b66/thumbnail/770x578/54d894c766a222d043d21947f3d99b17/ox-logo.jpg
Because of its ability to haul large amounts of gear, the SMSS is nicknamed the "Ox."
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/23/2007d986-0b55-4bdb-bda8-b0ca7aedf0a1/thumbnail/770x578/51b76bb66aa3c84d3e8ba45785d823f5/smss-camouflage.jpg
At Lockheed's robotics facility, it has three SMSS', including this one, painted in camouflage.
 
 
 
 
[Date]2014-06-26    How the Navy trains its pilots 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/2c7db9d9-cedc-447b-9650-f0944ce0be85/thumbnail/770x578/5e1edfda2a824dbff98d40fc35f7cf68/banking-left.jpg
How the Navy trains its pilots 
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/8d7e52c6-acfe-43d7-bd8f-87cbc664f402/thumbnail/770x578/044189fae6766a8610a8423b2786144f/cockpit.jpg
The "cockpit" of the T-6B simulator is just like its real-world counterpart, so pilots learn how to use all the instruments, switches, and other elements of the plane in the simulator as part of their training on the aircraft.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/4d6ff0f5-41b6-4d04-a3ba-7570e3abe20d/thumbnail/770x578/fd7d36915bb3de26bdb1ed031ce91bde/3-t-6-simulator-pods.jpg
Each of these giant black pods houses a T-6B simulator, and the Naval Air Station Corpus Christi has six of them, all grouped together inside a large training hangar near other T-6B and T-34 simulators.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/8a0bd4fe-a82f-4481-9c0c-02381341d43a/thumbnail/770x578/44524f3157d81aedcc84db2fc330ba7d/daniel-flying.jpg
CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman banks left, "flying" over Corpus Christi in the T-6B simulator.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/f8e38477-7f68-4600-a06b-0806443c50f4/thumbnail/770x578/065d6c29771b6c7ef514125a91e39b33/main-t-6-simulator.jpg
The T-6B simulator, moments before "takeoff."
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/f8d14c95-801d-41ba-8c7a-01dc72e28d00/thumbnail/770x578/5e1a27a609d46fd3cd5eeced49531805/t-6-simulator-weather-control-screen.jpg
Simulator instructors can control many factors about the conditions pilot trainees will be "flying" in, including choosing a wide variety of weather conditions. This is the screen that allows instructors to pick the conditions they want.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/7aba1c85-31cb-470f-9983-2abf6ec9a768/thumbnail/770x578/dd529f09a1ba69bd09919bd1ef575142/simulator-server.jpg
The simulator is run on this server, located alongside it.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/0e742734-a929-4728-a50b-e36d63860a0d/thumbnail/770x578/05fa09531508c4ad30af08280a578c51/dc-10-soars-overhead.jpg
Another element the instructor can throw at a pilot trainee is another airplane flying near its airspace, like this DC-10.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/35b89ac6-96e5-401a-8e9a-b7d2507412a4/thumbnail/770x578/0ad6dfcb74e61cb202718c51215b326e/t-6s.jpg
Parked on the tarmac at the Naval Air Station Corpus Christi are T-6Bs, the Navy's newest basic training airplane. It is replacing the T-34, which the Navy has been using for many years.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/d1f2da11-9654-4039-9d4f-07469d006551/thumbnail/770x578/1525675f5ce504af381eedbe98e21234/t-34.jpg
Although the Navy is still teaching pilots to fly in Corpus Christi using the T-34, the planes are soon going to be completely phased out.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/368ce858-3898-4cee-b648-f047cacc32ff/thumbnail/770x578/620d51658db50db9fd0ccf41d5b23e4b/super-king-air.jpg
Pilots who have graduated from their initial training choose a specialty. Those who choose multiple engine planes can fly the Super King Air (seen here) at Naval Air Station Corpus Christi.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/594c12a8-188d-4788-b7bc-0d348ff126c8/thumbnail/770x578/30c935b1fc1e6b1cd1dd769ba2a3479e/t-44s.jpg
Another advanced aircraft pilots can fly after their initial training is the T-44, seen here.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/e4a52ccd-9598-47bb-b204-1e31106a17da/thumbnail/770x578/f9346baae2c066000a595e2108a800c8/many-t-6s.jpg
There are about 60 T-6Bs being used for training -- not all at once -- at Naval Air Station Corpus Christi.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/88cff74f-11fc-4ba5-8c3f-e7f936887afa/thumbnail/770x578/9bc84b48b16eba21b4ff0eaa8aa06a03/t-34s.jpg
There are still plenty of T-34s being used at Naval Air Station Corpus Christi, although the planes will soon be phased out entirely.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/e71eaecc-f5c8-416f-b1bb-815a278c3537/thumbnail/770x578/a765b6d2e3a9c6a7eb5a3cff457662e5/t-6-simulator-without-screen-graphics.jpg
Before pilots get into the full T-6B simulator, they first "fly" in this, a similar trainer -- except that it doesn't display graphics on the three screens on the dashboard.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/a8e785b8-d647-420d-a7a0-16e682ab1341/thumbnail/770x578/28e504c1d152a1511b6ffd8c8dfe4034/steam-gauges.jpg
Pilots call the old-fashioned dials found on the T-34, and in the T-34 simulator "steam gauges."
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/1f7b5a7a-252f-41bd-9f22-08cc482af686/thumbnail/770x578/0d3b018bac0cec7af7c0e08268afa4ea/t-34-simulator.jpg
A look at a T-34 simulator from the side.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/25/8c581061-81ef-47da-a7b8-9a567601da71/thumbnail/770x578/ecd001541ef59e02128096903007c0dc/t-34-trainer-with-trampoline.jpg
On the T-34, pilots who needed to eject hit an ejection switch and then had to somersault out. In this simulator, a trampoline is set up alongside to allow the pilot to do so.
 
 
 
 
 
 
[Date]2014-06-28    If you're smuggling
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/c45a037b-4cee-4116-86d4-37880e56dedf/thumbnail/770x578/d1e0fce1704d127bd8aa78887c6bee2a/the-dome.jpg
Tracking smuggling
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/77d832aa-94b4-4133-bdfa-99b4f3d3d643/thumbnail/770x578/d4b9b81de046493599d80251f265a577/slick-off-to-the-right.jpg
As part of a training exercise, this P-3 Orion Long Range Tracker (LRT) located and tracked a Cessna that was playing the role of a smuggler's plane flying over the Gulf of Mexico south of Corpus Christi. Then, to demonstrate its ability to sneak up on other planes, the aircraft, known as a "Slick," flew behind the P-3 Orion that CNET reporter Daniel Terdiman was in.At first invisible -- it was below us and just to the left -- it then came up alongside and posed for this photo.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/77164d46-7929-49c2-9a90-70b066dcdc0b/thumbnail/770x578/2d69e0ecedb448e9b16bf3c344a4823d/manning-three-radar-screens.jpg
Three CBP agents, including Senior Detection Enforcement Officer (Airborne) Tom Mason, in the foreground, monitor radar screens aboard the P-3 Orion while in flight over the Gulf of Mexico. The three were overseeing the exercise, watching the other two planes on their radar screens.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/6bb3c3dc-dfed-47da-99e0-1178d72b64ed/thumbnail/770x578/35dda192974896a8a34d26b0e2d58e41/radar-map.jpg
Mason's screen displays what the P-3's APS-145 radar was detecting in and around the Gulf of Mexico. Most of the contacts on the screen are boats of one kind or another.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/31f683ea-b095-4ff1-b774-4363e49c3a56/thumbnail/770x578/044410b09b677e0630a14d57554f44d2/nose-cone-apg-66.jpg
Found inside the nose cone of the P-3 LRT, this APG-66 air-to-air radar allows the Slick to lock on to target planes, letting agents on board the LRT decide whether the target is worth spending time following and investigating.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/9c3b5602-5459-44a8-895e-fbbc8a1dba93/thumbnail/770x578/0ecddd8ea68bb005e1a6aeb71163432f/nose-cone-seavue.jpg
This is a SeaVue surveillance radar system, mounted below the Slick. A surface-search radar system, it can identify a trash can in water from 50 miles away.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/7d77293f-30bb-44fa-b073-5b005ff61453/thumbnail/770x578/dc11568558d49d8a53f41dff5bdd44ca/nose-cone-westcam.jpg
Mounted inside the nose cone of the P-3 LRT is this Wescam MX-20 optical system, which generates imagery that shows up in the signal coming in from the APG-66.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/0a667e30-965d-49e6-a110-cf5b2b4d2fc6/thumbnail/770x578/e3add852033d57cccb1460fb686f0a5e/radar-overlay-on-navigation-map.jpg
CBP agents like Tom Mason have many tools at their disposal as they work to interdict smugglers. One is the ability to overlay the radar information with a navigation map, allowing them to see precisely where a target is.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/2eacb1ed-9d30-4808-b7ce-a233caff03db/thumbnail/770x578/63ffc88ac92bf1e30901a1c403b3309c/mason-manning-the-radar.jpg
Mason monitors his screen to see the location of the P-3 LRT and the Cessna, as the two planes practice playing a cat-and-mouse game over the Gulf of Mexico.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/7b9dfbf7-5bc9-449e-b3c4-09cc9631d240/thumbnail/770x578/2a6d514325632e5225a9dbfb98da6c7c/the-radar-equipment.jpg
This is the machinery that powers the APS-145 radar on board the P-3 Orion. The radar is the same early warning system the US military uses on E2-C Hawkeyes that fly off aircraft carriers.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/aca41fe8-699c-4684-96d9-164853b1da65/thumbnail/770x578/b9f27a6e12dcd626b9e2e3c4f9f0d7d3/slick-banking-hard-right.jpg
After flying alongside the P-3 Orion high over the Gulf of Mexico, the P-3 LRT banks hard right to head back to base.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/808d5c7b-a357-4b34-84a4-24933ec2972f/thumbnail/770x578/0a3ad972f0a0ff2f9674373511924c5d/p-3-crew-flying-the-plane.jpg
The flight crew of the P-3 Orion flies the plane over the Gulf of Mexico.
 
http://cnet1.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/6e3e5b61-4f42-454e-b41b-26b6d551aae9/thumbnail/770x578/92ba744fec7fb5c79d1b8af06877951c/planes.jpg
A Customs and Border Protection P-3 LRT sits on the tarmac in Corpus Christi, Texas, with two P-3 Orions also parked nearby.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/61d0651b-e56a-4674-ae67-02f0e9d58cb0/thumbnail/770x578/4c326e5fe4af28af51fabd27c4e84f5b/guardian.jpg
A Customs and Border Protection MQ-9 Guardian inside the agency's Corpus Christi hangar. The drone can fly up to 30,000 feet for 20 hours and monitor the border and other areas for illicit activity. The Guardian is essentially a Predator that's outfitted with additional radar and optics packages, such as the SeaVue.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/ed3b8522-1bf3-4627-95f0-e32d418b88df/thumbnail/770x578/010c0c0fce6efbc61db1a01031e6bc1d/guardian-camera-mount.jpg
A Wescam MX-20 optics package is mounted underneath the Guardian. The mount includes various cameras and lasers used to identify and watch targets from as high as 30,000 feet.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/ef7c6a95-e301-4c96-9c07-2f9063863208/thumbnail/770x578/706d0365a0d6d4507ff93ccfa852d981/guardian-seavue.jpg
The SeaVue mounted underneath the Guardian allows the CBP to do surface searches from as far as 50 miles away.
 
http://cnet4.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/590e4815-ffe4-4682-b174-4a3d3f1d9f17/thumbnail/770x578/1fde29d98681aebedf283d04a13af1e6/predator.jpg
A CBP Predator drone sits in the agency's Corpus Christi hangar. The Predator has many of the same systems as the Guardian, but not the SeaVue.
 
http://cnet3.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/d16d53c8-e2ab-4770-85d9-ccca4dcbc079/thumbnail/770x578/dbddc40c17b69a5e74057049a0108876/predator-control-room.jpg
CBP can take the Predator on the road, and can run flights from this operations center inside a small trailer that can be moved around on board an airplane.
 
http://cnet2.cbsistatic.com/hub/i/r/2014/06/26/350d5fcd-ad01-41b9-9f1c-5e727c8551dc/thumbnail/770x578/cb04780646e8159710bcf3de7040fc57/cbp-logo-on-side-of-p-3.jpg
The Customs and Border Protection logo on the side of a P-3 LRT.
